Avot DeRabbi Natan
אבות דרבי נתן
The Minor Tractates of the Talmud, trans. A. Cohen, London: Soncino Press, 1965
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH002035163/NLI

Avot DeRabbi Natan



Chapter 1

Moses was sanctified by the cloud and received the Torah at Sinai, as it is stated, <i>And the glory of the Lord abode upon mount Sinai, and the cloud covered him six days</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 24, 16, E.V. <i>covered it</i>.</i> [It covered Moses] in order to cleanse him, and this occurred after [the proclamation of] the Ten Commandments. So expounded R. Jose the Galilean. R. Aḳiba said: The verse <i>and the cloud covered him six days</i> refers to [the mountain and not to] Moses.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is adopted by all Commentators and has MS. support. The sense of the controversy also requires this reading.</i> The continuation of the verse, <i>And the seventh day He called unto Moses out of the midst of the cloud</i>, is to single Moses out for special honour.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The two views represented in this passage originate in the meaning given to the suffix of the verb <i>covered</i> which can signify ‘him’ or ‘it’. R. Jose is of the opinion that immediately after the declaration of the Ten Commandments, which occurred on the sixth day of Sivan, Moses was enveloped by a Divine Cloud and thereby underwent a seven-day period of purification before he could ascend the mount and approach the Divine Presence for his forty days’ stay. A similar purification was prescribed in Temple times for the High Priest before entering upon the ritual of the Day of Atonement. R. ‘Aḳiba, on the other hand, holds that the Cloud covered the mountain and not Moses. Moses was not required to undergo a period of purification, because the call to him to ascend the mount came to him on the seventh day of the month counting from the day on which Israel arrived in the wilderness of Sinai (cf. Ex. 19, 1). The call to Moses from among the assembly of Israel was merely a mark of honour, to show that he stood high above the congregation. Cf. Yoma 4aff (Sonc. ed., pp. 13ff). For the background of this controversy, cf. L. Finkelstein, <i>Mabo le-Massektot Abot ve-Abot d’Rabbi Nathan</i>, English summary, p. XXIX, note 4.</i><br>R. Nathan said: Why was it that Moses remained on the mountain six whole days without the Word<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Dibbur</i>, a Rabbinic term for Divine Revelation.</i> descending upon him? It was that he might be purged of all food and drink which were in his bowels, until such time as he would become holy and be like the ministering angels.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">[For the idea of unevacuated food being a bodily defilement, cf. Semaḥot III, 10 (below, p. 338) and A. Büchler, <i>Studies in Sin and Atonement</i>, pp. 328ff.]</i> R. Mathia b. Ḥeresh said to him, ‘Master, surely the only purpose was to impress him so that he should receive the words of the Torah with awe and fear, with dread and trembling, as it is stated, <i>Serve the Lord with fear and rejoice with trembling</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 2, 11.</i><br>It is related that R. Josiah and R. Mathia b. Ḥeresh happened to be sitting engaged in the study of the Torah, when R. Josiah departed to pursue some secular affairs. R. Mathia b. Ḥeresh said to him, ‘Master, why do you forsake the words of the living God and immerse yourself in secular affairs? And though you are my teacher and I your pupil, [I venture to say that] it is not right to forsake the words of the living God and immerse oneself in secular affairs’. It was said of them that while they were engaged together in the study of the Torah they were like foes one towards the other, but when they departed they were like lifelong friends.
By the hand of Moses was the Torah given at Sinai, as it is stated, <i>And He wrote them upon two tables of stone, and gave them unto me</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 5, 19.</i> and in another passage it is stated, <i>These are the statutes and ordinances and laws, which the Lord made between Him and the children of Israel in mount Sinai by the hand of Moses</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 26, 46.</i> The Torah which the Holy One, blessed be He, gave to Israel was given solely through Moses, as it is stated, <i>Between Him and the children of Israel</i>. Thus Moses was worthy to become the intermediary between Israel and the All-present. Moses offered the ram of consecration<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">By which Aaron and his sons were initiated into the priesthood; cf. Ex. 29.</i> and prepared the anointing oil with which he anointed Aaron and his sons during the seven days of consecration. Of that same oil were anointed all subsequent High Priests and kings.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Hor. 11b (Sonc. ed., pp. 82f).</i> Eleazar burned the heifer of purification<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">More commonly known as the Red Heifer (cf. Num. 19, 3). As Eleazar, Aaron’s son, acted in this matter on the instruction and under the direction of Moses, the whole ritual is referable to Moses, and on that account it was effective for all future generations.</i> from whose ashes all the unclean of later generations were made clean. R. Eliezer said: Great was this act<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘measure’, of anointing oil.</i> because it was effective for all future generations, since Aaron and his sons were consecrated with this anointing oil [and their priesthood continued for many generations], as it is stated, <i>And thou shalt anoint Aaron and his sons, and sanctify them, that they may minister unto Me in the priest’s office</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 30, 30.</i>
Joshua received [the Torah] from Moses, as it is stated, <i>And thou shalt put of thy honour upon him, that all the congregation of the children of Israel may hearken</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 27, 20.</i><br>The elders received it from Joshua, as it is stated, <i>And the people served the Lord all the days of Joshua, and all the days of the elders that outlived Joshua, who had seen all the great work of the Lord, that He had wrought for Israel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Judg. 2, 7.</i><br>The Judges received it from the elders,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">So according to the MSS. and as corrected by GRA. V reads ‘from Joshua’.</i> as it is stated, <i>And it came to pass in the days when the judges judged</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Ruth 1, 1.</i><br>The prophets received it from the Judges, as it is stated, <i>I have sent unto you all My servants the prophets, sending them daily betimes and often</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Jer 7, 25.</i><br>Haggai, Zechariah and Malachi received it from the [earlier] prophets.<br>The men of the Great Assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘Great Synagogue’, a legislative council of one hundred and twenty men traditionally founded by Ezra and his associates. It functioned during the Persian period and later, about 500-300 <small>B.C.E.</small></i> received it from Haggai, Zechariah and Malachi. T<small>HEY SAID THREE THINGS: BE DELIBERATE IN JUDGMENT, RAISE UP MANY DISCIPLES, AND MAKE A FENCE ROUND THE TORAH</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Aboth 1:1. Quotations from Aboth are printed in capitals.</i>
<small>BE DELIBERATE IN JUDGMENT</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should be cautious in judgment, because the more cautious a man is in judgment the more confirmed is he in his judgment, as it is stated, <i>These are the proverbs of Solomon, which the men of Hezekiah king of Judah copied out</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 25, 1.</i>—they did not merely copy them out but deliberated on them.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. for <i>copied out</i> (<i>he ‘etiḳu</i>) may also be derived from a root meaning ‘to grow old, to spend much time’; hence the suggested interpretation ‘pondered over, deliberated’. The proverbs were studied for a long time and their meaning elucidated.</i> Abba Saul said: [The meaning is] not that they deliberated on them but brought them to light.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Giving the Heb. verb <i>he’etiḳu</i> the sense of ‘moving from a place’ as in Gen. 12, 8; hence bringing out the work from its place of concealment to the light of day. The interpretation is also borne by the late Heb. verb <i>perash</i> used by Abba Saul; cf. Reifmann in <i>Keneset Hagedolah</i> I, p. 91. According to Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i>, p. 126, <i>perash</i> here signifies ‘they put aside’ for concealment and burial.</i> At first it was held that the books of Proverbs, Song of Songs and Ecclesiastes were to be stored away<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. not regarded as canonical Scriptures.</i> since they merely contained aphorisms and were not part of the [holy] writings. Thereupon they condemned them to be stored away, [and so they remained] until the men of the Great Assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">All commentators rightly emend to ‘the men of Hezekiah’.</i> came and brought them to light again. For it is stated,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The verses which follow are typical examples of passages from the books mentioned which ostensibly deal with erotic themes and as such are unworthy of a place in the Scriptures.</i> <i>And I beheld among the thoughtless ones, I discerned among the youths, a young man void of understanding…. And, behold, there met him a woman with the attire of a harlot, and wily of heart. She is riotous and rebellious, her feet abide not in her house; now she is in the streets, now in the broad places, and lieth in wait at every corner. So she caught him, and kissed him, and with an impudent face she said to him: Sacrifices of peace-offerings were due from me; this day have I paid my vows. Therefore came I forth to meet thee, to seek thy face, and I have found thee. I have decked my couch with coverlets, with striped cloths of the yarn of Egypt. I have perfumed my bed with myrrh, aloes, and cinnamon. Come, let us take our fill of love until the morning; let us solace ourselves with loves. For my husband is not at home, he is gone a long journey; he hath taken the bag of money with him; he will come home at the full moon</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 7, 7, 10-20.</i> And it is written in the Song of Songs: <i>Come, my beloved, let us go forth into the field;</i> [17<i>a</i>] <i>let us lodge in the villages. Let us get up early to the vineyards; let us see whether the vine hath budded, whether the vine-blossom be opened, and the pomegranates be in flower; there will I give thee my love</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 7, 12f.</i> And it is written in Ecclesiastes: <i>Rejoice, O young man, in thy youth; and let thy heart cheer thee in the days of thy youth, and walk in the ways of thy heart, and in the sight of thine eyes; but know thou, that for all these things God will bring thee into judgment</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 11, 9.</i> And it is further written in the Song of Songs: <i>I am my beloved’s and his desire is toward me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 7, 11.</i> Thus they did not deliberate on them but brought them to light.<br>Another interpretation of <small>BE DELIBERATE IN JUDGMENT</small>: What does it mean? It teaches that a man should speak his words calmly and should not speak in anger; for he who speaks in anger will [in the end] cause his words to be forgotten. We find it so in the case of Moses our master that he forgot his words, as it is stated, <i>And Eleazar the priest said unto the men of war that went to the battle: This is the statute of the law which the Lord hath commanded Moses</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 31, 21.</i> He commanded Moses but not me; He commanded Moses, my father’s brother, but not me.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Although Moses received the law from God, he had forgotten it on account of his anger, and it was left to Eleazar the priest to instruct the warriors.</i> And where do we find that Moses had spoken in anger? It states, <i>And Moses was wrath with the officers of the Lord…. And Moses said unto them: Have ye saved all the women alive?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14f.</i> Why does the verse state <i>all the women?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is deleted by GRA who reads instead: ‘What is meant by <i>through the counsel of Balaam</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 16)?’</i> It is because of the advice which the wicked Balaam counselled against Israel, as it is written, <i>And now, behold, I go unto my people; come, and I will announce to thee what this people shall do to thy people in the end of days</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXIV, 14.</i> He said to Balak, ‘This people whom you hate is hungry for food and athirst for drink, since they have nothing to eat or drink except the manna alone. Go, then, erect booths for them, set out food and drink for them, and place there beautiful girls of royal station, to the end that the people may stray after Baal of Peor, and they will fall by the hand of the All-present’. Forthwith Balak proceeded to carry out all that the wicked Balaam had counselled. See, now, what calamity that wicked Balaam brought upon Israel, since through him there fell twenty and four thousand of them, as it is stated, <i>And these that died by the plague were twenty and four thousand</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXV, 9.</i> Is there not here an argument from the greater to the less? If it happened to Moses, who was the wisest among the wise and the greatest among the great, the father of the prophets, that when he spoke in anger he forgot his words, how much more so [does this apply to us ordinary persons]! It therefore teaches that a man should speak his words calmly and not speak in anger.<br>Ben ‘Azzai said: Beware of idle chatter in your speech.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Do not misuse the gift of speech with empty talk.</i>
<small>AND MAKE A FENCE ROUND THE TORAH</small>. Make a fence to your words as the Holy One, blessed be He, made a fence to His words; and likewise Adam the first man, the Torah, Moses, Job, the Prophets, the Writings,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the Hagiographa, the third division of the Scriptures. The word is inserted here by GRA and is attested by the MSS.</i> and the Sages all made a fence to their words.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The usual meaning given to the maxim ‘make a fence’ is that the Rabbis added restrictions and further severities to the Torah to avoid the infringement of the commandment. The examples cited, however, as illustrations of those who ‘made fences’, differ in character from each other and do not conform to the pattern of the traditional definition. The older and perhaps original meaning of the maxim was an injunction to the Rabbis to explain and present the reasons for their decision, thereby safeguarding it against ridicule and rejection. It is in this sense that God made a fence to His words. As a development of the original meaning of the maxim was the exhortation to the Rabbis to express the power of God in a form intelligible to human beings; this was the ‘fence’ adopted by the Prophets. The other examples of ‘fences’ are additional prohibitions in the usual sense. For a detailed discussion on this subject, cf. the essays of Finkelstein in <i>Journal of Biblical Literature</i>, vol. LVII (1938), pp. 13-50, and vol. LIX (1940), pp. 455-469.</i><br>What fence did the Holy One, blessed be He, make to His words? It is stated, <i>Even all the nations shall say; Wherefore hath the Lord done thus unto this land?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 29, 23.</i> This teaches that it was already revealed to Him Who spoke and the world came into being<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">[For this designation of God, cf. A. Marmorstein, <i>The Old Rabbinic Doctrine of God</i>, I, p. 89.]</i> that future generations will ask: <i>Wherefore hath the Lord done thus unto this land?</i> Consequently the Holy One, blessed be He, said to Moses, ‘Moses, write [down the answer] and place it on record for generations to come: <i>Then men shall say: Because they forsook the covenant of the Lord … and went and served other gods, and worshipped them, gods that they knew not, and that He had not allotted unto them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 24f. In this passage there is a vindication of God’s actions. Lest the nations misjudge and deem them tyrannical or arbitrary, it is stated from the beginning what calamities will befall the people in the event of disobedience. Cf. the parallel passage in <i>’Aboth d’Rabbi Nathan</i>, Recension II, ed. Schechter, p. 3.</i> Hence you learn that the Holy One, blessed be He, made a fence to His words, and recorded in the Torah what people would say in the future; thus bearing witness that the Holy One, blessed be He, pays His creatures their reward in full.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A difficult passage with the text in an uncertain state. The translation follows the text as emended by Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i>, p. 116, reading <i>dikthab battorah</i> for <i>dikthib bathreh</i> and ‘<i>edin</i> (i.e. <i>‘edim</i>) for <i>‘adayin</i>. The Meiri in his commentary on Aboth explains the last sentence to mean that God prescribed in His Torah only such laws as were acceptable to man and within the capacity of human activity, and did not lay upon His creatures impossible demands. In this way mankind was afforded an opportunity of drawing a full reward.</i><br>What fence did Adam, the first man, make to his words? It is stated, <i>And the Lord God commanded the man, saying: Of every tree of the garden thou mayest freely eat; but of the tree of the knowledge of good and evil, thou shalt not eat of it; for in the day that thou eatest thereof thou shalt surely die</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 2, 16f.</i> Adam was unwilling to tell this to Eve exactly as the Holy One, blessed be He, had commanded him; he therefore made a fence to his words, over and above the words which the Holy One, blessed be He, had spoken to him, and this is what he said to her, <i>But of the fruit of the tree which is in the midst of the garden, God hath said: Ye shall not eat of it, neither shall ye touch it, lest ye die</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> III, 3.</i> By so doing he meant to keep both himself and Eve away from the tree, even to the extent of not touching it. At that moment the wicked serpent reasoned to himself saying, ‘Since I cannot bring about the fall of Adam, I will bring about the fall of Eve’. Thereupon he went and seated himself beside Eve and entered into much conversation with her. He said, ‘If you think it is the touching [of the tree] that the Holy One, blessed be He, has forbidden us, behold I will touch it and not die; so you, too, will not die if you touch it’. What did the wicked serpent then do? He arose, touched the tree with his hands and feet, and shook it violently so that its fruit fell to the ground. There are some, however, who say that he did not actually touch it, but that as soon as the tree saw him it cried out, ‘O wicked one, do not touch me’; as it is stated, <i>Let not the foot of pride overtake me, and let not the hand of the wicked shake me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ps. 36, 12. E.V., <i>let not the hand of the wicked drive me away</i>.</i>
Another interpretation of the verse, <i>Let not the foot of pride overtake me</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This was the cry of the altar when Titus entered the Temple.</i> refers it to Titus, may his bones rot! For he raised his hand in contempt and struck the altar exclaiming, ‘Lukos! Lukos!<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">A Greek word meaning ‘wolf’, here applied opprobiously to the altar. Cf. Suk. 56b (Sonc. ed., p. 276).</i> You are a king just as I am a king, come and engage in battle with me. How many oxen have been slain upon you! How many birds have been killed upon you! How much wine has been poured out upon you! How much incense has been burned upon you! It is you who are destroying the whole world’, as it is stated, <i>Ah, Ariel, Ariel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A poetical name for the altar.</i> <i>the city where David was encamped! Add ye year to year, let the feasts come round!</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 29, 1. The reference is to the countless sacrifices offered on the altars at every season because of the many sins of the people.</i><br>Furthermore the serpent said to her, ‘If you think it is the eating [of the fruit of the tree] that the Holy One, blessed be He, has forbidden us, behold I will eat of it and will not die; so you, too, will not die if you eat of it’. Eve then began to think to herself, ‘All that my master has commanded me from the beginning is false’—for Eve used to address Adam as ‘master from the beginning’. She immediately took of the fruit and ate it, and also gave some to Adam, which he ate, as it is stated, <i>And when the woman saw that the tree was good for food, and that it was a delight to the eyes</i>, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 3, 6.</i>
With ten curses was Eve cursed at that time, as it is stated, <i>Unto the woman He said: I will multiply and multiply thy pain and thy travail; in pain thou shalt bring forth children; and thy desire shall be to thy husband, and he shall rule over thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 16, so lit. E.V., <i>I will greatly multiply</i>.</i> <i>I will multiply and multiply</i>—these are the two discharges of blood [which cause pain to women], the menstrual and [at the termination of] virginity. [<i>Thy pain</i>—this is the anxiety connected with the rearing of children.]<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA in accordance with ‘Erub. 100b (Sonc. ed., p. 697).</i> <i>And thy travail</i>—this is the pain associated with conception. <i>In pain thou shalt bring forth children</i>—this is to be understood in its literal meaning. <i>And thy desire shall be to thy husband</i>—this teaches that a woman particularly yearns for her husband when he is about to set out on a journey. <i>And he shall rule over thee</i>—for the man solicits by word of mouth whereas the woman solicits in her heart. [Furthermore, during her periods] she is wrapped up like a mourner, shut up as in a prison and banished from the company of all men. Now what brought about the act of touching? The answer is the fence which Adam had made to his words.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Adam’s fault was that he had attached as great importance to the ‘fence’ as to the original command.</i> Hence [the Sages] said: When a man makes a fence to his words, he is not always able to stand by his words. They further said: Hence let not a man add to the words which he has heard. R. Jose said: A firm [wall] of ten hand-breadths is better than a tottering [wall] of a hundred cubits.<br>What was in the mind of the wicked serpent at that time? ‘I will go,’ he thought, ‘slay Adam and marry his wife; then I will be king over the whole world. I shall also walk erect and eat all the dainties of the world.’ Whereupon the Holy One, blessed be He, said to him, ‘You reckoned [17<i>b</i>] to slay Adam and marry Eve; therefore <i>I will put enmity between thee and the woman</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 15.</i> You reckoned to be king over the whole world; therefore <i>Cursed art thou from among all cattle</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14.</i> You reckoned to walk erect; therefore <i>Upon thy belly shalt thou go</i>. You reckoned to eat all the dainties of the world; therefore <i>Dust shalt thou eat all the days of thy life</i>.<br>R. Simeon b. Menasia said: Alas for the most useful servant that was lost to the world! For had not the serpent been cursed, every Israelite would have been provided with two serpents for his personal use at home; one he would send to the west and the other to the east, to bring him thence gems, precious stones, pearls and every desirable thing in the world, and no creature could ever injure them.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Or, according to another reading: ‘could ever hold them back’.</i> Moreover, they could have been employed in the place of camels, asses and mules to provide manure for gardens and orchards.
R. Judah b. Bathyra said: Adam, the first man, was reclining in the Garden of Eden with ministering angels at his service, roasting meat and cooling wine for him. Then the serpent came and, seeing him in all his glory, at once grew envious of him.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The two paragraphs are quoted from Sanh. 59b (Sonc. ed., p. 405), but the second is there given in the name of R. Judah b. Tema.</i><br>How was Adam, the first man, created? In the first hour his dust was gathered together, in the second his outline was shaped, in the third he was made into solid substance, in the fourth his limbs were articulated, in the fifth his orifices were opened, in the sixth a soul was infused into him, in the seventh he stood on his feet, in the eighth Eve became his mate, in the ninth he was brought into the Garden of Eden, in the tenth he received his command [concerning the tree], in the eleventh he sinned, and in the twelfth he was driven out, thus fulfilling what is stated, <i>Man tarrieth not overnight in his glory</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 49, 13, indicating that Adam forfeited his glory within a day before the oncoming of night. E.V., <i>man abideth not in honour</i>.</i><br>Which Psalm did Adam recite on the first day?<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. R.H. 31a (Sonc. ed., pp. 145f) where the same Psalms are selected for recital by the Levites in the Temple on the successive days of the week.</i> <i>The earth is the Lord’s and the fulness thereof; the world, and they that dwell therein;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 24, 1.</i> because He created the world, assigned it to mankind, and is the sole Judge of the world. Which did he recite on the second day? <i>Great is the Lord, and highly to be praised, in the city of our God, His holy mountain;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XLVIII, 1.</i> because He divided the work of creation and became Sovereign in His universe. Which did he recite on the third day? <i>God standeth in the congregation of God; in the midst of the judges He judgeth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> LXXXII, 1.</i> because He then created the sea and the dry land, the earth was rolled<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘was folded’.</i> into position, and a place was set apart for His people. Which did he recite on the fourth day? <i>O Lord, Thou God to whom vengeance belongeth, Thou God to whom vengeance belongeth, shine forth;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XCIV, 1.</i> because He then created the sun, the moon, the stars and the planets, which give light on earth, and He will in the future punish those who worship them. Which did he recite on the fifth day? <i>Sing aloud unto God our strength; shout unto the God of Jacob;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> LXXXI, 2.</i> because He then created the birds, the fishes and the sea-monsters, which sing aloud His praises in the world. Which did he recite on the sixth day? <i>The Lord reigneth; He is clothed in majesty; the Lord is clothed, He hath girded Himself with strength; yea the world is established, that it cannot be moved;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XCIII, 1.</i> because He then completed all His works, was exalted and sat enthroned in the heights of the universe. Which did he recite on the seventh day? <i>A Psalm, a Song. For the Sabbath day;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XCII, 1.</i> because of the day which will be all Sabbath, when there will be neither eating nor drinking nor worldly affairs, but the righteous will sit with crowns on their heads enjoying<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Or, according to another reading: ‘feasting on’.</i> the brilliance of the Divine Presence, as it is stated, <i>And they beheld God, and did eat and drink</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 24, 11. The food and drink on that occasion were not of the material kind.</i> like the ministering angels. And why was this so?<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. why was Adam created last of all, on the sixth day just before the commencement of the Sabbath?</i> In order that [Adam] might immediately partake of the Sabbath meal.<br>R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: I will expound this to you with a parable. To what might Adam be compared? To a man who married a proselyte and used to give her instruction, saying, ‘My dear, do not eat bread when your hands are defiled, do not eat untithed produce, do not profane the Sabbath, do not make vows rashly, and do not associate with another man. If you break one of these rules, you will die’. Now what did a certain man do?<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">So according to GRA, the reference being to a stranger who came to tempt her. V reads ‘what did that man do’, apparently referring to the husband.</i> He arose and ate in her presence bread although his hands were defiled, he ate before her untithed produce—he profaned the Sabbath and made vows rashly—and also offered her some [of the food].<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Adopting the reading of MS. E, lit. ‘he handed her with his hands’. V ‘and brought out to her’.</i> What could that proselyte have thought to herself? All that my husband forbade me in the beginning was without foundation. She thereupon went and transgressed them all.<br>R. Simeon b. Yoḥai said: I, too, will explain this to you by a parable. To what might Adam be compared? To a man who had his wife at home with him. Now what did that man do? He went and brought a cask into which he put an exact number of figs and nuts. He also caught a scorpion, placed it in the mouth of the cask, covered the cask with a tight-fitting lid and put it away in a corner. He then said to his wife, ‘My dear, all that I possess in this house is at your disposal except this cask which you may not touch under any circumstances’. What did this woman do? No sooner had her husband gone off to market than she arose, opened the cask, put her hand in it and was bitten by the scorpion. Immediately she went and flung herself on her bed. When her husband returned from market he asked her what had happened, and she replied, ‘I put my hand in the cask and was bitten by a scorpion, and now I am dying’. He said to her, ‘Did I not tell you in the beginning that everything I had in the house was at your disposal except that cask which you were not to touch under any circumstances?’ He at once became furious with her and drove her out. So it was with Adam, the first man, when the Holy One, blessed be He, charged him, <i>Of every tree of the garden thou mayest freely eat; but of the tree of the knowledge of good and evil, thou shalt not eat of it; for in the day thou eatest thereof thou shalt surely die</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 2, 16f.</i> No sooner had Adam eaten of it than he was driven out [of Eden], to fulfil that which is stated, <i>Man tarrieth not overnight in his glory; he is like the beasts that perish</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 49, 13. See above, p. 10, n. 59</i><br>On the same one day he was formed, created, his outline was shaped, he became solid substance, his limbs were articulated, his orifices opened, a soul was infused into him, he stood on his feet, Eve became his mate, he named [all creatures], was brought into the Garden of Eden, given a command, sinned and was driven out, to fulfil that which is stated, <i>Man tarrieth not overnight in his glory</i>. On that same day two went upon the bed and four came down.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The four are: Adam, Eve, Cain and Abel.</i> R. Judah b. Bathyra said: On that day two went upon the bed and six came down.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The six are: Adam, Eve, Cain, Abel, each of the last two with a twin sister. According to GRA and MS. E seven came down, since two twin sisters were born with Abel; cf. Midrash Rabbah, Genesis, XXII, §2 (Sonc. ed., p. 180).</i><br>On that day three decrees were pronounced upon Adam, as it is stated, <i>And unto Adam He said: Because thou hast hearkened unto the voice of thy wife … cursed is the ground for thy sake; in toil shalt thou eat of it … thorns and thistles shall it bring forth to thee; and thou shalt eat the herb of the field</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 3, 17f.</i> When Adam heard that the Holy One, blessed be He, had decreed against him, <i>Thou shalt eat the grass of the field</i>, his limbs forthwith began to tremble and he exclaimed, ‘Lord of the universe! Am I and my beast to eat from one manger?’ Whereupon the Holy One, blessed be He, said to him, ‘Since your limbs trembled [at the prospect]’<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This was considered by God as an act of penitence.</i> <i>In the sweat of thy face shalt thou eat bread</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 19.</i><br>Just as three decrees were pronounced upon Adam so were three decrees pronounced upon Eve, as it is stated, <i>I will greatly multiply thy pain and thy travail; in pain thou shalt bring forth children</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 16.</i> [<i>I will greatly multiply</i>]—for when a woman begins to experience her monthly flow she suffers pain; <i>I will greatly multiply</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. is lit. ‘multiply I will multiply’ indicative of two pains; cf. above, p. 8, n. 53</i> [<i>thy pain</i>]—for when a woman experiences intercourse for the first time it is painful to her; <i>thy pain</i> [<i>and thy travail</i>]—for during the first three months of pregnancy a woman’s features become coarsened and pallid.<br>As [his first] evening set in and Adam saw the world darkening in the west, he exclaimed, ‘Woe is me! Because I have sinned, the Holy One, blessed be He, is darkening the world upon me!’ He was unaware that such was the course of nature; but in the morning when he saw the world becoming light in the east he was exceedingly happy. He arose and built an altar, he took a bullock whose horns came into being before its hoofs<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the creation the first bullock came up from the ground fully grown and in erect posture (cf. Ḥul. 60a, Sonc. ed., pp. 329f), its horns necessarily appeared first and its hoofs last.</i> and offered it as a burnt-offering, as it is stated, <i>And it shall please the Lord better than a bullock that hath horns and hoofs</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 69, 32.</i><br>The bullock which Adam offered, the bull which Noah offered,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Gen. 8, 20. This verse, however, does not mention that Noah offered a bull. Since the word quoted from Ps. 69, <i>bullock</i> is lit. ‘ram bullock’, it has been suggested that one was Adam’s and the other that of Noah.</i> and the ram which our father Abraham offered on the altar instead of his son, were all beasts whose horns came into being before their hoofs, as it is stated, [18<i>a</i>] <i>And Abraham lifted up his eyes, and looked, and behold behind him a ram caught</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 22, 13. Tradition tells that Abraham’s ram was created on the eve of the first Sabbath at twilight (Aboth 5:6, Sonc. ed., p. 64), and as all the animals of the creation were brought forth in their full-grown stature, their horns must have appeared before their hoofs.</i><br>Then and there<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Immediately Adam had brought his offering.</i> three bands of ministering angels came down with harps and lyres and other musical instruments in their hands and joined with him in a song of praise, as it is stated, <i>A Psalm, a Song. For the Sabbath day. It is a good thing to give thanks unto the Lord…. To declare Thy lovingkindness in the morning, and Thy faithfulness in the night seasons</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 92, 1-3.</i> <i>To declare Thy lovingkindness in the morning</i>—this refers to the world to come which is likened to the brightness of the morning, as it is stated, <i>They are new every morning; great is Thy faithfulness</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Lam. 3, 23. By reason of God’s faithfulness the righteous will be renewed in the morning, i.e. they will be revived in the world to come.</i> <i>And Thy faithfulness in the night seasons</i>—this refers to this world which is likened to [the darkness of] night, as it is stated, <i>The burden of Dumah. One calleth unto me out of Seir: Watchman, what of the night? Watchman, what of the night?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 21, 11. The faithful who watch and wait for God’s deliverance cry out: How long will the sorrows and sufferings of this world continue?</i><br>At that time the Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘If I do not inflict punishment upon the serpent I will be destroying the whole world’. He also said, ‘As for man whom I made lord and king over the world, how he has become entangled [in the wiles of the serpent] and eaten of the [forbidden] tree!’ Thereupon He turned His attention to him and cursed him, as it is stated, <i>And the Lord God said unto the serpent</i>, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 3, 14.</i> R. Jose said: Had the curse of the serpent been deferred to later,<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘had the curse of the serpent been written in the end’.</i> He would in the meantime have destroyed the whole world.<br>When the Holy One, blessed be He, created Adam, the first man, He formed him behind and before,<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. with two faces, in front and behind, from one of which Eve was formed. Cf. Ber. 61a (Sonc. ed., p. 381).</i> as it is stated, <i>Thou hast formed me behind and before, and laid Thy hand upon me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 139, 5. E.V., <i>Thou hast hemmed me in</i>.</i> Ministering angels descended to adore him,<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Or, to worship him. According to MS. E: ‘to destroy him’, because they were jealous of him; this is also the view of GRA.</i> whereupon the Holy One, blessed be He, took him up and placed him beneath His wings, as it is stated, <i>And laid Thy hand upon me</i>.<br>Another interpretation of <i>And laid Thy hand upon me:</i> when Adam sinned, the Holy One, blessed be He, removed one [of His hands] from him. From this it may be inferred that when Adam and the Temple were created, they were each created by His two hands. Whence do we know that man was created by His two hands? For it is stated, <i>Thy hands have made me and fashioned me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXIX, 73. The noun is plural.</i> And whence do we know that the Temple was created by His two hands? For it is stated, <i>The sanctuary, O Lord, which Thy hands have established</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 15, 17.</i> And it is also said, <i>And He brought them to His holy border, to the mountain which His right hand had gotten;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 78, 54.</i> and further it is written, <i>The Lord shall reign for ever and ever</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 15, 18. The last two verses which are quoted have no connection with the theme of the passage and are deleted by GRA. They are found in the original context at the conclusion of tractate Aboth.</i>

Chapter 2

What fence did the Torah make to her words? It is stated, <i>And thou shalt not approach unto a woman … as long as she is impure by her uncleanness</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 18, 19.</i> It might be supposed that he may embrace or kiss her, or engage with her in frivolous talk; the text therefore declares, <i>Thou shalt not approach</i>. It might be further supposed that he may sleep with her if she is clothed in the one bed; the text therefore declares, <i>Thou shalt not approach</i>. Again it might be supposed that she may wash her face and paint her eyes; the text therefore declares, <i>And of her that is sick with her impurity</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XV, 33.</i>—all the days of her impurity she shall be in banishment [from society].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. <span dir="rtl">נדה</span>, ‘a woman in her impurity’, is here connected with its original meaning of ‘cast out, banished from society’.</i> Hence [the Sages] said: Every woman who makes herself unattractive during the period of her impurity wins the approbation of the Rabbis, and she who adorns herself during the period of her impurity incurs their censure.
The story is told<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted from Shab. 13a, b (Sonc. ed., p. 53) with some variants.</i> of a certain man who had read much Scripture, studied much Mishnah, and had attended on many scholars, yet had died in the midst of his days. His wife used to take his <i>tefillin</i>, carry them round the Synagogues and Houses of Study, and weepingly complain, ‘Masters, is it not written in your Torah, <i>That is thy life, and the length of thy days?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 30, 20.</i> Why, then, did my husband die in the midst of his days, he who read much Scripture, studied much Mishnah, and attended on many scholars?’ There was no one who could answer her. One day, Elijah, of blessed memory, met her and said to her, ‘My daughter, why do you weep and complain?’ She replied, ‘My husband had read much Scripture, studied much Mishnah and attended on many scholars; yet he died in the midst of his days’. He asked her, ‘During the first three days of your impurity what was his relationship towards you?’<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The number ‘three’ found in V is difficult to explain, as the woman’s impurity continues, according to Biblical law, for a minimum period of seven days. Consequently there is no ground for drawing a distinction between the first three and the subsequent days. Most scholars, following GRA, substitute ‘seven’ for ‘three’, and the reference is to the monthly period of uncleanness. The ‘latter days of impurity’ would be, what is known in Talmudic phraseology, ‘the days of white garments’, i.e. the seven days of observation after the. cessation of the discharge. During this period a woman is forbidden by Rabbinic law all intimacy with her husband, including acts of endearment.</i> She said to him ‘Master, God forbid! He did not touch me even with his little finger, but used to say to me, “Touch no vessel lest you bring me into a state of doubt” ’. ‘And what was his relationship towards you during the latter days of your impurity?’<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In Shab. 13a the text reads: ‘during the days of your white garments’. Cf. the preceding note.</i> She replied, ‘Master, I ate and drank with him, slept with him<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">V adds ‘in my clothes’ which is omitted in the Talmudic version and appears incompatible with the following words, ‘with our bodies touching’. Some authorities retain the former phrase and alter the latter to ‘without our bodies touching’.</i> in the one bed with our bodies touching, but he had no other intention’. Whereupon Elijah said to her, ‘Blessed be the All-present for slaying him, since it is written in the Torah, <i>And thou shalt not approach unto a woman … as long as she is impure by her uncleanness</i>.’<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 18, 19.</i><br>It is stated, <i>None of you shall approach to any that is near of kin to him;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 6.</i> hence [the Sages] said: Let no man remain alone with a woman at an inn, even though she be his sister or daughter, on account of public opinion. Let no man converse with a woman in the market-place, not even with his wife and needless to say with another woman, on account of public gossip. Let no man walk behind a woman in the market-place, not even behind his wife and needless to say behind another woman, on account of public gossip.<br>It is stated here,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the Biblical passage concerning unlawful unions.</i> <i>None of you shall approach</i>, and it is stated there, <i>Thou shalt not approach;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 19. In the passage dealing with menstrual impurity.</i> do not therefore approach to any conduct which may lead to transgression.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">By the analogy: just as any manner of approach to a woman in her impurity is forbidden, so any approach whatsoever to a woman prohibited as being near of kin is likewise forbidden.</i> Keep aloof from anything hideous or from whatever seems hideous.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A Rabbinic rule of conduct; cf. Ḥul. 44b (Sonc. ed., p. 239).</i> Accordingly the Sages said: Keep aloof from a trivial sin lest it lead you on to a grave sin. Run to perform a minor precept for it will lead you on to an important precept.<br>It is stated, <i>Thy belly is like a heap of wheat set about with lilies</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 7, 3.</i> <i>Thy belly is like a heap of wheat</i>—this describes the congregation of Israel;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. 37a (Sonc. ed., pp. 231f). As all derive sustenance from a heap of wheat, so the whole world enjoys favour because of Israel.</i> <i>set about with lilies</i>—these are the seventy elders.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Who constitute the Great Sanhedrin and whose duty it is to make ‘fences’ to safeguard the Torah.</i><br>Another interpretation of <i>Thy belly is like a heap of wheat</i> [<i>set about with lilies</i>]—these are those light precepts<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the precautionary restrictions, light in themselves, but safeguarding against violation of the laws. Cf. Sanh. <i>loc. cit.</i></i> which are as tender as a hedge of lilies and through their observance by Israel lead them to life in the world to come. How is this so? A man’s wife is at home with him during the period of her impurity. He could have intimacy with her or not at his will, since no man sees him and no man knows of it to rebuke him. Hence he fears only Him Who ordained the law of [the uncleanness of women. Similarly if a man suffers a seminal emission, he may immerse himself or not as he will, since no man sees him and no man knows of it to rebuke him. Hence He fears only Him Who ordained the law of] immersion.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The lines enclosed within brackets are missing in V, but have been inserted here from MS. E. The origin of the omission is due to a scribe who jumped from the first ‘who ordained the law of’ to the second.</i> You may reason similarly with the law of the dough-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Num. 15, 20f.</i> and the law of the first of the fleece.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Deut. 18, 4. These are further examples of precepts observed within the privacy of the home over which no control can be exercised by other persons.</i> Such are the light precepts which are as tender as lilies and through their observance by Israel lead them to life in the world to come.
What fence did Moses make to his words? It is stated, <i>And the Lord said unto Moses: Go unto the people, and sanctify them to-day and to-morrow</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 19, 10.</i> Now the righteous Moses was not willing to tell it to Israel in the manner in which the Holy One, blessed be He, spoke to him, but said to them, <i>Be ready against the third day; come not near a woman</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 15.</i> Thus Moses added a third day for them of his own accord, because Moses reasoned as follows, ‘If a man will cohabit with his wife and on the third day the seed will issue from her they will become unclean,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In accordance with the law that if a woman discharged semen at any time within three days of coitus she is unclean, since the semen is still potent; after three days she is clean. Cf. Shab. 86a (Sonc. ed., p. 408) where this rule is derived from the last quoted verse.</i> and in consequence Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">GRA reads ‘the women of Israel’, which is preferable.</i> would receive the Torah from Mount Sinai in a state of defilement. I will therefore add a third day for them, so that no man will cohabit with his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Within these three days.</i> and there will be no fear of the seed issuing from her. All will be clean, and in consequence [the women of] Israel will receive the Torah from Mount Sinai in a state of cleanness’.<br>This<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The addition by Moses of an extra day to the Divine command.</i> is one of the things which Moses did of his own accord, reasoning by <i>a fortiori</i> argument, and his decision conformed to the will of the All-present.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shab. 87a (Sonc. ed., p. 411), Yeb. 62a (Sonc. ed., p. 412).</i> He broke the tablets, and this act of his accorded with the will of the All-present; he kept away from the Tent of Meeting, and this act of his accorded with the will of the All-present; he separated from his wife, and this act of his accorded with the will of the All-present.<br>[‘He separated from his wife, and this act of his accorded with the will of the All-present.’] How was this? He reasoned as follows: If with regard to the Israelites, who were sanctified for a short period only and summoned for the sole purpose of receiving the Ten Commandments at Mount Sinai, the Holy One, blessed be He, said to me, <i>Go unto the people, and sanctify them to-day and to-morrow;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 19, 10.</i> I, who am in readiness for the purpose [of receiving] the word of God daily and hourly, since I know not when He will speak with me, whether by day or by night, must all the more separate myself from a woman! And his decision accorded with the will of the All-present.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Proved from the fact that when the people were permitted to resume marital relations, God said to Moses, <i>But as for thee, stand thou here by Me</i> (Deut. 5, 28).</i> R. Judah b. Bathyra said: Moses did not separate from his wife until he was ordered to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>With him do I speak mouth to mouth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 12, 8.</i>—by a <i>mouth-to-mouth</i> order I commanded him to separate from his wife, and he did so. Others interpret in this way: Moses did not separate from his wife until he was ordered to do so by the [18<i>b</i>] Almighty, as it is stated, <i>Go say to them: Return ye to your tents;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 5, 27. This was a permission to resume marital relations.</i> and after that it is written, <i>But as for thee, stand thou here by Me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 28.</i> Moses then turned about<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">A strange expression in this context. The meaning seems to be that Moses extended the temporary restriction to all the days of his life, and this met with God’s approval. Cf. the second recension on this passage in Schechter’s ed., p. 10.</i> and separated from his wife, and in so doing he was in accord with the will of the All-present.<br>‘He kept away from the Tent of Meeting.’ How was this? He reasoned as follows: ‘If in connection with my brother Aaron, who has been anointed with the anointing oil, clothed in the additional vestments of the High-priesthood and ministers therein in holiness, the Holy One, blessed be He, admonished, <i>Speak unto Aaron thy brother, that he come not at all times into the holy place;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 16, 2.</i> I, who have not been ordained for the purpose, must all the more keep away from the Tent of Meeting’. He kept away from the Tent of Meeting and in so doing he was in accord with the will of the All-present.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">No verse or exposition is cited to prove this. In the Talmudic passages (cf. above, p. 19, n. 28) the instance of keeping away from the Tent of Meeting is not included.</i><br>‘He broke the tablets.’ How was this? It is related that when Moses ascended on high to receive the tablets which had been inscribed and were lying in readiness [to be revealed] since the six days of creation,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Aboth 5:9 (Sonc. ed., V, 6, p. 63).</i> as it is stated, <i>And the tables were the work of God, and the writing was the writing of God, graven</i> [ḥaruth] <i>upon the tables</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 32, 16. As <i>the work of God</i> refers primarily to the six days of creation, it follows that the tablets were then prepared.</i>—read not <i>ḥaruth</i> [graven] but <i>ḥeruth</i> [freedom], for only he is truly free who occupies himself with the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This incidental word-play interrupts the sequence of the passage, and is borrowed from Aboth 6:2 (Sonc. ed., p. 80).</i>—at that time the ministering angels arraigned Moses, saying, ‘Lord of the universe! <i>What is man, that Thou art mindful of him? And the son of man, that Thou thinkest of him? Yet Thou hast made him but little lower than the angels, and hast crowned him with glory and honour. Thou hast made him to have dominion over the works of Thy hands; Thou hast put all things under his feet: sheep and oxen, all of them, yea, and the beasts of the field; the fowl of the air, and the fish of the sea’</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 8, 5-9.</i> They were referring disparagingly to Moses, saying, ‘What virtue is there in man born of woman that he has ascended on high, as it is stated, <i>Thou art ascended on high, thou hast led captivity captive, thou hast received gifts?</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> LXVIII, 19. E.V. refers to God.</i> Moses took the tablets and descended with them, rejoicing exceedingly. But as soon as he saw the depravity with which they had depraved themselves in the episode of the calf, he said to himself, ‘How can I give them the tablets, thereby binding them to the performance of weighty commandments, and in consequence condemning them [if they disobeyed] to death before Heaven, for it is written therein, <i>Thou shalt have no other gods before Me?</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 20, 3.</i> He turned back, but when the seventy elders saw this they hurried after him. He seized one end of the tablets while they seized the other end, but the strength of Moses prevailed over theirs, as it is stated, <i>And in all the mighty hand, and in all the great terror, which Moses wrought in the sight of all Israel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 34, 12.</i> He glanced at the tablets and saw that the writing had flown from them; so he exclaimed, ‘How can I give Israel these worthless tablets? I will grasp hold of them and break them, as it is stated, <i>And I took hold of the two tables, and cast them out of my two hands, and broke them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IX, 17.</i><br>R. Jose the Galilean said: I will illustrate this to you by a parable. To what can the matter be compared? To a human king who ordered his agent, ‘Go and betroth for me a beautiful, graceful and well-behaved maiden’. The agent went and performed the betrothal. After the betrothal he discovered that she was unfaithful with another. Thereupon he reasoned to himself, ‘If I give her the contract<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Presumably the betrothal document. By destroying this document the verbal betrothal could be disclaimed, and the maiden’s misbehaviour would be treated as that of an unbetrothed girl which does not involve the extreme penalty. Cf. Midrash Rabbah, Exodus, XLVI,§1 (Sonc. ed., pp. 526f).</i> now, I will be condemning her to death<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Deut. 22, 23f.</i> and depriving my master of her for all time; [I will rather tear up the contract]’. In the same way did the righteous Moses reason to himself, ‘How can I now give Israel the tablets, binding them thereby to the performance of weighty commandments, and in consequence condemning them [if they disobeyed] to death, for it is written therein, <i>He that sacrificeth unto the gods, save unto the Lord only, shall be utterly destroyed?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 22, 19.</i> I will rather seize the tablets and break them, and lead Israel back to the right way. Then Israel might say,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In their defence when charged with idolatry.</i> “Where are the first tablets which [you say] you brought down? There were no such things at all!” ’<br>R. Judah b. Bathyra said: Moses only broke the tablets because he was commanded to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>With him do I speak mouth to mouth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 12, 8.</i>—by a <i>mouth-to-mouth</i> order I commanded him to break the tablets. Some explain it in this way: Moses only broke the tablets because he was commanded to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>And I looked, and, behold, ye had sinned against the Lord your God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 9, 16.</i>—the expression <i>I looked</i> means that he saw that the writing had flown from off the tablets.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The tablets thereby became worthless, and Moses understood it as an order to break them.</i> Others explain: Moses only broke the tablets because he was commanded to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>And there they are, as the Lord commanded me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> X, 5.</i>—the words <i>commanded me</i> can only mean that he was so commanded and broke them.<br>R. Eleazar b. Azariah said: Moses only broke the tablets because he was commanded to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>Which Moses wrought in the sight of all Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXIV, 12. Cf. Shab. 87a (Sonc. ed., p. 412), Yeb. 62a (Sonc. ed., p. 412).</i>—as everywhere else he acted on the command of God, so here, too, he acted on the command of God.<br>R. ‘Aḳiba said: Moses only broke the tablets because he was commanded to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>And I took hold of the two tables</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IX, 17.</i>—what is it that a man takes hold of? Is it not that which he is about to break?<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A difficult sentence, the text being dubious. The meaning seems to be: since Moses held the tablets firmly he certainly had no intention of breaking them; as he did break them it must have been at the behest of the Almighty. The text varies between <i>leshabberan</i> and <i>lebor’an;</i> the latter makes no sense unless the root <i>bara’</i> be taken in the sense of ‘cut down, destroy’ (cf. Josh. 17, 15, 18, and Ezek. 23, 47).</i><br>R. Meir said: Moses only broke the tablets because he was commanded to do so by the Almighty, as it is stated, <i>Which</i> [’asher] <i>thou didst break</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 10, 2.</i>—I thank [<i>yishar</i>] you for breaking them.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The words <i>’asher</i> (which) and <i>yishar</i> (thank) are homiletically explained as deriving from a common root.</i>
Hezekiah king of Judah did four things [of his own accord] and his acts conformed to the will of the All-present. He hid the Book of Healing<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">A book containing remedies for various ailments believed to have been compiled by King Solomon. Hezekiah hid it so that people should pray to God for healing. Cf. Ber. 10b (Sonc. ed., p. 56), Pes. 56a (Sonc. ed., pp. 277f). In the Talmud six acts are mentioned, God approving of three and disapproving of three.</i> and his act conformed to the will of the All-present, [as it is stated, <i>And I have done that which is good in Thy sight</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 20, 3. The words in brackets are inserted by GRA.</i> He broke the brazen serpent<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Num. 21, 9.</i> into pieces and his act conformed to the will of the All-present, [as it is stated, <i>And he broke in pieces the brazen serpent … for unto those days the children of Israel did offer to it; and it was called Nehushtan</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 18, 4. The verse is omitted in V but is found in MS. E and inserted by GRA.</i> He removed the high places and the altars and his act conformed to the will of the All-present, as it is stated, <i>Hath not the same Hezekiah taken away His high places and His altars, and commanded Judah and Jerusalem, saying: Ye shall worship before one altar, and upon it shall ye offer?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">2 Chron. 32, 12.</i> He stopped up the waters of Gihon, and his act conformed to the will of the All-present, as it is stated, <i>Hezekiah also stopped the upper spring of the waters of Gihon, and brought them straight down on the west side of the city of David. And Hezekiah prospered in all his works</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 30. The last words of the verse indicate that his <i>works</i> had divine approval.</i>
What fence did Job make to his words? It states, <i>A whole-hearted and an upright man, one that feareth God, and shunneth evil</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Job 1, 8.</i> This<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The phrase <i>feareth God and shunneth evil</i>.</i> teaches that Job held aloof from anything that could lead to sin, from anything improper or having the semblance of impropriety.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">These were the ‘fences’ which Job erected to safeguard him from wrong-doing. In view of the description <i>that feareth God</i>, etc., what precedes it seems superfluous.</i> And what is the significance of the words <i>a whole-hearted and an upright man?</i> They teach that Job was born circumcised.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. <i>tam</i>, translated ‘whole-hearted’, is interpreted in the sense of physically perfect, the lack of circumcision being regarded as an imperfection.</i><br>Adam, the first man, also came into the world circumcised, for it is stated, <i>And God created man in His own image</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 27. ‘In God’s image’ implies perfection.</i> Seth also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>And begot a son in his own likeness, after his image</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> V, 3, physically perfect like his father.</i> Noah also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>In his generations a man righteous and whole-hearted</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VI, 9.</i> Shem also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>And Melchizedek king of Salem</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIV, 18. The Heb. <i>shalem</i> also signifies physical perfection. [On the identification of Shem with Melchizedek, cf. L. Ginzberg, <i>The Legends of the Jews</i>, V, pp. 225f.]</i> Jacob also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>And Jacob was a whole-hearted man, dwelling in tents</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXV, 28, E.V. <i>a quiet man</i> (Heb. <i>tam</i>).</i> Joseph also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>These are the generations of Jacob: Joseph</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXVII, 2.</i> Surely Scripture should have said here, ‘These are the generations of Jacob: Reuben!’<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Reuben was Jacob’s firstborn.</i> Why does it say <i>Joseph?</i> [To teach] that as Jacob was born circumcised so Joseph was born circumcised. Moses also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>She saw him that he was a goodly child</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 2, 2. <i>Goodly</i> indicates physically perfect.</i> Now what did his mother see in him finer and better than in all children? [She saw] that he was born circumcised. Even the wicked Balaam was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>The saying of him who heareth the words of God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 24, 4. God would not have communicated with one who was uncircumcised.</i> Samuel also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>And the child Samuel grew up, increased and was good</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 2, 26. E.V., <i>increased in favour</i>.</i> David also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>Michtam of David. Keep me, O God; for I have taken refuge in Thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 16, 1. The word <i>michtam</i> is interpreted in Soṭ. 10b (Sonc. ed., p. 49) as <i>makkatho tammah</i>, ‘his wound was whole’, i.e. the place where there should have been a wound after the operation of circumcision was sound, since he was born circumcised.</i> Jeremiah also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>Before I formed thee in the belly I knew thee, and before thou camest forth out of the womb I sanctified thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 1, 5. Since he was consecrated even before birth he must have been formed perfect in body, i.e. already circumcised.</i> Zerubbabel also was born circumcised, for it is stated, <i>In that day, saith the Lord of hosts, will I take thee, O Ẓerubbabel, My servant, the son of Shealtiel, saith the Lord, and I will make of thee as a signet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Hag. 2, 23. The Heb. for ‘signet’, <i>ḥotam</i>, suggests the seal of circumcision. Alternatively, since the word ends with the syllable <i>tam</i> it suggests physical perfection, as above, p. 24, n. 67.</i><br>Scripture states, <i>I made a covenant with mine eyes; how then should I look upon a maid?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Job 31, 1. After the digression, the subject of Job making a ‘fence’ is resumed.</i> This teaches that Job was most strict with himself and would not even glance at a maiden. Is there not here an argument from the less to the greater? If in the case of an unmarried girl whom, if he so wish, he may marry or may give as a wife either to his son or his brother or some other relative of his, Job was most strict with himself and would not glance at her, how much more strict was he in the case of a married woman! Why was Job so strict with himself and would not even glance at a maid? Because he reasoned, ‘Perhaps I look at her to-day, and to-morrow a man comes and takes her to wife; the consequence might be that I should be thinking of a married woman’.
What fence did the Prophets make to their words? It states, <i>The Lord will go forth as a mighty man, He will stir up jealousy like a man of war; He will cry, yea, He will shout aloud</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 42, 13.</i> Surely He is not like one mighty man, but like all the mighty men of the world put together! Similarly, <i>The lion hath roared, who will not fear? The Lord God hath spoken, who can but prophesy?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Amos 3, 8.</i> Surely He is not like one lion, but like all the lions of the world put together! Similarly, <i>And, behold, the glory of the God of Israel came from the way of the east, and His voice was like the sound of many waters; and the earth did shine with His glory</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Ez. 43, 2.</i> [We know that] the phrase <i>the sound of many waters</i> refers to the angel Gabriel, and the words <i>and the earth did shine</i> [19<i>a</i>] <i>with His glory</i> refer to the manifestation of the Divine Presence. Is there not here an argument from the less to the greater? If of the angel Gabriel, who is but one of the countless thousands and myriads who stand before Him, it is said that his voice reaches from one end of the world to the other, how much mightier is the voice of the King of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He, Who created the entire universe and created the celestial and terrestrial beings! But the eye of man is shown only that which it can perceive, and the ear of man is made to hear only that which it can comprehend.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the three examples quoted from the Prophets it is obvious that the expressions used are inadequate to describe the glory and might of God; the Prophets, however, deliberately employed them so that thereby they conveyed to the people an idea of God’s majesty, using terms which could be comprehended by the limited understanding of man. This choice of expressions and similes is the ‘fence’ which the Prophets made to their words.</i>
What fence did [the authors of] the Holy Writings make to their words? It states, <i>Remove thy way far from her, and come not nigh the door of her house</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 5, 8.</i> <i>Remove thy way far from her</i> refers to heresy; for a man is warned, ‘Go not among heretics, nor come in to them, lest you stumble through them’. Should he, however, say, ‘I am sure of myself, and even though I go among them I will not stumble through them’; or should he say, ‘I will hear their words and then come away’? Scripture therefore states, <i>None that go unto her return, neither do they attain unto the paths of life</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> II, 19.</i> It is also written, <i>She hath prepared her meat, she hath mingled her wine; she hath furnished her table</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IX, 2.</i> This verse describes the wicked [heretics].<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The word ‘heretics’ is omitted in the text, due, no doubt, to fear of the censor. It is added here by GRA.</i> When a man comes among them they give him to eat and drink, they clothe and cover him, and give him much money. But no sooner does he become one of them, than each recognizes what was his property and takes it back. Concerning these it is stated, <i>Till an arrow strike through his liver; as a bird hasteneth to the snare—and knoweth not that it is at the cost of his life</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VII, 23.</i><br>Another interpretation of <i>Remove thy way far from her</i> refers to the harlot; for a man is warned, ‘Go not in this square, enter not in this side-way, for a harlot is there, attractive and bedecked’. Should he, however, say, ‘I am sure of myself, and even if I go there I will not stumble through her’, he is again warned, ‘Go not there even if you are sure of yourself, lest you stumble through her’. For the Sages have said: A man should not make it his habit to pass the door of a harlot, as it is stated, <i>For she hath cast down many wounded; yea, a mighty host are all her slain</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 26.</i>
What fence did the Sages make to their words? They ruled that the evening <i>Shema‘</i> may be recited only until midnight, although Rabban Gamaliel said: Until cockcrow.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the opening paragraph of Mishnah Berakoth. The Sages were also of the opinion that by strict law the <i>Shema‘</i> may be recited at any time until the morning, but as a precautionary measure they ruled only until midnight. This is an example of a ‘fence’ by the Sages.</i> How is this? When a man comes home from his work, let him not say, ‘I will eat a little, drink a little, sleep a little, and then I will recite the <i>Shema‘’;</i> it may result in his sleeping through the whole night and he will have missed reciting it. He should rather act thus: when he comes home from his work in the evening let him go to the Synagogue or the House of Study. If he is in the habit of reading Scripture, let him read it; if he is in the habit of studying the Mishnah, let him study it; otherwise, let him recite the <i>Shema‘</i> and say the <i>Tefillah;</i> and whosoever transgresses the words of the Sages is deserving of death.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ber. 4b (Sonc. ed., p. 13).</i><br>Rabban Gamaliel said: There are times when a man recites the <i>Shema‘</i> twice in one night, once before the rise of dawn and once after the rise of dawn, and he thereby fulfils his obligation both for the day and for the night.<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ber. 8b (Sonc. ed., p. 44).</i><br>The Sages rose up and often made fences to their words.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i>, pp. 26-29 for an original and interesting interpretation of this sentence.</i>
<small>AND RAISE UP MANY DISCIPLES</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Added by all commentators as the heading of the paragraph.</i> The School of Shammai said: A master should teach only those who are wise, meek, of good family and wealthy; whereas the School of Hillel said: He should teach everyone, for many sinners in Israel have in this way been brought near to the study of the Torah, and from them issued righteous, pious and worthy men.

Chapter 3

R. ‘Aḳiba said: He who takes a <i>peruṭah</i> from charity when not in need of it will not depart from this world before he falls in need of his fellow-men. He also said: He who wraps rags over his eyes or about his loins, and cries, ‘Help the blind! Help the leper!’<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘one who is afflicted with boils (or, ulcers)’.</i> will in the end cry so in earnest. He also said: He who throws his bread to the ground, or scatters his money in his anger, will not depart from this world before he falls in need of his fellow-men.
He also said: He who tears his clothes in his anger or breaks his vessels in his rage, will in the end worship idols; for such is the device of the evil inclination: to-day it bids man, ‘Tear your clothes’ and to-morrow it bids him, ‘Worship idols’.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shab. 105b (Sonc. ed., p. 510) where it is quoted by R. Simeon b. Eleazar in the name of Ḥalfa b. Agra in R. Joḥanan b. Nuri’s name.</i>
He also said: He who hopes for the death of his wife that he may inherit her property or that he may marry her sister, or hopes for the death of his brother<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Without issue, so that the law of Deut. 25, 5 might apply.</i> that he may marry the widow, will surely be survived by them.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in the end they will bury him during their lifetime’.</i> Concerning such a person Scripture states, <i>He that diggeth a pit shall fall into it, and whoso breaketh through a fence, a serpent shall bite him</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 10, 8.</i>
The story is told<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. B.Ḳ. 90b (Sonc. ed., p. 521).</i> of a certain man who, in contravention of R. ‘Aḳiba’s teaching, unloosed a woman’s hair in the marketplace. When the woman came before R. ‘Aḳiba he ordered the man to pay her the sum of four hundred <i>zuz</i>. The man said, ‘Rabbi, give me time to pay’, and he agreed.
