[When the man had come out] a friend of his said to him, ‘I will advise you how you will not have to pay her even one <i>peruḳah</i>’. ‘Do advise me’, said the man; and the friend said, ‘Go and take oil to the value of an <i>issar</i>, and break the jar in front of the door of that woman’. [He did so.] What did the woman do? She came out of her house, unloosed her hair in the open street, scooped up [the oil with her hand], and then laid her hand on her head.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">She rubbed the oil into her head in order not to waste it.</i> The man had arranged witnesses against her, and he proceeded before R. ‘Aḳiba and said, ‘To such a worthless woman must I pay four hundred <i>zuz?</i> For the sake of oil worth an <i>issar</i> she lost all self-respect, came out of her house, unloosed her hair in the open street, scooped up the oil, and then laid her hand on her head!’ R. ‘Aḳiba replied, ‘Your words prove nothing. For he who wounds himself, though he is not permitted to do so, is free from liability, whereas if others wounded him they are liable. If this woman wounds herself she is free from liability, but you, having wounded her, must pay her four hundred <i>zuz</i>’.
R. Dosethai b. R. Jannai said: If you have prepared your soil<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘selected’ or cleared (the soil)’. MS. E reads ‘set out early’; GRA suggests ‘plowed’.</i> and sown your seed at the first rainfall<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">There are three periods of rain expected in the early winter: the first from the 17th to the 23rd of the eighth month (Marḥeshwan), the second from the 23rd to the end of the month, the third from the 1st of the ninth month (Kislew) and onwards.</i> go and sow again at the second rainfall, lest hail fall in the world and the first sowing will be affected while the second sowing may endure, <i>for thou knowest not which shall prosper, whether this or that, or whether they both shall be alike good</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 11, 6.</i> or both alike bad, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed, and in the evening withhold not thy hand</i>. If you have prepared your soil and sown your seed at the first and second rainfalls, go and sow again at the third rainfall, lest a blight come upon the world and the early sowings will be blasted while the late sowing may endure, as it is stated, <i>For thou knowest not which shall prosper, whether this or that, or whether they both shall be alike good</i>, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed</i>, etc.
R. Ishmael b. R. Jose said: Study Torah in your old age. If you have studied Torah in your youth, say not, ‘I will not study in my old age’; but continue studying <i>for thou knowest not which shall prosper</i>. If you studied in a time of wealth do not leave off<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘turn away’; according to a variant reading ‘sit idle’.</i> in a time of poverty; if in a time of plenty do not leave off in a time of famine; if in a time of ease do not leave off in a time of hardship; because one word [learnt] by a man in adversity is more precious to him than a hundred learnt in prosperity, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed, and in the evening withhold not thy hand</i>.<br>R. ‘Aḳiba said: If you have studied Torah in your youth, study it also in your old age, and say not, ‘I will not study Torah in my old age’, <i>for thou knowest not which shall prosper</i>, whether both will remain with you, <i>or whether they both shall be alike good</i>, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed</i>, etc.<br>R. Meir said: If you have studied under one teacher, say not, ‘It is enough for me’; but go to [another] scholar and study Torah under him. Go not, however, to anyone, but to him who is akin to you from the outset,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. whose style and manner of study approximates to your own. Alternatively <i>ḳarob</i> means ‘near’, i.e. who resides near your house. Another reading is <i>ḳarob lah</i> ‘near to her’, i.e. a scholar who is sincere and near to the Torah.</i> as it is stated, <i>Drink waters out of thine own cistern, and running waters out of thine own well</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 5, 15. The verse counsels one to learn from such sources as are similar in scope to one’s own springs of learning.</i> It is incumbent upon a man to attend upon three scholars [19<i>b</i>] such as R. Eliezer, R. Joshua and R. ‘Aḳiba, as it is stated, <i>Happy is the man that hearkeneth to me, watching daily at my gates, waiting at the posts of my door</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VIII, 34.</i>—read not <i>my gates</i> but ‘the door of my gates’.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Thus arriving at the number ‘three’: ‘door’ suggests one and ‘gates’ in the plural a further two; hence the need to study under three teachers. The text, however, is difficult. In the Yalḳuṭ on this verse the text reads: ‘It says not <i>delathai</i> but <i>dalthothai</i>’, and the explanation is that the former term, being in the plural, implies two, therefore the latter term, which is in an amplified form, implies three.</i> For<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This is the continuation of R. Meir’s dictum, explaining why it is necessary to study under more than one master.</i> you know not whether both will remain with you <i>or whether they both shall be alike good</i>, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed</i>, etc.<br>R. Joshua said: Take a wife in your youth and take a wife in your old age; beget children in your youth and beget children in your old age. Say not, ‘I will no more take a wife’, but take her and beget sons and daughters and increase your offspring on the earth, because you know not whether both<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the children of your youth and the children of your later years.</i> will remain with you <i>or whether both shall be alike good</i>, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed</i>, etc.
He used to say: If you have given a <i>peruṭah</i> to a poor man in the morning, and there comes to you in the evening another poor man asking for alms, give to him also, because you know not whether both<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the two acts of charity.</i> will remain with you <i>or whether they both shall be alike good</i>, as it is stated, <i>In the morning sow thy seed</i>, etc.
The story is told<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ber. 18b (Sonc. ed., pp. 110f).</i> of a certain pious man who, in a year of drought, gave a poor man a <i>dinar</i>. His wife scolded him for it; so he went and passed the night in a cemetery. There he overheard two spirits conversing with one another. ‘Come, my dear,’ said one to her companion, ‘let us wander about the world and find out what calamity is about to come upon the world.’<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Talmudic version the incident happened on the eve of the New Year when the fate of the world in the coming year is decided.</i> The other replied, ‘My dear, I cannot go forth because I am buried in a matting of reeds;<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. a shroud of inferior quality and not the usual linen shroud.</i> but you go and tell me whatever you hear’. She departed and on her return was asked, ‘Did you hear from behind the curtain<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Which screens the heavenly Court of Justice.</i> what calamity is about to come upon the world?’ She replied, ‘I heard that whoever sows at the first rainfall will have his crops smitten by hail’. The man went and sowed at the second rainfall, with the result that everybody’s crop was smitten but his was not. The following year he again went and passed the night in the cemetery and overheard the same two spirits conversing with one another. ‘Come,’ said one to her companion, ‘let us wander about the world and find out what calamity is about to come upon the world.’ The other replied, ‘My dear, have I not previously told you that I cannot go forth because I am buried in a matting of reeds? You go, and tell me whatever you hear’. She departed and on her return her companion asked her, ‘Did you hear anything from behind the curtain?’ She replied, ‘I heard that whoever sows at the second rainfall will have his crops blasted’. He went and sowed at the first rainfall. When the blight came into the world, everybody’s crop was blasted but his was not. His wife asked him, ‘How was it that when the calamity came into the world, everybody’s crops were smitten and blasted but yours were not?’ He told her the whole story. After a time a quarrel broke out between that pious man’s wife and the mother of the young woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Who had been buried like a pauper in a reed matting.</i> and the former said to the other, ‘Come and I will show you your daughter buried in a matting of reeds!’ The following year the man went and spent the night in the cemetery and overheard those two spirits conversing with one another. One said, ‘Come, my dear, let us wander about the world and listen to what is said behind the curtain’. But her companion replied, ‘My dear, leave me alone; what has passed between you and me has in the past been overheard by the living’.
It is told of a certain pious man who was in the habit of dispensing charity that he was once travelling in a ship when a storm arose and the ship was sunk in the sea. R. ‘Aḳiba who witnessed [the tragedy] came before the Beth Din to testify on behalf of the wife to enable her to remarry. Before the time came for him to testify, that pious man entered and stood before him. R. ‘Aḳiba said to him, ‘You are the man who was drowned at sea’. ‘Yes, I am,’ he replied. ‘And who brought you up from the sea?’ asked R. ‘Aḳiba. He answered, ‘The charity which I dispensed brought me up from the sea’. ‘How do you know it?’ asked R. ‘Aḳiba. He replied, ‘When I went down to the nethermost depths, I heard a mighty roaring of the waves calling one to the other, “Hurry and let us bring up this man from the sea because he dispensed charity all his days” ’. At this R. ‘Aḳiba began [to praise] saying, ‘Blessed be God, the God of Israel, Who has chosen the words of the Torah and the sayings of the wise; for the words of the Torah and the sayings of the wise endure for ever and to all eternity, as it is stated, <i>Cast thy bread upon the waters, for thou shalt find it after many days</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 11, 1.</i> It is also written, <i>Charity delivereth from death</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 10, 2, E.V., <i>righteousness delivereth</i>.</i>
It is told<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. B.B. 11a (Sonc. ed., p. 52).</i> of Benjamin the Just, who was the overseer of the charity fund, that one day a woman came to him and said, ‘Master, assist me’. He replied, ‘By the Temple Service, there is nothing left in the charity fund’. She said, ‘Master, if you do not assist me, I perish with my four<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In B.B. <i>loc. cit.</i> ‘seven’, and so corrected here by GRA.</i> children’. He thereupon assisted her from his own purse. After a time Benjamin the Just fell ill and lay in pain on his bed. The ministering angels addressed the Holy One, blessed be He, saying, ‘Sovereign of the universe, Thou hast said that whoever saves a single soul in Israel is deemed as though he had saved alive the whole world.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. 37a (Sonc. ed., p. 234).</i> How much greater must be the merit of Benjamin the Just who saved alive a widow and her four children! He is now on his bed suffering much pain in his sickness’. Thus they sought mercy on his behalf and the decree against him was annulled, twenty-two years being added to his life.

Chapter 4

<small>SIMON THE</small> J<small>UST WAS ONE OF THE LAST SURVIVORS OF THE GREAT ASSEMBLY. HE USED TO SAY: UPON THREE THINGS THE WORLD IS BASED: UPON THE TORAH, UPON THE TEMPLE SERVICE, AND UPON THE PRACTICE OF LOVINGKINDNESS</small>.<br><small>UPON THE TORAH</small>—how is this? It states, <i>For I desire mercy, and not sacrifice, and the knowledge of God rather than burnt-offerings</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Hos. 6, 6.</i> Hence one may infer that of all sacrifices the burnt-offering is the most acceptable since it is entirely consumed by fire, as it is stated, <i>And the priest shall make the whole smoke on the altar, for a burnt-offering</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 1, 9.</i> In another passage it states, <i>And Samuel took a sucking lamb, and offered it for a whole burnt-offering unto the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 7, 9. Samuel’s prayer was immediately answered by God on account of the acceptability of the burnt-offering.</i> Nevertheless, the study of Torah is even more acceptable to the All-present than burnt-offerings, since if a man studies Torah he knows the will of the All-present, as it is stated, <i>Then shalt thou understand the fear of the Lord, and find out the will of God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 2, 5. E.V., <i>find the knowledge of God</i>.</i> Hence it follows that when a scholar sits and expounds the Torah in an assembly, Scripture accounts it to him as though he had offered the fat and the blood [of a sacrifice] upon the altar.<br>Should<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This and the subsequent passages are inserted here to teach that though the study of the Torah is the highest good, even that may be interrupted for a wedding ceremony or a funeral procession in the circumstances stated here.</i> two scholars be sitting engaged in the study of the Torah, and a bridal procession or a cortège passes by them, if there are sufficient people present to attend to the needs of the occasion,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">As to what is considered sufficient, cf. Keth. 17a (Sonc. ed., p. 95).</i> let them not interrupt their studies; otherwise it is their duty to rise and cheer<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A rare word from the root <i>shanan</i>, ‘to repeat a thing’, and hence ‘ to ac claim’ or ‘applaud repeatedly’. Cf. Jastrow s.v. and the next note but one.</i> and applaud the bride or accompany the dead to the grave.
It once happened that R. Judah b. Ilai was sitting and lecturing to his disciples when a bride passed before him. At once he took in his hand what was usual for the occasion,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. myrtle branches. The text is uncertain, and GRA reads: ‘as there were not sufficient people for her needs’.</i> and cheered<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel passage in <i>Midrash Haggadol</i>, ed. Schechter, p. 82, the word is not <i>meshannen</i> (var. <i>meshannenen</i>) but <i>mesha’anen</i> from a root <i>sha‘an</i> ‘to shake, wave about’. The text there reads: ‘He took in his hand a myrtle branch and waved it until the bride had passed’.</i> the bride until she had passed by.
On another occasion while R. Judah b. Ilai was sitting and lecturing to his disciples, a bride passed before him. ‘What was that?’ he asked, and they replied, ‘A bridal party’. He said to them, ‘My sons, arise and attend to the bride, for so we find that the Holy One, blessed be He, attended to a bride, as it is stated, <i>And the Lord God built up the rib</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 2, 22; so the text lit.</i> And if He attended to a bride, how much more should we do so!’ Where do we find that the Holy One, blessed be He, attended to a bride? For it is stated, <i>And the Lord God built up the rib;</i> and in coastal towns a bride is referred to as <i>ben’itha</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. ‘with hair plaited’. The Talmud, Ber. 61a (Sonc. ed., p. 382), Shab. 95a (Sonc. ed., p. 454), connects the Heb. root for ‘build’ (<i>banah</i>) with <i>binyatha</i>, a word used in the coastal towns for ‘plaits’, and explains that God plaited Eve’s hair.</i> Hence we learn that the Holy One, blessed be He, equipped Eve, adorned her as a bride and conducted her to Adam, as it is stated, <i>And He brought her unto Adam</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. <i>ibid.</i></i><br>On one occasion only did the Holy One, blessed be He, act as a groomsman to man; thereafter a man provides his own groomsman, as it is stated, <i>Bone of my bone, and flesh of my flesh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 23.</i> On one occasion only was the wife formed out of the man;<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘was Eve taken from Adam’.</i> thereafter a man takes the daughter of his fellow to wife.
<small>UPON THE TEMPLE SERVICE</small>—how is this? As long as the Temple Service continued, the world was a source of blessing to its inhabitants and the rains fell [20<i>a</i>] in their seasons, as it is stated, <i>To love the Lord your God, and to serve Him with all your heart and with all your soul, that I will give the rain of your land in its season, the former rain and the latter rain…. And I will give grass in thy fields for thy cattle</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 11, 13-15.</i> But when the Temple Service ceased, the world was no longer a source of blessing to its inhabitants and the rains did not fall in their season, as it is stated, <i>Take heed to yourselves, lest your heart be deceived … and He shut up the heaven, so that there shall be no rain</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 16f.</i> And it also declares in the Book of Haggai, <i>I pray you, consider from this day and forward—before a stone was laid upon a stone in the temple of the Lord, through all that time, when one came to a heap of twenty measures, there were but ten; when one came to the winevat to draw out fifty measures, there were but twenty</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Hag. 2, 15f.</i> Why does not the verse also state in regard to the winevat ‘to draw out twenty measures, there were but ten’ as it does in regard to the wheat? Because the winevat is a significant omen, more so than wheat, thus teaching you that whenever the yield of wine is affected it is a bad omen for the whole year. [At that time] Israel said to the Holy One, blessed be He, ‘Sovereign of the universe! Why hast Thou done this to us?’ And the Divine Spirit replied, ‘<i>Ye looked for much, and, lo, it came to little … Because of My house that lieth waste, while ye run every man for his own house</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> I, 9.</i> But if you will devote yourselves to the service of the Temple, I will bless you as formerly, as it is stated, <i>Consider, I pray you … from the four-and-twentieth day of the ninth month, even from the day that the foundation of the Lord’s temple was laid … Is the seed yet in the barn? Yea, the vine, and the fig-tree, and the pomegranate, and the olive-tree hath not brought forth—from this day will I bless you</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> II, 18f.</i> Hence you learn that no service is more precious to the Holy One, blessed be He, than the Temple Service.
<small>UPON THE PRACTICE OF LOVINGKINDNESS</small>—how is this? Scripture states, <i>I desire lovingkindness and not sacrifice</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Hos. 6, 6. E.V., <i>I desire mercy</i>.</i> In the beginning the world was created by lovingkindness alone, as it is stated, <i>For I have said: The world is built by lovingkindness; Thou dost establish the heavens with Thy faithfulness</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 89, 3. E.V., <i>For ever is mercy built; in the very heavens Thou dost establish Thy faithfulness</i>.</i><br>It happened once that Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai was coming out of Jerusalem, followed by R. Joshua, and he beheld the Temple in ruins. ‘Woe to us,’ cried R. Joshua, ‘for this house that lies in ruins, the place where atonement was made for the sins of Israel!’ Rabban Joḥanan said to him, ‘My son, be not grieved, for we have another means of atonement which is as effective, and that is, the practice of lovingkindness, as it is stated, <i>For I desire lovingkindness and not sacrifice</i>’. And so we find it with Daniel, the man greatly beloved, who devoted himself to acts of lovingkindness. What were the acts of lovingkindness to which Daniel devoted himself? If you say that he offered burnt-offerings and peace-offerings in Babylon, had it not been previously ordained, <i>Take heed to thyself that thou offer not thy burnt-offerings in every place that thou seest; but in the place which the Lord shall choose in one of thy tribes, there thou shalt offer thy burnt-offerings</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 12, 13f.</i> What, then, were the acts of lovingkindness to which he devoted himself? He provided for the bride and made her to rejoice, he attended the dead to the grave, he gave alms to the poor, and prayed three times daily, and his prayer was accepted with favour, as it is stated, <i>And when Daniel knew that the writing was signed, he went into his house—now his windows were open in his upper chamber toward Jerusalem—and he kneeled upon his knees three times a day, and prayed, and gave thanks before his God, as he did aforetime</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Dan. 6, 11.</i><br>When<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This passage is quoted here to indicate the active steps taken by Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai to preserve Judaism although the Temple was destroyed.</i> Vespasian came to destroy Jerusalem, he proclaimed [to its inhabitants], ‘Fools, why do you seek to destroy this city and burn the Temple? What do I demand of you? Merely that you deliver to me one bow or one arrow<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A token of submission.</i> and then I will depart from you’. They answered him, ‘As we went forth against your two predecessors<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The allusion is uncertain. The version given here differs in many respects from other passages in Rabbinic literature relating to Vespasian; cf. Giṭ. 56b (Sonc. ed., p. 258). [S. Zeitlin, <i>Megillat Taanit</i>, p. 104, writes: “The two former generals were undoubtedly Florus and Cestius”, whose armies were defeated in 66 <small>C.E.</small>, but the generals were not killed by the Jews.]</i> and slew them, so we will go out against you and slay you’. When Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai heard this, he sent for the men of Jerusalem and said to them, ‘My sons, why would you destroy this city and burn the Temple? What does he demand of you? Merely one bow or one arrow, and then he will depart from you’. They answered him, ‘As we went forth against his two predecessors and slew them, so we will go out against him and slay him’.<br>Now Vespasian had some men of his resident [within and] close to the walls of Jerusalem, and whatever they heard they wrote upon an arrow which they shot beyond the wall. [In this manner] they reported that Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai was among Caesar’s friends. So Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai appealed to the men of Jerusalem. After he had pleaded with them for several days<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘one day and two and three days’.</i> and they would not agree with him, he sent for his disciples, R. Eliezer and R. Joshua, and said to them, ‘My sons, arise and carry me forth from here. Make a coffin for me and I will lie in it’. [They did so, and] R. Eliezer took hold of it at the head and R. Joshua at the foot, and they carried him until sunset when they arrived at the gates of Jerusalem. The gatekeepers asked, ‘What is this?’ They replied, ‘A dead man; do you not know that a corpse may not be kept overnight in Jerusalem?’<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. below XXXV, 2, p. 171.</i> They said, ‘If it is a corpse, carry it out’. They carried it out (and bore it until sunset)<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The words in brackets are clearly to be deleted.</i> when they arrived before Vespasian, opened the coffin and [Rabban Joḥanan] stood before him. He asked, ‘You are Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai? Ask what I shall give you’. He replied, ‘I ask for nothing but Jabneh whither I may go and teach my disciples, where I can institute prayers,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘the house of prayer’. A variant gives the curious reading: ‘the precept of <i>tefillin</i> (phylacteries)’.</i> and observe all the commandments prescribed in the Torah’. He said to him, ‘Go and do all that you wish to perform’. Rabban Joḥanan then said, ‘May I be permitted to say a word to you?’ ‘Speak,’ he said. Rabban Joḥanan then said, ‘You are about to become Emperor’. ‘How do you know this?’ he asked, and Rabban Joḥanan replied, ‘There is a tradition with us that the Temple will not be delivered into the hand of a common soldier but to a king, as it is stated, <i>And He shall cut down the thickets of the forest with iron, and Lebanon shall fall by a mighty one</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 10, 34. Cf. Giḳ., <i>loc. cit.</i>, where <i>Lebanon</i> is identified with the Temple and <i>mighty one</i> is applied to a king. [In Jewish War III, viii, 9 Josephus relates that he made the same prediction to Vespasian.]</i> It is related that a few days had not elapsed before there came to him a despatch<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘two deputies’; so Rashi in Ta‘an. 18b. [This interpretation is accepted by Krauss, <i>Lehnwörter</i>, p. 201.]</i> from his city [Rome], informing him that Caesar had died, and he had been elected Emperor. They brought to him a battering-ram which he directed<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <i>wesamah</i> as suggested by GRA. The word in V, <i>wetifa’</i>, is apparently an abbreviation which has so far defied satisfactory explanation.</i> towards the wall of Jerusalem. Then they brought to him logs of cedar which he inserted in the battering-ram and hurled at the wall until he breached it. They then brought to him a pig’s head which he inserted in the battering-ram and shot it on to the sacrificial limbs that were upon the altar. At that moment Jerusalem was captured. Rabban Joḥanan was sitting and anxiously waiting for news, as Eli had sat and waited for news, as it is stated, <i>Lo, Eli sat upon his seat by the wayside, watching; for his heart trembled for the ark of God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 4, 13.</i> As soon as Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai heard that Jerusalem was destroyed and the Temple in flames, he rent his clothes, his disciples also rent their clothes, and they wept and cried out and lamented.<br>Scripture states,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Some attach the verse which is cited to the preceding paragraph, thus: ‘and lamented saying, <i>Open thy doors</i>’ etc.</i> <i>Open thy doors, O Lebanon, that the fire may devour thy cedars</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Zech, 11, 1.</i> This alludes to the chief priests who were in the Temple, took their keys in their hands and hurled them upwards, exclaiming before the Holy One, blessed be He, ‘Lord of the universe! here are Thy keys which Thou didst entrust to us, seeing that we have not been faithful treasurers to discharge the duties imposed by the King, and we are no longer worthy to eat from the King’s table’.<br>The patriarchs, Abraham, Isaac and Jacob, and the twelve tribes also wept and cried out and lamented, saying, <i>Wail, O cypress-tree, for the cedar is fallen, because the glorious ones are spoiled;</i> [<i>wail, O ye oaks of Bashan, for the strong forest is come down</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 2.</i> <i>Wail, O cypress-tree, for the cedar is fallen</i> refers to the Temple; <i>because the glorious ones are spoiled</i> refers to Abraham, Isaac and Jacob and the twelve tribes; <i>wail, O ye oaks of Bashan</i> refers to Moses, Aaron and Miriam; <i>for the strong forest is come down</i> refers to the Holy of Holies. <i>Hark! the wailing of the shepherds, for their glory is spoiled</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 3.</i> refers to David and his son Solomon. <i>Hark! the roaring of young lions, for the thickets of the Jordan are spoiled</i> refers to Elijah and Elisha.
In three things did the Holy One, blessed be He, make human beings to differ one from the other: in voice, in temperament<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘pleasantness’.</i> and in features.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel passage in Sanh. 38a (Sonc. ed., p. 240) the text reads: ‘in voice, in appearance, and in mind’.</i> Why in voice? The Holy One, blessed be He, made the voices of men different one from the other; for had He not done so, immorality would have increased in the world, since as soon as a man left his home another might enter and ravish his wife in his own house. Therefore did the Holy One, blessed be He, make men differ from each other in voice, one man’s voice being different from his fellow’s. Why in temperament? The Holy One, blessed be He, made the temperament of men different one from the other; because had He not done so men would be jealous of each other. Therefore did the Holy One, blessed be He, make mankind differ from one another in temperament, one man’s temperament being different from his fellow’s. Why in appearance? The Holy One, blessed be He, made the features of human beings different one from another; because had He not done so, the daughters of Israel would not recognize their husbands nor the husbands their wives. Therefore did the Holy One, blessed be He, make them different in facial features.

Chapter 5

A<small>NTIGONOS OF</small> S<small>OCHO RECEIVED THE TRADITION FROM</small> S<small>IMON THE</small> J<small>UST. HE USED TO SAY: BE NOT</small> [20<i>b</i>] <small>LIKE SERVANTS WHO SERVE THE MASTER FOR THE SAKE OF RECEIVING A REWARD</small>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘gratuity’; so Maimonides <i>ad loc.</i></i> <small>BUT BE LIKE SERVANTS WHO SERVE THE MASTER WITHOUT THE EXPECTATION OF RECEIVING A REWARD; AND LET THE FEAR OF HEAVEN BE UPON YOU</small>, in order that your reward may be double in the world to come.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The last clause, ‘in order … to come’, is not found in the statement of Antigonos as reported in Aboth 1:3 (Sonc. ed., p. 3), and apparently did not form part of the original teaching. It actually contradicts the doctrine of disinterested service. Moreover, if these words formed part of the teaching, there would be no foundation for the arguments of the disciples stated in the following paragraph.</i>
Antigonos of Socho had two disciples<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Zadok and Boethus mentioned below.</i> who repeated this doctrine to their disciples and their disciples to their disciples. They rose up and examined the matter, saying, ‘Why did our predecessors say this? Is it right for a labourer to toil all day and not receive his reward in the evening? Had our predecessors known that there was another world and that there would be the resurrection of the dead they surely would not have taught thus!’ So they arose and turned away from the Torah. Two sects sprang from them: the Sadducees and the Boeth-usians,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For the historical origin and doctrines of these two sects, cf. J.E. under their respective headings. [On this passage, cf. Finkelstein, <i>The Pharisees</i>, p. 80 and p. 663, n. 20.]</i> the Sadducees named after Zadok and the Boethusians after Boethus. All their lives they used vessels of silver and gold, but not<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">GRA omits ‘not’; the text is uncertain. For a recent interpretation of this passage, cf. Finkelstein, <i>Journal of Biblical Literature</i>, vol. LVII, pp. 35-37.</i> because they were arrogant in mind. The Sadducees said that it was a tradition of the Pharisees to subject themselves to austerity in this world,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">[Cf. Josephus, Antiquities XVIII, i, 3, ‘The Pharisees live simply, and despise delicacies’.]</i> and in the world to come they possess nothing at all.

Chapter 6

J<small>OSE B</small>. J<small>OEZER SAID: LET YOUR HOUSE BE A MEETING-HOUSE FOR THE WISE</small>. What is meant by this? It teaches that a man’s house should be available to the wise, their disciples, and the disciples of their disciples; in the same manner that a man might say to his fellow, ‘I will wait for you at such and such a place’.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">So should your house be the rendezvous of scholars.</i><br>Another interpretation of <small>LET YOUR HOUSE BE A MEETING-HOUSE FOR THE WISE</small>: this means that when a disciple comes into your presence and says to you, ‘Give me instruction’, if it is within your power to teach him, instruct him, but if not dismiss him at once.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Your house should be a place for instruction and study; if therefore you are not in a position to impart knowledge, do not detain the would-be learner, but let him seek instruction elsewhere.</i> Moreover, let him not sit before you on a couch or on a chair or on a bench, but on the ground; and every word that you utter he should accept with awe and reverence, with fear and dread.
<small>AND SIT AMIDST THE DUST OF THEIR FEET</small>. What is meant by this? When a scholar comes to a town, say not, ‘I have no need of him’; but go to him, and sit not before him on a couch or on a chair or on a bench, but sit before him on the ground; and every word that he utters accept with awe and reverence, with fear and dread, in the same manner that our forefathers received the Torah at Mount Sinai with awe and reverence, with fear and dread.<br>Another interpretation of <small>AND SIT AMIDST THE DUST OF THEIR FEET</small>—as did R. Eliezer; <small>AND DRINK IN THEIR WORDS WITH THIRST</small>—as did R. ‘Aḳiba.<br>What was the beginning of R. ‘Aḳiba? It was said of him that at the age of forty he had learnt nothing at all. On one occasion, as he was standing by the mouth of a well, he enquired, ‘Who carved out this stone?’ They answered, ‘The water which constantly falls on it, day in and day out’. They continued, ‘ ‘Aḳiba, have you not read the verse, <i>The waters wear the stones?</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Job 14, 19.</i> Forthwith R. ‘Aḳiba applied to himself the following <i>a fortiori</i> argument: If the soft [water] can wear away the hard [stone], how much more can the words of the Torah, which are hard like iron, carve a way into my heart which is of flesh and blood! Immediately he turned to the study of the Torah. Both he and his son went and sat down before the school-teacher and said to him, ‘Master, teach us the Torah’. R. ‘Aḳiba took hold of the tablet by one end and his son by the other end, and on it the teacher wrote <i>’alef beth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The first and second letters of the Hebrew alphabet.</i> which he learnt, <i>’alef taw</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The first and last letters of the alphabet.</i> which he learnt, and then the Book of Leviticus which he also learnt.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">All the verbs used here are in the singular, obviously referring to ‘Aḳiba; but no further information is given of the son.</i> So he continued to study until he had learnt the whole Torah. He then went and sat before R. Eliezer and R. Joshua and said to them, ‘My masters, initiate me into the reasoning of the Mishnah’. As soon as they recited one <i>halakah</i> to him, he went away and sat down alone asking himself, ‘Why is <i>’alef</i> so written? Why is <i>beth</i> so written? Why is this stated?’ He thereupon returned to his masters and asked them, and held them up with his words.<br>R. Simeon b. Eleazar said, ‘I will illustrate this to you by a parable. To what can this be compared? To a stonemason who was quarrying stones in a mountain. One day he took his pick in his hand, went out and sat on the mountain, and chipped away small stones. When people came and asked him what he was doing, he told them, “I mean to uproot the mountain and cast it into the Jordan”. They said to him, “You cannot possibly uproot the whole mountain”. Nevertheless, he continued chipping away until it became the size of a large boulder. He inserted himself beneath it, unloosed it, uprooted it, and cast it into the Jordan, saying, “Here is not your place but there” ’.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The point of the parable is that by slow degrees, perseverance and skill, one can master even what seems impossible.</i> In this way did R. ‘Aḳiba glean the knowledge of Torah from<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘did to, act towards’.</i> R. Eliezer and R. Joshua. R. Ṭarfon said to him, ‘ ‘Aḳiba, it is of you that the verse speaks, <i>He bindeth the streams that they trickle not; and the thing that is hid bringeth he forth to light</i>.’<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Job 28, 11.</i> The things which were hidden from men R. ‘Aḳiba brought to light.<br>Every day he used to gather a bundle of wood, half of which he sold to provide food for himself, and the other half he used for his personal needs. His neighbours rose up against him and cried, ‘ ‘Aḳiba, you are ruining us with your smoke! Come, sell all [the wood] to us, and with the money you can buy oil, and study by the light of an oil lamp’. But he replied, ‘I derive many uses from [the wood]: first I study by its light, secondly I keep myself warm by it, and thirdly I am able to sleep on it’.<br>In the Hereafter [the example of] R. ‘Aḳiba will condemn all the poor; for when they will be charged, ‘Why did you not study Torah?’ and they plead, ‘Because we were too poor’, it will be retorted, ‘Was not R. ‘Aḳiba very poor and in straitened circumstances?’ And if they plead, ‘Because of our little children’, it will be retorted, ‘Did not R. ‘Aḳiba have many sons and daughters for whom he had to provide as well as his wife Rachel [and yet he studied the Torah]?’<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the last sentence is far from clear and the translation follows the reading suggested by GRA; cf. Schechter <i>ad loc.</i> According to Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i> p. 188, the original reading is: ‘many sons and daughters who were provided for by his wife Rachel’; i.e. the poor will be able to offer as defence that their wives were not like Rachel,</i><br>He was forty years old when he started to study Torah and by the end of thirteen years he taught Torah in public. It is said that he did not depart this life before he had enjoyed the luxury of tables of silver and gold, and ascended his bed by golden steps. His wife went out dressed in fine robes<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">There are many variants of the word in the text and the meaning is dubious. According to Jastrow, the word is <i>ḳarduṭin</i>, ‘a long-sleeved tunic’. [Krauss, <i>Lehnwörter</i>, p. 519, connects it with the Latin <i>scordisci</i>, ‘leather shoes’; or in his <i>Talmudische Archäologie</i> I, p. 182 (<i>soleae</i>) <i>corticeae</i>, ‘shoes with cork soles’.]</i> and wearing a ‘golden city’ tiara.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">An ornament of gold shaped like the city of Jerusalem worn by women; cf. Shab. 59a (Sonc. ed., p. 276).</i> When his disciples said to him, ‘Master, you put us to shame by the [lavish] way you treat her’, he replied, ‘Much hardship has she endured with me for the sake of the Torah’.
What was the beginning of R. Eliezer b. Hyrḳanos? He was twenty-two years old and had not yet studied Torah. One day he said [to his father], ‘I will go and study Torah under Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai’. His father Hyrḳanos replied, ‘You shall not taste a morsel of food until you shall have ploughed a complete furrow’. He rose early in the morning and ploughed a complete furrow. It is said that that day was the eve of the Sabbath, so that he went and dined at the house of his father-in-law. Another version is that he tasted nothing between six hours before the Sabbath and six hours after the Sabbath. As he was walking on his way he saw a stone which he thought<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">An unusual word in this context. MS. E. reads <i>herimah</i> (he lifted it up).</i> [to be food]; he took it and put it in his mouth—some say that it was cattle dung. He walked on until he came to an inn where he spent the night. He went and sat before Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai in Jerusalem. Soon an offensive smell came forth from his mouth; whereupon Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai said, ‘Eliezer, my son, have you eaten anything to-day!’ He did not answer. Again the question was put to him and he still remained silent. The innkeeper was sent for and asked, ‘Has Eliezer eaten with you?’ He replied, ‘I thought that perhaps he had eaten at your table, Master’. ‘I, too, thought that perhaps he had eaten at your table,’ said Rabban Joḥanan; ‘between us we might have lost R. Eliezer.’ He then said to Eliezer, ‘Just as an offensive smell came forth from your mouth, so shall there go forth from you a distinguished name in Torah’.<br>When his father Hyrḳanos heard that he was studying Torah under Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai, he declared, ‘I shall go [to Jerusalem] and prohibit my son Eliezer by vow [from deriving any benefit] from my estate’. It was said that on that day Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai was sitting and expounding the Torah in Jerusalem, and all the notables of Israel were sitting before him. On learning that Hyrḳanos had arrived, he posted watchmen and charged them, ‘If Hyrḳanos comes in and wishes to sit down,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Among the scholars in the back rows.</i> do not let him.’<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">He would then have to move forward to the front rows, thus coming closer to his son and witnessing his distinction.</i> When he came in and wished to sit down, they did not allow him to do so, and he was compelled to move forward<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘he was leaping and going forward’.</i> until he came to where Ben Ẓiẓith Hakkeseth, Naḳdimon b. Gorion, and Ben Kalba Sabua‘<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">These three men are named as being extremely wealthy in Giṭ. 56a (Sonc. ed., p. 256).</i> were seated, and he sat down among them trembling. It was said that on that day Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai turned his gaze upon R. Eliezer and bade him commence the discourse. He said, ‘I cannot do so’. The master as well as the disciples urged him, whereupon he began the discourse and expounded matters about which no ear had ever heard [the like]. At every utterance that came from his lips, Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai stood up and kissed him on the head; but R. Eliezer exclaimed, ‘My master, you have taught me the truth’. Before the time of adjournment had arrived, his father Hyrḳanos stood up and said, ‘My masters, I came here for the sole purpose of depriving my son Eliezer by vow of my property, but now [I declare] all my property assigned to my son Eliezer, [21<i>a</i>] and all his brothers stand dispossessed and deprived of everything’.<br>Why was Ẓiẓith Hakkeseth so named? Because he used to recline upon a silver couch at the head of all the notables of Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">On the basis of the explanation given here it has been argued that the correct form of the name is Ẓiẓith Hakkesef, i.e. ‘Ẓiẓith of the silver (couch)’. This variant has some MS. authority; cf. Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i> p. 135. In the Talmud (Giṭ. <i>loc. cit.</i>) he was so called because his fringes (<i>ẓiẓith</i>) used to trail on cushions (<i>keseth</i>), or his seat (<i>kisse’</i>) was among those of the Roman nobility.</i><br>It is related concerning the daughter of Naḳdimon b. Gorion that her bed had been arrayed at the cost of twelve thousand golden <i>dinars</i>, and that from one eve of the Sabbath to the next a Tyrian golden <i>dinar</i> was spent by her on sweetmeats. She was then a childless widow awaiting the decision of her brother-in-law.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In accordance with the law of Deut. 25, 5ff.</i><br>Why was Naḳdimon b. Gorion so named? Because the sun had again broken through for his sake.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Naḳdimon is connected with the root <i>naḳad</i> ‘to pierce, break through’, hence ‘to shine’ (of the sun).</i> Once<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ta‘an. 19b, 20a (Sonc. ed., pp. 97f).</i> when all Israel had come up to Jerusalem for the festival, there was no water for them to drink. Naḳdimon went to a certain nobleman and said to him, ‘Lend me twelve wells of water from now until such and such a day, and if I do not repay you twelve wells of water I will give you twelve talents of silver’. He fixed a time-limit for repayment. When the time came the nobleman sent him [the message], ‘Deliver to me either twelve wells of water or twelve talents of silver’. He replied, ‘There is still time during the day [for repayment]’. The nobleman sneeringly said to him, ‘There has been no rain the whole year, will it rain now?’ The nobleman repaired in a happy mood to the bath-house, while Naḳdimon b. Gorion entered the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">So according to GRA and Ta‘an. <i>loc. cit.</i> V reads: ‘House of Study.’ [Büchler, <i>Studies in Jewish History</i>, pp. 99f. holds that “though many legendary features are interwoven in this story, there is no ground for doubting the actual occurrence related.”]</i> wrapped himself in his cloak and stood up to pray. He said, ‘Lord of the universe! It is revealed and known before Thee that I did not act for my own glory or for the glory of my father’s house, but for Thy glory did I act that the pilgrims should have water to drink’. Immediately the sky became overcast and rain began to fall until the twelve wells were filled to overflowing. He then sent [a message] to that nobleman, ‘Deliver to me the money for the extra water which you have because of me’. He replied, ‘The sun has already set and the rain has fallen in my possession’. Naḳdimon returned to the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">So according to GRA and Ta‘an. <i>loc. cit.</i> V reads: ‘House of Study.’ [Büchler, <i>Studies in Jewish History</i>, pp. 99f. holds that “though many legendary features are interwoven in this story, there is no ground for doubting the actual occurrence related.”]</i> wrapped himself in his cloak and again stood up in prayer, saying, ‘Lord of the universe! Perform for me a miracle now as Thou didst perform before’. Immediately the wind blew, the clouds dispersed, and the sun shone through. When they met the nobleman said, ‘I know that it was only for your sake that the Holy One, blessed be He, disorganized His world’.<br>Why was Kalba Sabua‘ so named? Because whoever entered his house hungry like a dog came out fed to the full.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Kalba means ‘a dog’ and Sabua‘ ‘satiated’.</i><br>When the Emperor Vespasian came to destroy Jerusalem, the zealots sought to burn all his stores with fire. Kalba Sabua‘ said to them, ‘Why do you destroy this city and seek to burn all [my] stores with fire? Wait until I go in and ascertain what I have in the house’. He went in and found that he had enough to supply food for every citizen in Jerusalem for a period of twenty-two years. He immediately gave orders and the produce was stacked and cleaned, ground and sifted, kneaded and baked into bread, so providing a food-supply for every citizen in Jerusalem for twenty-two years. They, however, paid no heed to him. What did the men of Jerusalem do? They took the loaves of bread,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <i>ha’iggulim</i> for <i>ha’agalim</i> with Jastrow, p. 1066b.</i> cut them through with saws and soiled them with mud. In consequence the men of Jerusalem had to resort to cooking straw and eating it.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the famine that ensued.</i> Every man of Israel who was encamped near the walls of Jerusalem called out, ‘For a reward of five dates I will go down and fetch five heads of the enemy’. One man was given five dates, and he went down and brought back five heads of the men of Vespasian. When Vespasian examined the excrement of the besieged men and saw that it was without a trace of corn, he remarked to his soldiers, ‘If these men who subsist on straw only can slay so many of you, how many more of you would they slay if they were to eat and drink like you!’

Chapter 7

J<small>OSEPH B.</small> J<small>OḤANAN OF</small> J<small>ERUSALEM SAID: LET YOUR HOUSE BE OPENED WIDE, AND LET THE POOR BE MEMBERS OF YOUR HOUSEHOLD; AND TALK NOT MUCH WITH A WOMAN</small>.<br><small>LET YOUR HOUSE BE OPENED WIDE</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man’s house should be opened wide to the south, to the east, to the west and to the north, like Job who made four doors to his house. And why did Job make four doors to his house? So that the poor should not have the trouble of going round the entire house. He who came from the north entered straight ahead, and he who came from the south entered straight ahead, and so on all sides. For this reason Job made four doors to his house.<br><small>AND LET THE POOR BE MEMBERS OF YOUR HOUSEHOLD</small>. This does not mean literally that they should be members of your household, but that the poor should be able to talk freely about what they ate and drank in your house, just as the poor talked freely about what they had eaten and drunk in Job’s house. When the poor met, one would ask the other, ‘Whence do you come?’ and he would reply, ‘From Job’s house’. Or one would ask the other, ‘Where are you going?’ and he would reply, ‘To Job’s house’. When the great calamity befell him, he addressed the Holy One, blessed be He, saying, ‘Lord of the universe! Did I not feed the hungry and give drink to the thirsty, as it is stated, <i>If I have eaten my morsel myself alone, and the fatherless hath not eaten thereof?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Job 31, 17.</i> And did I not clothe the naked, as it is stated, <i>And warmed with the fleece of my sheep?</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 20.</i> The Holy One, blessed be He, answered him, ‘For all that, Job, you have not attained to one half the standard [of hospitality] displayed by Abraham. You remained in your house waiting for guests to come to you. To him who was accustomed to eat wheaten bread you gave wheaten bread, to him who was accustomed to eat meat you gave meat, and to him who was accustomed to drink wine you gave wine. Abraham, however, did not act so. He went abroad,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in the world’.</i> and when he met wayfarers he brought them to his home. Even to him who was unaccustomed to eat wheaten bread he gave wheaten bread, to him who was unaccustomed to eat meat he gave meat, and to him who was unaccustomed to drink wine he gave wine. Not only that; but he went and built large mansions along the roadways and placed in them a supply of food and drink, so that whoever entered ate and drank and blessed Heaven. Therefore was he granted happiness;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">And was blessed with a child in his old age. The Hebrew phrase is frequently used in connection with the Deity and the text could be rendered, ‘Therefore it afforded Him much satisfaction’.</i> and whatever the heart desired<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the mouth asked for’.</i> was to be found in Abraham’s home, as it is stated, <i>And Abraham planted a tamarisk-tree in Beer-sheba</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 21, 33. The Heb. for <i>tamarisk-tree</i> is <i>’eshel</i>, which by transposition of letters can produce the verb <i>sha’al</i>, ‘to ask for’. [Cf. Midrash Rabbah to Genesis <i>ad loc.</i>, LIV, 6 (Sonc. ed., p. 480): ‘R. Judah said: <i>’Eshel</i> means an orchard, the word meaning ask for whatever you wish, figs, grapes or pomegranates’.] See also Rashi on Keth. 8b where the word is regarded as made of the initial letters of <i>’akilah</i> (eating), <i>shethiyah</i> (drinking) and <i>lewiyyah</i> (escorting a guest on his way), descriptive of Abraham’s hospitality.</i>
Teach the members of your household humility; for when a man is humble and the members of his household are also humble, if a poor man comes to the door and asks, ‘Is your father at home?’ they will reply, ‘Yes, come in’. Before he even enters the house the table is already laid for him, and when he comes in he eats and drinks and blesses the name of Heaven. On this account that man is granted much happiness.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘and it affords Him much satisfaction’.</i> But when a man is not<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V omits ‘not’.</i> humble and the members of his household are ill-tempered, if a poor man comes to the door and asks, ‘Is your father at home?’ they will reply, ‘No’, rebuke him and drive him away with harsh words.<br>Another interpretation of ‘Teach the members of your household humility’. What does it mean? It teaches that when a man is humble and the members of his household are also humble, if he sets out to a land beyond the sea, he will say [with confidence], ‘I thank Thee, O Lord my God, that my wife is not engaged in strife with her neighbours’. His heart is not anxious, but his mind is at ease until the time of his return. But when a man is not humble and the members of his household are ill-tempered, if he sets out for a distant land, he will say [with apprehension], ‘May it be Thy will, O Lord my God, that my wife be not engaged in strife with her neighbours nor my children in conflict’. His heart is anxious and his mind ill at ease until the time of his return.<br><small>AND TALK NOT MUCH WITH A WOMAN.</small> This is so even though she be his wife, and needless to say if she be his fellow’s wife. For when a man talks much with a woman he brings evil upon himself, neglects the study of the Torah, and in the end will inherit Gehinnom.
Another interpretation of <small>AND TALK NOT MUCH WITH A WOMAN</small>. What does it mean? When a man has come into the House of Study and has not there received the respect due to him, or he has had an argument with his fellow, let him not go and report it to his wife, saying, ‘I had an argument with my neighbour; this is what he said to me and this is what I replied’. Thereby he disgraces himself, disgraces his wife and disgraces his neighbour; and his wife who previously held him in esteem [now inwardly] laughs at him. Moreover, when the neighbour hears of it, he exclaims, ‘Alas! words which concerned only me and him he has gone and gossiped to his wife’. The outcome is that this man disgraces himself, his wife and his neighbour.

Chapter 8

J<small>OSHUA B.</small> P<small>ERAḤIAH AND</small> N<small>ITTAI THE</small> A<small>RBELITE RECEIVED THE TRADITION FROM THE PRECEDING</small>. J<small>OSHUA B.</small> P<small>ERAḤIAH SAID: PROVIDE YOURSELF WITH A TEACHER, AND GET YOURSELF A COMPANION, AND JUDGE ALL MEN FAVOURABLY</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in the scale of innocence’.</i><br><small>PROVIDE YOURSELF WITH A TEACHER</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should provide himself with a permanent teacher from whom he may learn Scripture, Mishnah, Midrash, <i>halakah</i> and <i>’aggadoth</i>. Accordingly, points which the teacher omitted to tell him in Scripture he can teach him later in the Mishnah, [what he omitted in the Mishnah he can teach him later in the Midrash,]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">So in MS. E., omitted in V.</i> what he omitted in the Midrash he can teach him later in the <i>halakoth</i>, and finally what he omitted in the <i>halakoth</i> he can teach him in the <i>’aggadoth</i>. Consequently this man remains in his place and is replete with well-being and blessing.
R. Meir used to say: He who learns Torah from one teacher, to what can he be compared? To a man who has but one field; he sowed it partly with wheat and partly with barley, in another part he planted olive-trees and in still another fruit-trees. The consequence is that this man is [in one place and is] replete with well-being and blessing. When, however, a man learns from two or three teachers, he may be compared to a person who has many fields; in one he sowed wheat and in another barley, in a third he planted olive-trees and in yet another he planted fruit-trees. The result is that this man is always on the move<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">lit ‘dispersed’.</i> in the country and has no enjoyment of well-being and blessing.
<small>AND GET YOURSELF A COMPANION</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should get a companion for himself one who will eat with him, drink with him, read the Scriptures with him, study Mishnah with him, lodge with him, and reveal to him all secret lore, the mysteries of the Torah and the problems of everyday life. When they sit together engaged in the study of the Torah, if one of them errs in a <i>halakah</i> or in the opening passage of a chapter, or he pronounces what is unclean to be clean or what is clean to be unclean, or he declares what is forbidden to be permitted or what is permitted to be forbidden, his companion can correct him. [21<i>b</i>] Whence do we know that when his companion corrects him and continues to read with him they both receive a good reward for their labour? Because it is stated, <i>Two are better than one; because they have a good reward for their labour</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 4, 9.</i>
If three sit together and are engaged in the study of the Torah, the Holy One, blessed be He, accounts it to them as though they had formed a conclave<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. <i>’aguddah</i>, translated ‘conclave’, is frequently applied in other contexts to a collection consisting of three units, e.g. the <i>lulab</i> which is a combination of three plants: the palm, the myrtle and the willow.</i> before Him, as it is stated, <i>It is He that buildeth His upper chambers in the heaven, and hath founded His vault upon the earth; He that calleth for the waters of the sea, and poureth them out upon the face of the earth; the Lord is His name</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Amos 9, 6. The Heb. for <i>vault</i> is <i>’aguddah</i>, homiletically understood as ‘conclave’.</i> Hence you learn that if three sit together and are engaged in the study of the Torah, it is accounted to them as though they formed a conclave before the Holy One, blessed be He.
If two sit together and are engaged in the study of the Torah, their reward is set aside for them on high, as it is stated, <i>Then they that feared the Lord spoke one with another, and the Lord hearkened</i> … [<i>and a book of remembrance was written before Him, for them that feared the Lord, and that thought upon His name</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Mal. 3, 16. The expression <i>one with another</i> suggests a group of two.</i> Who are <i>they that feared the Lord?</i> Those who decide upon a course, [and declare their intention,] saying, ‘We will go to free the bound and redeem the captive’. The Holy One, blessed be He, provides them with the opportunity, and they proceed to do it forthwith.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is in accordance with the verse, <i>Thou shalt also decree a thing, and it shall be established unto thee</i> (Job 22, 28); for God abets the good intentions of the righteous.</i> And who are <i>they that thought upon His name?</i> Those who merely think in their hearts [without declaring an intention], ‘We will go to free the bound and redeem the captive’. These the Holy One, blessed be He, affords no opportunity,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since they did not expressly declare their course, it would seem that their original intention was not meant to be translated into action, but was to remain with them as a pious wish.</i> and an angel comes and strikes them to the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">‘Them’ in all probability refers to the captives who come to grief before their would-be deliverers decide to act.</i>
If one man sits and occupies himself in the Torah, his reward is set aside for him on high, as it is stated, <i>Though he sit alone and meditate in stillness, yet he hath taken</i> [his reward] <i>unto himself</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Lam. 3, 28. E.V., <i>Let him sit alone and keep silence, because He hath laid it upon him</i>.</i> This may be illustrated by a parable.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">What follows is hardly a parable, but merely an example of an individual, in this case a small child, studying alone and being praised for his act.</i> To what can the matter be likened? To a man who had a young son. When the father went to market leaving his son on his own, the child arose, took a scroll, placed it on his lap, and began to study it. When the father returned from market he exclaimed, ‘See what my young son has done! When I left him and set out to market he, of his own accord, took a scroll, placed it on his lap, and began to study it!’ Hence you learn that, even if one man sits alone and occupies himself in the Torah, his reward is set aside for him on high.
<small>AND JUDGE ALL MEN FAVOURABLY</small>. It once happened that a young girl was taken captive and two pious men set out after her to ransom her. One of them entered a house of ill-fame.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Where the girl had been taken.</i> When he came out he said to his companion, ‘Of what did you suspect me?’ The other replied, ‘I thought that perhaps [you went there] to find out at what price she was held for ransom’. He said, ‘By the Temple Service! So it was; and as you judged me favourably, so may the Holy One, blessed be He, judge you favourably.’
Again it happened that a young girl was taken captive and two pious men set out after her to ransom her. One of them, however, was arrested on a [false] charge of robbery and was detained in prison, where his wife daily brought him bread and water. One day he said to her, ‘Go to my colleague and tell him that I am detained in prison [because of my efforts to save the girl] from harlotry,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The phrase is strange in its present context and is deleted by GRA.</i> while he stays at home, making merry, with no concern about the girl’. She replied, ‘Is it not enough for you that you are detained in prison that you must occupy yourself with futile matters?’ She did not go, but occupied herself with matters of no moment. He then said to her, ‘I beg of you, go and inform him’. She went and informed his colleague. Now what did that man do? He went and took with him much silver and gold as well as several men, and released the two of them. On his release he said to his companions, ‘Permit this young girl to sleep with me in bed with her clothes on’.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">His intention was to protect her from those who might take advantage of her plight.</i> [They did so.] The following morning he said to them, ‘Prepare a ritual bath for me and also for her’. They did so. He then said to those who had prepared the baths, ‘When I asked for a ritual bath for myself, of what did you suspect me?’ They replied, ‘We thought that during all the days that you were confined in prison you must have suffered hunger and thirst, and that now that you have come out into the free world your emotions were stirred and you probably suffered a pollution’. He further said to them, ‘And when I asked for a ritual bath for the young girl, of what did you suspect her?’ They replied, ‘Seeing that all those days that she had been living among heathens she was obliged to eat of their food and drink with them, now [that she is free] you asked that a ritual bath be prepared for her in order that she may become clean’. He said to them, ‘By the Temple Service! So it was; and as you judged me favourably, so may the Holy One, blessed be He, judge you favourably’.<br>Just as the righteous men of old were pious, so also were their beasts. It was said that the camels of our father Abraham would not enter a house in which there was an idol, as it is stated, <i>For I have cleared the house, and made room for the camels</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 24, 31.</i> <i>I have cleared the house</i> of the terafim; why, then, does the verse add <i>and made room for the camels?</i> It teaches that they would not enter the house of Laban the Aramean until all the idols had been cleared out before them.<br>Once the ass of R. Ḥanina b. Dosa was stolen by bandits. They locked it up in a courtyard and placed before it hay, barley and water, but it would neither eat nor drink. They said, ‘Why should we let it die here and befoul the courtyard with its stench?’ So they proceeded to open the gate for it and let it go free. It dragged itself<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Because it was enfeebled by hunger and thirst. For the verb <span dir="rtl">מושכת</span> there is a variant <span dir="rtl">משחקת</span>, ‘being merry’, but it does not make sense here. Schechter reads <span dir="rtl">מנהקת</span>, ‘braying’.</i> all the way until it reached the house of R. Ḥanina b. Dosa. When it arrived, R. Ḥanina’s son heard its cry and said to his father, ‘Father, the sound is very similar to that of our beast.’ The father said to him, ‘My son, open the door for it, since it is almost dead from hunger’. He at once opened the door for it, and put before it hay, barley and water which it ate and drank. Because of this incident the Sages declared: Just as the righteous men of old were pious, so too were their beasts.

Chapter 9

N<small>ITTAI</small><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In V. this chapter begins with the name of Joshua b. Peraḥiah followed by that of Nittai the Arbelite. This is an obvious error since the former’s aphorism was the theme of the preceding chapter.</i> <small>THE</small> A<small>RBELITE SAID: KEEP FAR FROM A BAD NEIGHBOUR, ASSOCIATE NOT WITH THE WICKED, AND ABANDON NOT BELIEF IN RETRIBUTION</small>.<br><small>KEEP FAR FROM A BAD NEIGHBOUR</small>. It is all one whether the neighbour is within or without your house or in a [nearby] field; for leprosy affects only the house of the wicked, as it is stated, <i>His own iniquities shall ensnare the wicked</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 5, 22.</i> It teaches that leprosy is caused only through the iniquity of the wicked. Indeed, the sins of the wicked cause the righteous man’s wall to be pulled down. For instance, where there is a party-wall between the house of the wicked and the righteous, and a plague-spot appears in the party-wall on the side of the wicked man’s house [so that the wall must be demolished], the result is that the righteous man’s wall is pulled down through the iniquity of the wicked. R. Ishmael, the son of R. Joḥanan b. Beroḳah, said: Woe to the wicked and woe to his neighbour, since the sins of the wicked cause the righteous man’s wall to be pulled down.
With ten trials did our fathers try the Holy One, blessed be He, yet they were only punished because of their evil tongue,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to the evil report spread by the spies; cf. Num. 13, 32.</i> which was but one of them. They are as follows:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the variant list in ‘Arak. 15a (Sonc. ed., pp. 85f) and below XXXIV, 1, p. 162.</i> once at the Red Sea,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ps. 106, 7, <i>But were rebellious at the sea, even at the Red Sea</i>.</i> once when the manna first fell<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Israelites were commanded not to leave of the manna until the morning but some disobeyed (Ex. 16, 19f).</i> and again later on,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">When they were charged not to go out and gather it on the Sabbath (<i>ibid.</i> 26f.).</i> once at the [first] appearance of the quails<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 12f.</i> and again later on,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Num. 11, 31-33.</i> once at Marah,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ex. 15, 23.</i> once at Rephidim,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVII, 1ff.</i> once at Horeb,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Another name for Sinai. The temptation at Horeb is usually taken to refer to the making of the golden calf, but as this incident follows immediately in the list, the inclusion of Horeb is redundant. GRA and other commentators substitute ‘Taberah’ (cf. Num. 11, 1-3).</i> once at the episode of the golden calf,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ex. 32, 1ff.</i>, and once in connection with the spies.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 13, 1ff.</i> The incident of the spies was the gravest of them all, as it is stated, <i>Ye have put Me to proof these ten times, and have not hearkened to My voice</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIV, 22.</i> And it is similarly [stated], <i>Even those men that did bring up an evil report of the land, died by the plague before the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 37.</i> Now is there not an inference from the less to the greater? If [for making disparaging remarks about] the Land, which can neither speak, nor blush, nor feel shame, the Holy One, blessed be He, exacted punishment from the spies for its humiliation, how much more will He exact punishment for a man’s humiliation from his fellow who speaks [evil] words against him and puts him to shame!<br>R. Simeon said: Upon those who utter slander does the plague of leprosy fall. We find it so with Aaron and Miriam who slandered Moses and were visited with punishment, as it is stated, <i>And Miriam and Aaron spoke against Moses</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XII, 1.</i> Why does Scripture mention Miriam’s name first before Aaron’s? It teaches that Zipporah<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The wife of Moses.</i> went and spoke of it to Miriam; Miriam went and spoke of it to Aaron, and both of them arose and spoke ill of that righteous man [Moses]. Because the two of them stood speaking ill of that righteous man, Divine punishment came upon them, as it is stated, <i>And the anger of the Lord was kindled against them, and departed</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 9, E.V., <i>and He departed</i>.</i> What is the significance of the expression <i>and departed?</i> It suggests that [the leprosy] departed from Aaron<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shab. 97a (Sonc. ed., p. 464).</i> and attached itself to Miriam, because Aaron did not enter into the details of the matter whereas Miriam did; therefore she was immediately punished with greater severity. Miriam said, ‘To me came the Word yet I did not separate myself from my husband’. Aaron said, ‘To me came the Word yet I did not separate myself from my wife. The Word also came to our fathers of old and they did not separate themselves from their wives. But [Moses], in his arrogance, has separated from his wife!’ They did not criticize him to his face<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid shaming him to his face.</i> but only behind his back, and their criticism was not stated as a fact but only as a surmise; since it was a matter of doubt whether he had acted so from arrogance or not. Now is there not here an inference from the less to the greater? If Miriam, who spoke only against her brother, spoke only in [22<i>a</i>] secret<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The words ‘and spoke only in secret’ are not found in many texts and appear to be redundant. The clause is apparently a doublet of the preceding one.</i> and spoke only behind Moses’ back, was punished, how much greater will be the punishment of an ordinary man who speaks ill to the face of his fellow and puts him to shame!<br>At that time Aaron said to Moses, ‘Moses, my brother, do you think that this leprosy affects only Miriam? It touches also the person of our father Amram. Let me illustrate this to you. To what can the matter be compared? To a man who had taken a glowing coal in his hand; no matter how much he shifts it about from place to place his flesh is still scorched. For so it is stated, <i>Let her not, I pray, be as one dead</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 12, 12.</i> Thereupon Aaron began to conciliate Moses, saying, ‘ Moses, my brother, have we ever done evil to any person in the world?’ He answered, ‘You have not’. ‘Well then,’ he said, ‘if we have never hurt anyone in the world, how could we hurt you, our brother? What can I do now? It was a misunderstanding between us; we have broken<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Adopting the variant as given in the margin of V.</i> the covenant between us and you, as it is stated, <i>And remembered not the brotherly covenant</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Amos 1, 9.</i> Are we to lose our sister because the covenant which was made between us is broken?’ Immediately Moses drew a small circle, stood within it and prayed for mercy on her behalf, saying, ‘I shall not stir from here until my sister Miriam is healed’, as it is stated, <i>Heal her now, O God, I beseech Thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 12, 13.</i> Forthwith the Holy One, blessed be He, said to Moses, ‘If a king had rebuked her, or if her father had rebuked her, would it not be proper that she hide in shame for seven days? Surely, if I, the King of kings, rebuke her, how much more should she hide in shame for fourteen days [at the least]! Yet for your sake I pardon her’. As it is stated, <i>And the Lord said unto Moses: If her father had but spit in her face</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14.</i> etc.<br><i>Now the man Moses was very meek</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 3.</i> It is possible [to think] he was meek but not handsome and imposing;<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘worthy of praise’.</i> therefore Scripture states, <i>And he spread the tent over the tabernacle:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 40, 19.</i> as the tabernacle was ten cubits in height, so Moses must have been ten cubits tall. It is possible [to think] that he was as meek as the ministering angels; therefore Scripture continues, [<i>Moses was very meek</i>] <i>above all the men:</i> above all men, it is said, but not above the ministering angels. It is possible [to think] that he was as meek as those of the former generations;<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the patriarchs.</i> therefore Scripture continues, <i>Upon the face of the earth</i>, implying above those of his own generation, but not above those of the former generations.<br>Three types of leprous condition were created in the world: the moist, the dry and the polypus;<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Here a type of skin disease; strictly a morbid growth in the nose. Persons so afflicted must feel very lowly.</i> but the soul of Moses was lowlier than them all.
R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: Also upon those who utter calumny<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">GRA reads ‘who utter lies’.</i> does the plague [of leprosy] fall. We find it so in the case of Gehazi; because he uttered calumny against his master leprosy clung to him until his dying day, as it is stated, <i>The leprosy, therefore, of Naaman shall cleave unto thee…. And he went out from his presence a leper as white as snow</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 5, 27.</i><br>He further said: Also upon those who are arrogant does the plague [of leprosy] fall. We find it so in the case of Uzziah, as it is stated, <i>But when he was strong, his heart was lifted up so that he did corruptly, and he trespassed against the Lord his God; for he went unto the temple of the Lord to burn incense upon the altar of incense. And Azariah the priest went in after him, and with him fourscore priests of the Lord, that were valiant men; and they withstood Uzziah the king, and said unto him: It pertaineth not unto thee, Uzziah, to burn incense unto the Lord, but to the priests the sons of Aaron that are consecrated it pertaineth to burn incense; go out of the sanctuary; for thou hast trespassed; neither shall it be for thy honour from the Lord God.
