Then Uzziah was wrath, and he had a censer in his hand to burn incense; and while he was wroth with the priests, the leprosy broke forth in his forehead</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">2 Chron. 26, 16-19.</i> At that moment the sanctuary was split from end to end,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">By a violent earthquake, alluded to in Zech. 14, 5.</i> and [the chasm covered an area of] twelve square miles. The priests fled in alarm. The king also made haste to go out <i>because the Lord had smitten him. And he was a leper unto the day of his death, and dwelt in a house set apart, being a leper; for he was cut off from the house of the Lord; and Jotham his son was over the king’s house, judging the people of the land</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">2 Chron. 26, 20f.</i>
<small>ASSOCIATE NOT WITH THE WICKED</small>. This teaches that a man should not associate himself either with an evil man or a wicked man. We find it so in the case of Jehoshaphat; because he associated with Ahab and went up with him to Ramoth Gilead, the wrath of the Lord came upon him, as it is stated, <i>Shouldest thou help the wicked, and love them that hate the Lord? For this thing wrath is upon thee from the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIX, 2. The Biblical text is omitted in V but is found in MS. E. and inserted by GRA.</i> And also later when he associated with Ahaziah, and they made themselves ships in Ezion Geber, the Lord wrecked his enterprise, as it is stated, <i>Because thou hast joined thyself with Ahaziah, the Lord hath made a breach in thy works. And the ships were broken</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XX, 37.</i> So, too, we find with Amnon who associated with Jonadab and received from him bad advice, as it is stated, <i>But Amnon had a friend, whose name was Jonadab, the son of Shimeah David’s brother; and Jonadab was a very subtle man;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 13, 3.</i> he was <i>subtle</i> to do evil.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This unusual connotation of <i>ḥakam</i>, translated <i>subtle</i>, is quoted from Sanh. 21a (Sonc. ed., p. 114).</i><br>Another interpretation of <small>ASSOCIATE NOT WITH THE WICKED</small>: even to [bring him near to] the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Although the purpose is laudable, there is always the danger of oneself becoming corrupt by the association. Cf., however, Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i>, pp. 19-22.</i>
<small>AND ABANDON NOT BELIEF IN RETRIBUTION</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man’s heart should be daily in fear of retribution<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The text therefore means: Do not consider yourself exempt from chastisement under the illusion of self-righteousness.</i> and he should say, ‘Woe is me, perhaps reckoning will overtake me to-day or to-morrow’. As a consequence he is fearful every day, as it is stated of Job, <i>For I feared a fear</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Job 3, 25. So ilt., E.V., <i>For the thing which I did fear</i>.</i>
Another interpretation of <small>AND ABANDON NOT BELIEF IN RETRIBUTION</small>. What does this mean? When a man sees that he is successful in whatever he undertakes, let him not say, ‘Because I am meritorious the All-present has provided me with food and drink in this world, while the capital [of my reward] is laid up for me in the world to come’. Rather should he say, ‘Woe is me, perhaps I have only one meritorious deed to my credit before Him, and as a reward for that He has granted me food and drink in this world, so as to cut me off utterly in the world to come’.

Chapter 10

J<small>UDAH B.</small> Ṭ<small>ABBAI AND</small> S<small>IMEON B.</small> S<small>HEṬAḤ RECEIVED THE TRADITION FROM THE PRECEDING</small>. J<small>UDAH B.</small> Ṭ<small>ABBAI SAID: ACT NOT AS THOSE THAT WOULD INFLUENCE THE JUDGES; AND WHEN THE SUITORS STAND BEFORE YOU LET THEM BE AS WRONGDOERS IN YOUR SIGHT, AND WHEN THEY HAVE DEPARTED LET THEM BE AS INNOCENT IN YOUR SIGHT, ONCE THEY HAVE ACCEPTED THE JUDGMENT</small>.
<small>ACT NOT AS THOSE THAT WOULD INFLUENCE THE JUDGES</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that if you come to the House of Study and hear a ruling or a decision, be not hasty to dispute it, but sit and ask yourself, ‘Why did they say it? On what grounds did they arrive at their decision?’ Or if they stated a law, ask them first of all the fundamental principle of the law about which they were consulted.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The text is in disorder although the meaning is clear. A warning is issued against a scholar’s hasty criticism of a judge’s decision, and unwarranted interference with a sentence of the Court.</i><br>When two suitors come before you to law, one poor and the other rich, do not say, ‘How can I declare the poor man innocent and the rich man guilty?’ or ‘How can I declare the rich man innocent and the poor man guilty?’ or ‘If I find against the poor man, he will become my enemy; and if I find in favour of the poor man, the rich man will become my enemy.’ Furthermore, do not say, ‘How dare I take the money from one and give it to the other?’ For the Torah declares, <i>Ye shall not respect persons in judgment</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 1, 17.</i><br>R. Meir used to say: What does Scripture intend by the words, <i>Ye shall hear the small and the great alike?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> It means that one suitor should not sit and the other stand; one should not speak all that he wishes and the other be told to be brief. R. Judah said: I have heard that if [the judges] desire both suitors to sit they may do so, and there is no objection to it. What is prohibited is for one to sit and the other to stand.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shebu. 30a (Sonc. ed., p. 167).</i><br>[Another interpretation:] What is meant by <i>the small and the great alike?</i> A minor law-suit should be as important to you as a major law-suit, a law-suit involving a <i>peruṭah</i> should be as important to you as one involving one hundred <i>mina</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. 8a (Sonc. ed., p. 32).</i>
He<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel passage, Men. 109b (Sonc. ed., p. 678), the author of this statement is R. Joshua b. Peraḥiah. Here it might be attributed to Judah b. Ṭabbai, the author of the aphorism at the beginning of the chapter.</i> used to say: If anyone had said to me before I occupied high office, ‘Assume that office’, I would go so far as to deprive him of his livelihood.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In Men. the reading is: ‘I would bind him and put him in front of a lion’. In both versions the threat is, of course, not meant to be taken seriously.</i> But once I had assumed office, if anyone were to say to me, ‘Give it up’, I would pour a pot of boiling water on his head. For it is a hard thing to assume power, and hard as it is to assume power, it is harder to give it up. We find it so in the case of Saul; when he was asked to assume the regal dignity he hid himself, as it is stated, <i>And the Lord answered: Behold, he hath hid himself among the baggage</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 10, 22.</i> But when he was told to surrender the crown, he pursued David to kill him.
S<small>IMEON B.</small> S<small>HEṬAḤ SAID: EXAMINE THE WITNESSES THOROUGHLY</small>; and when examining them <small>BE CAREFUL WITH YOUR WORDS</small>, lest through your words those who are listening may add falsehood to them: [a necessary precaution] on account of dishonest persons.

Chapter 11

S<small>HEMAIAH AND</small> A<small>BṬALYON RECEIVED THE TRADITION FROM THE PRECEDING</small>. S<small>HEMAIAH SAID: LOVE WORK AND HATE PUBLIC OFFICE, AND BECOME NOT KNOWN TO THE RULING POWER</small>.<br><small>LOVE WORK</small>. What does this mean? It teaches [22<i>b</i>] that a man should love work and not hate it; for just as the Torah was given [to man] by a covenant, so work too was given by a covenant, as it is stated, <i>Six days shalt thou labour and do all thy work; but the seventh day is a sabbath unto the Lord thy God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 20, 9f. This verse, however, does not contain the word ‘covenant’ required for the purpose of the analogy, and the commentators substitute Ex. 20XI, 15, <i>Six days shall work be done; but on the seventh day is a sabbath of solemn rest</i>, etc., concluding with <i>a perpetual covenant</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 16).</i><br>R. ‘Aḳiba said: There are times when a man does work [on the Sabbath] and escapes the penalty of death, and there are times when a man does no work [on the Sabbath] and incurs death by Heaven. How can this be? If a man sat the whole week doing no work and on the eve of the Sabbath, having nothing to eat, took of the sacred money which is in his possession and used it for his needs, he thereby incurred the penalty of death by Heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For misappropriating Temple funds.</i> On the other hand, if he is at work on the Temple building, although he receives sacred money in payment which he uses for his needs, he escapes the penalty of death.<br>R. Dosethai said: When do we find that if a man does no work all the six days he will eventually have to work all the seven days? If he is idle all the days of the week doing no work, when the Sabbath eve arrives he will have nothing to eat. He then goes forth and joins a band of robbers; he is caught and put in chains, and is now compelled to work [also] on the Sabbath. All this is the sequel of his not working during the six days of the week.<br>R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: Even Adam, the first man, did not taste a morsel until he had done some work, as it is stated, <i>And He put him into the garden of Eden to dress it and to keep it</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 2, 15.</i> and only then [is it recorded], <i>Of every tree of the garden thou mayest freely eat</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 16.</i><br>R. Ṭarfon said: Even the Holy One, blessed be He, did not cause His Presence to dwell in Israel until they had performed some work, as it is stated, <i>And let them make Me a sanctuary, that I may dwell among them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 25, 8.</i><br>R. Judah b. Bathyra said: What should a man do when he has no work to undertake? If he has a dilapidated courtyard or a waste field let him go and attend to it;<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Rather than be idle.</i> for it is stated, <i>Six days shalt thou labour, and do all thy work</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XX, 9.</i> Why does the verse add the words <i>and do all thy work?</i> To include the case of a man who has dilapidated courtyards or waste fields, that he should go and occupy himself with them.<br>R. Jose<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">So in all MSS.; V reads: R. Ṭarfon.</i> said: No man dies except in idleness, as it is stated, <i>And he expired, and was gathered unto his people</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 49, 33. The term <i>he expired</i> is interpreted as ‘he ceased to work’, and when idleness set in death follows. Schechter suggests that the proof-text should read, <i>And they shall die without knowledge</i> (Job 36, 12) which follows immediately on <i>If they hearken and work</i> [E.V., <i>serve Him</i>], <i>they shall spend their days in prosperity</i> (<i>ibid.</i> 11). This is Scriptural proof that work leads to prosperity, idleness to death.</i> Hence if a man had a seizure, came under the care of his physician and died, he only died because he was in idleness.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. through inability to work.</i> If a man was standing [idly] on the top of a roof or a castle or a building or by the river’s edge and fell and died, he only died because he was in idleness.<br>We have heard this<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The duty to work.</i> in connection with men, but whence in connection with women? As it is stated, <i>Let neither man nor woman make any more work for the offering of the sanctuary</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 36, 6.</i> And whence in connection with children? As it is stated, <i>So the people were restrained from bringing</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i>; the term <i>people</i> includes the young.</i><br>R. Nathan said: At the time that Moses was engaged upon the work of the Tabernacle, he declined to consult with the princes of Israel. Consequently they sat in silence, thinking to themselves, ‘The time will soon come when Moses will have need of us’. When they heard the proclamation made in the camp which declared, <i>The stuff they had was sufficient</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 7.</i> they exclaimed, ‘Alas, we have had no share in the work of the Tabernacle!’ They arose and added a large contribution of their own accord, as it is stated, <i>And the rulers brought the onyx stones</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXV, 27.</i>
<small>AND HATE PUBLIC OFFICE</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that no man should himself put a crown on his head, but others should put it on him; as it is stated, <i>Let another man praise thee and not thine own mouth; a stranger, and not thine own lips</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 27, 2.</i><br>R. ‘Aḳiba said: Whoever exalts himself by his knowledge of Torah, to what is he like? To a carcase which lies in the road, anybody passing by puts his hand to his nose and moves far away from it; as it is stated, <i>If thou hast done foolishly in lifting up thyself, or if thou hast planned devices, lay thy hand upon thy mouth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXX, 32.</i> Ben ‘Azzai said to him, ‘Interpret the text in this sense: If a man debases himself for the sake of Torah, eats dry dates, wears shabby clothes, and sits and keeps guard at the door of the wise, though every passer-by may think him a fool, in the end you will find that the whole Torah is within him’.<br>R. Jose said: Go down and you will be up,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A fine pithy saying. The more humble a man is, the higher he stands in the estimation of his fellows.</i> go up and you will be down. He who exalts himself by his knowledge of Torah will in the end be brought low; he who humbles himself by his knowledge of Torah will in the end be exalted.
<small>AND BECOME NOT KNOWN TO THE RULING POWER</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should not wish to have a reputation [for wealth] with the ruling powers,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The harsh sentiments expressed here refer particularly to the cruel and despotic rule of the Roman local governors in the Herodian era.</i> because if this happens they will eventually have their eyes upon him, put him to death and confiscate all his wealth. [Similarly, a man should not cause a reputation for wealth to be spread abroad about his neighbour among the ruling powers.]<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA. This is the maxim for the illustration that follows.</i> Thus if a man meets his friend in the market and exclaims, ‘May the Holy One, blessed be He, bless that man [still more]! Only to-day there came from his establishment a hundred oxen, a hundred sheep and a hundred goats!’, an inspector may hear it and proceed to inform the governor, who has the man’s house surrounded and confiscates all his wealth. Of such a man Scripture says, <i>He that blesseth his friend with a loud voice … it is counted a curse to him</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXVII, 14. E.V., <i>it shall be counted</i>.</i><br>Another interpretation of <small>AND BECOME NOT KNOWN TO THE RULING POWER</small>. A man’s friend is sitting in the market and the former exclaims, ‘May the Holy One, blessed be He, bestow still more blessings upon him! To-day he brought into his house so many <i>kors</i> of wheat and so many <i>kors</i> of barley!’ Robbers hear of it and come and surround his house, taking away all his wealth, so that in the morning he is left with nothing. Of such a man Scripture says, <i>He that blesseth his friend with a loud voice</i>, etc.<br>Another interpretation of <small>AND BECOME NOT KNOWN TO THE RULING POWER</small>. What does it mean? It teaches that a man should not intend [to occupy office] in order to say, ‘I am the city’s governor’, or ‘I am the deputy-governor’, because they rob Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A curious passage and its meaning is obscure. Perhaps the explanation is to be sought in Midrash Rabbah, Genesis 76, §6 (Sonc. ed., p. 706), which tells that the Romans used to offer petty local governorships to wealthy Jews in order to hold them responsible for the collection of the taxes and other impositions. As in many cases the taxes were unjustly levied, they who occupied these offices were assisting in robbing their own brethren.</i>
Another interpretation: A man should not have the intention [to occupy] a governmental position, for though in the beginning they show him favour, ultimately they trouble him sorely.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘they open to him a door that is pleasant … a door that is harsh’.</i>
A<small>BṬALYON SAID: WISE MEN, BE HEEDFUL OF YOUR WORDS</small>, lest men teach doctrines in your name not in accord with the Torah, and <small>YOU THEREBY INCUR THE PENALTY OF EXILE, AND ARE EXILED TO A PLACE OF EVIL WATERS</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. exiled among the nations where pernicious and heretical ideas are current.</i> Moreover, the disciples who come after you may also teach doctrines in your name not in accord with the Torah, and they too incur the penalty of exile and are exiled to a place of evil waters.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">V repeats ‘and you thereby incur’, etc.</i> What is to be understood by ‘evil waters’? That which is implied in the verse, <i>But they mingled themselves with the nations, and learned their works</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 106, 35. ‘Evil waters’ has to be interpreted metaphorically.</i> Others say: The phrase ‘evil waters’ bears its literal significance.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Unhealthy waters, productive of physical ills.</i> Another view is: [The meaning is] lest they be exiled for hard labour.

Chapter 12

H<small>ILLEL AND</small> S<small>HAMMAI RECEIVED THE TRADITION FROM THE PRECEDING</small>. H<small>ILLEL SAID: BE OF THE DISCIPLES OF</small> A<small>ARON, LOVING PEACE AND PURSUING PEACE</small>, promoting peace between man and wife, <small>LOVING MANKIND AND BRINGING THEM NEAR TO THE TORAH</small>.<br><small>HE USED TO SAY: WHOEVER MAKES HIS NAME GREAT LOSES HIS NAME; WHOSOEVER INCREASES NOT HIS KNOWLEDGE DECREASES IT; HE WHO DOES NOT STUDY DESERVES DEATH; AND HE WHO MAKES USE OF THE CROWN</small><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Usually explained: he who makes worldly use of the crown of the Torah; but cf. the end of this chapter.</i> <small>WILL PASS AWAY</small>.
<small>HE FURTHER USED TO SAY: IF I AM NOT FOR MYSELF WHO IS FOR ME? AND IF I AM ONLY FOR MYSELF, WHAT AM I? AND IF NOT NOW, WHEN?</small>
<small>LOVING PEACE</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should love [to foster] peace in Israel between man and man, just as Aaron loved [to foster] peace in Israel between man and man, as it is stated, <i>The law of truth was in his mouth, and unrighteousness was not found in his lips; he walked with Me in peace and uprightness, and did turn many away from iniquity</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Mal. 2, 6.</i> R. Meir said: What is meant by <i>and did turn many away from iniquity?</i> Whenever Aaron walked along the road and met a wicked man, he would offer him greetings. On the following day when that man was about to commit a transgression, he would say, ‘Woe is me! How can I raise my eyes after doing that and look Aaron in the face? I should be ashamed before him, since he greeted me’. Consequently that man withholds himself from transgression.<br>Similarly, when two men had quarrelled one with the other, Aaron would go and sit with one of them and say, ‘My son, see what your companion is doing!<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">So according to GRA; V reads ‘saying’.</i> He beats his breast and tears his clothes, exclaiming, “Woe is me! how can I raise my eyes and look my companion in the face? I am ashamed before him since it is I who offended him” ’. Aaron would sit with him until he had removed all enmity from his heart. Then Aaron would go and sit with the other and say likewise, ‘My son, see what your companion is doing! He beats his breast and tears his clothes, exclaiming, “Woe is me! how can I raise my eyes and look my companion in the face? I am ashamed before him since it was I who offended him” ’. Aaron would sit with him until he had removed all enmity from his heart. Later when the two met, they embraced and kissed each other. Therefore it is stated, <i>They wept for Aaron thirty days, even all the house of Israel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 20, 29.</i>
Another interpretation—why was it that for Aaron <i>all the house of Israel</i> wept thirty days, i.e. the men as well as the women? Because Aaron judged everyone favourably,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The usual and literal connotation of the phrase is: ‘he judged a judgment of truth according to its truth’, i.e. he dealt out justice strictly and in all its severity. This quality, however, is inapplicable to Aaron in this context where he is described as overlooking faults. In the parallel passage in the Yalḳuṭ on the verse in Num. <i>ibid.</i>, this phrase is descriptive of Moses and not of Aaron; consequently the commentators have resorted to emendation here in order to make the passages correspond (cf. GRA and the Glosses of J. Bachrach). The translation given in the text retains the original reading and is to be interpreted: he regarded every matter in the light of truth, i.e. he considered every person’s action as right (he ‘gave him right’), or at least endeavoured to see justification in every person’s action, taking a favourable view of it. Cf. the expression in Aboth 1:6, ‘Judge all men in the scale of merit’.</i> for he never said to any man or any woman, ‘You have done wrong’. Therefore it is written of him that <i>all the house of Israel</i> wept for him. Of Moses, on the other hand, who rebuked the people with harsh words, it is stated, <i>And all the children of Israel wept for Moses</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 34, 8.</i> Moreover, many thousands in Israel were given the name Aaron, since but for him they would not have come into the world, because he was the peacemaker between husband and wife. When [after being reconciled] they were united, they would name the child who was born after him. [23<i>a</i>]<br>Others say that for this reason it is stated, <i>And they wept for Aaron thirty days, even all the house of Israel</i>—because who could look upon Moses our teacher sitting and weeping and himself not weep? Others express it in this way: Who could look upon Eleazar and Phinehas,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The son and grandson of Aaron respectively.</i> the two High Priests, standing and weeping and himself not weep?<br>
At that time Moses wished to die in the same manner as Aaron had died; because he saw Aaron’s couch arrayed in great splendour, and hosts of ministering angels lamenting him. Did Moses express this desire in the hearing of another person? No, he only expressed the wish to himself and the Holy One, blessed be He, heard his whisper [and granted his wish,] as it is stated, <i>And die in the mount whither thou goest up, and be gathered unto thy people; as Aaron thy brother died in mount Hor</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 32, 50.</i> From this you may infer that Moses wished to die the same death as Aaron.<br>At that hour [when Moses was to die], He said to the angel of death, ‘Go and bring Me the soul of Moses’. The angel of death went and stood before him and said, ‘Moses, deliver your soul to me’. Moses rebuked him saying, ‘In the place where I am seated you have not the right to stand,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For Moses was engaged in the study of the Torah, and the angel of death cannot approach one who is so engaged; cf. Shab. 30b (Sonc. ed., p. 134).</i> yet you say to me, “Deliver your soul to me” ’. He rebuked him and drove him away in anger. At length the Holy One, blessed be He, [intervened and] said to Moses, ‘Moses, you have had enough of this world; the world to come awaits you since the six days of creation’, as it is stated, <i>And the Lord said: Behold there is a place by Me, and thou shalt stand upon the rock</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 33, 21.</i> The Holy One, blessed be He, took the soul of Moses and stored it under the Throne of Glory, as it is stated, <i>The soul of my lord shall be bound in the bundle of life with the Lord thy God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 25, 29.</i> When He took his soul it was with a kiss, as it is stated, <i>By the mouth of the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 34, 5. So lit.; E.V., <i>according to the word of the Lord</i>.</i><br>Not only the soul of Moses is stored under the Throne of Glory but the souls of the righteous are also stored there, as it is stated, <i>The soul of my lord shall be bound in the bundle of life with the Lord thy God</i>. It is possible [to think that] it is the same with the souls of the wicked; therefore Scripture declares, <i>And the souls of thine enemies, them shall He sling out as from the hollow of a sling</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. <i>ibid.</i></i>. To what may this be compared? To a man who took a stone and placed it in a sling; though he hurls it from place to place he knows not where it will come to rest. Similarly, the souls of the wicked are held fast [as in a vice],<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘muzzled’; cf. Shab. 152b (Sonc. ed., p. 779) and Rashi’s comment <i>ad loc.</i></i> and driven about aimlessly in the world, knowing not whereon to rest.<br>Again said He to the angel of death, ‘Go and bring Me the soul of Moses’. He went and searched for him in his usual place but could not find him. He went to the Great Sea and asked, ‘Has Moses been here?’ The Sea replied, ‘Since the day Israel passed through me I have not seen him’. He went to the mountains and hills and asked, ‘Has Moses been here?’ They replied, ‘Since the day Israel received the Torah on Mount Sinai we have not seen him’. He then went to Sheol and Abaddon<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘[place of] Destruction’; used in the Bible as a term for Sheol (Job 26, 6) death (<i>ibid.</i> XXVIII, 22) and the grave (Ps. 88, 12).</i> and asked, ‘Has Moses been here?’ They replied, ‘We have heard his name but have not seen him’. He went to the ministering angels and asked, ‘Has Moses been here?’ They replied, ‘<i>God understandeth his way, and He knoweth his place</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Job 28, 23, E.V., <i>the way thereof … the place thereof</i>.</i> God has hidden him away for life in the world to come, and no creature knows his whereabouts’, as it is stated, <i>But wisdom, where shall it be found? And where is the place of understanding? Man knoweth not the price thereof; neither is it found in the land of the living. The deep saith: It is not in me; and the sea saith: It is not with me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 12-14. The passage is related to Moses who possessed the wisdom of the Torah.</i> <i>Destruction and Death say: We have heard a rumour thereof with our ears</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 22.</i> Joshua also was sitting and grieving over Moses, because he did not know where he was, until the Holy One, blessed be He, said to him, ‘Joshua, why do you grieve for Moses? <i>Moses My servant is dead</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Josh. 1, 2.</i>
<small>PURSUING PEACE</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should pursue peace in Israel among all men in the same way that Aaron pursued peace in Israel among all men; as it is stated, <i>Depart from evil, and do good; seek peace, and pursue it</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 34, 15.</i> R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: If a man stays at home in quietude, how can he pursue peace in Israel among all men? He should rather leave his home and go about the world so that he can pursue peace in Israel, as it is stated, <i>Seek peace, and pursue it</i>: meaning <i>seek peace</i> in your own place, <i>and pursue it</i> by going elsewhere.<br>Even the Holy One, blessed be He, made peace on high.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Job 25, 2.</i> What manner of peace did He make on high? By not naming ten [angels] ‘Gabriel’, ten ‘Michael’, ten ‘Uriel’, or ten ‘Raphael’, after the manner of men who name ten [persons] ‘Reuben’, ten ‘Simeon’, ten ‘Levi’, or ten ‘Judah’. Had He done as men do, on summoning one [angel, all bearing that name] would come before Him and they would be envious of one another. He therefore named one [angel] ‘Gabriel’ and one ‘Michael’, so that when He summons an angel [by name] only one appears before Him and is sent wherever He wishes. And whence do we know that [the angels] reverence one another, respect one another, and are more humble than human beings? When they are about to open their mouths to sing hymns, one says to the other, ‘You begin, for you are greater than I’; and the other replies, ‘No, you begin, for you are greater than I’. This is quite unlike the behaviour of human beings, everyone saying to his fellow, ‘I am greater than you’. According to another view [the courteous remarks are made by] groups of angels, one group saying to the other, ‘You begin, for you are greater than we’; as it is stated, <i>And one called unto another, and said</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 6, 3 where the reference is to groups of seraphim.</i>
<small>LOVING MANKIND</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should love his fellow-creatures and not hate them; for so we find it among the men of the generation of the Dispersion. Since they loved one another,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This is inferred from the statement that they were <i>of one language and of one speech</i> (Gen. 11, 1).</i> the Holy One, blessed be He, did not wish to destroy them, but dispersed them to the four corners of the earth.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 8.</i> Because the men of Sodom, on the other hand, hated one another, the Holy One, blessed be He, destroyed them from this world and the world to come; as it is stated, <i>The men of Sodom were wicked and sinners against the Lord exceedingly</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIII, 13.</i> [<i>Wicked</i> signifies that they hated one another;]<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA; it is also found in all the parallel passages.</i> <i>sinners</i>, that they offended against morality; <i>against the Lord</i>, that they profaned the Name; <i>exceedingly</i>, that they sinned with premeditation. Hence you learn that because they hated one another, the Holy One, blessed be He, destroyed them from this world and from the world to come.
<small>AND BRINGING THEM NEAR TO THE TORAH</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should prevail upon his fellows and bring them beneath the wings of the Divine Presence, as our father Abraham used to prevail upon men and bring them beneath the wings of the Divine Presence. Not only Abraham did this, but also Sarah, as it is stated, <i>And Abram took Sarai his wife, and Lot his brother’s son, and all their substance that they had gathered, and the souls that they had made in Haran</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XII, 5; so lit.; E.V., <i>had gotten in Haran</i>. [Midrash Rabbah, Genesis, XXXIX, 14 (Sonc. ed., p. 324) explains it of the proselytes made by Abraham and Sarah.]</i> Is it not a fact that all mankind cannot create even a gnat? How, then, can Scripture declare, <i>And the souls that they had</i> made <i>in Haran!</i> It teaches that the Holy One, blessed be He, accounted it to them as though they had created them.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is clearly out of place here; it is inserted by GRA after the first sentence in the paragraph which follows.</i> Just as a man does not share his fellow’s reward in this world, so a man does not share his fellow’s reward in the world to come; as it is stated, <i>And behold the tears of such as were oppressed, and they had no comforter; and on the side of their oppressors there was power, but they had no comforter</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 4, 1.</i> Why does the verse repeat <i>they had no comforter?</i> It speaks of those men who in this world eat and drink and are fortunate in their sons and daughters, but in the world to come have nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Because they did not live a good life but a life of self-centred pleasure.</i> and are without a comforter. In this world, if a man has been robbed or suffered a bereavement, his sons, brothers and kinsmen come to comfort him. It is possible [to imagine] that it is the same in the world to come; therefore Scripture declares, <i>He hath neither son nor brother</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 8.</i> Similarly, if a man sins and begets an illegitimate child, they say to him, ‘Fool that you are! You have harmed yourself and also the child’. For that illegitimate son may be desirous of studying Torah together with the scholars who sit and study in Jerusalem. He joins their company until they reach Ashdod; there he halts and cries, ‘Woe is me! Had I not been of illegitimate birth, I should now be studying with those who were hitherto my fellow-students; but being illegitimate I may not sit and study with them because an illegitimate is not allowed to enter Jerusalem; as it is stated, <i>And a bastard shall dwell in Ashdod, and I will cut off the pride of the Philistines</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Zech. 9, 6.</i>
<small>HE USED TO SAY: IF I AM NOT FOR MYSELF, WHO IS FOR ME</small>? If I do not win merit for myself in my lifetime, who will do so for me?<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">According to GRA the preceding paragraph should follow here.</i> <small>AND IF I AM ONLY FOR MYSELF, WHAT AM I</small>? If I have won merit for myself alone, what have I achieved?<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">V reads ‘If I do not win merit for myself, who will do so for me?’ This cannot be correct because it merely repeats the preceding sentence. The translation in the text is based on a suggestion by Schechter <i>ad loc.</i></i> <small>AND IF NOT NOW, WHEN</small>? If I do not win merit for myself in my lifetime, who will do so for me after my death? As Scripture declares, <i>For a living dog is better than a dead lion</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 9, 4.</i> <i>A living dog is better</i> refers to a wicked man while he exists in this world;<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Because as long as he is alive there is hope that he may repent.</i> <i>than a dead lion</i> refers to the patriarchs Abraham, Isaac and Jacob who sleep in the dust.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">And all their achievements have come to an end.</i><br>Another interpretation: <i>A living dog is better</i> refers to a wicked man while he exists in this world, because if he repents the Holy One, blessed be He, receives him; whereas a righteous man, once dead, cannot add to his merits.
He used to say: If you will come to my house I will go to yours. Wheresoever my heart desires [to go] there my feet lead me.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted as a saying of Hillel in Suk. 53a (Sonc. ed., p. 253).</i><br>‘If you will come to my house I will go to yours.’ What does this mean? It refers to the persons who are found in the Synagogues and Houses of Study early in the morning and late at night. The Holy One, blessed be He, will bless them in the world to come, in accordance with what is stated, <i>In every place where I cause My name to be mentioned I will come unto thee and bless thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 20, 21.</i><br>‘Wheresoever my heart desires [to go] there my feet lead me.’ What does this mean? It refers to the persons who leave their silver and gold behind and go up [to Jerusalem] for the Festival to appear before the Divine Presence in the Temple. The Holy One, blessed be He, stands on guard for them in the midst of their homesteads, as it is stated, <i>Neither shall any man covet thy land, when thou goest up to appear before the Lord thy God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXIV, 24.</i>
He used to say: If I am here, all are here; if I am not here, who is here?<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A continuation of the passage quoted from Suk. 53a.</i> Turn it<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah.</i> and turn it again, for everything [23<i>b</i>] is in it. And with regard to all things [the rule applies]: according to the labour so is the reward.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The last two maxims are quoted from Aboth 5 (end) in the names of Ben Bag Bag and Ben He He respectively, who were reputed to have been Hillel’s disciples. [In Ḳid. 10b (Sonc. ed., p. 40) the full name appears as Joḥanan b. Bag Bag. Some scholars interpret ‘Bag’ as an abbreviation of ‘Ben Ger’ (son of a proselyte) and conjecture that ‘Bag’ and ‘He’ are identical, the numerical value of BG, being five, indicated by the letter <i>he</i>.]</i>
The story is told of Hillel the Elder that he was walking along the road when he met some men carrying wheat. He asked them, ‘How much a <i>se’ah?</i>’ They replied, ‘Two <i>dinars</i>’. Later he met other men [carrying wheat] and he asked them, ‘How much a <i>se’ah?</i>’ They replied, ‘Three <i>dinars</i>.’ He said to them, ‘But those who were in front of you only asked two <i>dinars</i>’. They rejoined, ‘You stupid Babylonian, do you not know the rule: according to the labour so is the reward?’<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the latter had brought the wheat from a longer distance their charge was higher.</i> He said to them, ‘You foolish and empty-headed persons! Because I ask you [a civil question], do you answer me [rudely]?’ Nevertheless Hillel the Elder so affected them that he turned them on to the good path.<br><small>MOREOVER, HE SAW A SKULL FLOATING ON THE SURFACE OF THE WATER; HE SAID TO IT, ‘BECAUSE YOU DROWNED OTHERS THEY DROWNED YOU, AND THEY THAT DROWNED YOU WILL THEMSELVES BE DROWNED</small>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted from a maxim of Hillel in Aboth 2:7 (Sonc. ed., II, 6, p. 16). It is in Aramaic, not Hebrew.</i>
He also said the following four [aphorisms] in Aramaic: <small>WHOSOEVER MAKES GREAT HIS NAME LOSES HIS NAME; HE WHO DOES NOT WAIT UPON SCHOLARS DESERVES DEATH; HE WHO DOES NOT INCREASE HIS KNOWLEDGE LOSES WHAT HE HAS; AND HE WHO MAKES USE OF THE CROWN WILL PASS AWAY AND PERISH</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A quotation from Aboth 1:13, with variants, the most important being in the third saying which reads in the Mishnaic version, ‘he who does not study deserves death’. [The version given here, ‘he who does not wait’, etc., occurs in Nazir VII, 1, 56b, where it is attributed to R. ‘Aḳiba.]</i><br><small>WHOEVER MAKES GREAT HIS NAME LOSES HIS NAME</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should not spread abroad of himself a reputation [for wealth] in the kingdom, because if he does so, before long the authorities will cast their eyes upon him, kill him, and confiscate his wealth.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. XI, 3, above, p. 68.</i><br><small>HE WHO DOES NOT WAIT UPON SCHOLARS DESERVES DEATH</small>. What does it mean? The story is told of a certain man of Beth-Ramah<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The modern Beit Rima, about 20 miles north-west of Jerusalem.</i> who used to conduct himself in the strictest piety, that Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai once sent a disciple to him to test him in his conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as stated later, the man was the High Priest, who generally belonged to the Sadducean party, Rabban Joḥanan investigated a report that he followed Pharasaic teaching and practice (cf. Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i>, pp. 152f).</i> When he arrived, he found that the man took some oil and set it on the oven-range [to be warmed], then he removed it from the range and poured it into a dish of crushed beans. On being asked what he was doing, the man replied, ‘I am a High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Not necessarily the High Priest in office at the time, but who had served at one time in that office and had therefore retained the title.</i> and I always eat <i>terumah</i> in conditions of cleanness’. ‘Is this oven-range clean or unclean?’ [the disciple] asked him, and he answered, ‘Is it ever mentioned in the Torah about an oven-range being unclean? Surely the Torah speaks only of a common oven as being unclean; as it is stated, <i>Whatsoever is in it shall be unclean</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 11, 33, where the reference is to an <i>earthen vessel</i>.</i> The other retorted, ‘Just as the Torah speaks of a common oven as being unclean, so it likewise speaks of an oven-range as being unclean; for it is stated, <i>Whether oven, or range for pots, it shall be broken in pieces; they are unclean</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 35.</i> He added, ‘If that is what you were accustomed to do, you have never eaten clean <i>terumah</i> in your life’.<br><small>HE WHO DOES NOT INCREASE HIS KNOWLEDGE LOSES WHAT HE HAS</small>. What does it mean? It teaches that if a man has studied one tractate, or even two or three tractates, and does not add anything thereto, eventually he will forget what he learnt in the beginning.<br><small>AND HE WHO MAKES USE OF THE CROWN WILL PASS AWAY AND PERISH</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that he who makes use of the Expressed Name<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Generally identified with the Tetragrammaton, and synonymous with the ‘Unique Name’. It was commonly held that by pronouncing the Name of God as it is written, one could work miracles, and Hillel’s saying is interpreted as a warning against the practice.</i> has no portion in the world to come.

Chapter 13

S<small>HAMMAI SAID: MAKE YOUR STUDY OF THE TORAH A FIXED HABIT; SAY LITTLE AND DO MUCH; AND RECEIVE ALL MEN WITH A CHEERFUL COUNTENANCE</small>.
<small>MAKE YOUR STUDY OF THE TORAH A FIXED HABIT</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that if a man hears a theme from a Sage in the House of Study, he should not treat it as something casual, but should regard it as a fixed norm. Moreover, what a man learns he should perform and teach to others that they, too, may perform it; as it is stated, <i>That ye may learn them, and observe to do them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 5, 1.</i> It similarly states of Ezra, <i>For Ezra had set his heart to seek the law of the Lord, and to do it</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Ezra 7, 10.</i> the verse continuing, <i>And to teach in Israel statutes and ordinances</i>.
<small>SAY LITTLE AND DO MUCH</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that the righteous say little and do much, whereas the wicked say much and do not even a little. Whence do we know that the righteous say little and do much? We find it so with Abraham our father who said to the angels, ‘You will eat with me to-day a morsel of bread’; as it is stated, <i>And I will fetch a morsel of bread, and stay ye your heart</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 18, 5.</i> But in the end, see how much Abraham prepared for the ministering angels—he went and prepared for them three oxen and nine measures of fine flour! Whence do we know that he prepared for them nine measures of fine flour? For it is stated, <i>And Abraham hastened into the tent unto Sarah, and said: Make ready quickly three measures of fine meal</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 6.</i> Now the word <i>three</i> signifies literally what it says; <i>fine</i> also implies three making six; and <i>meal</i> implies a further three, making nine in all. And whence do we know that he prepared for them three oxen? For it is stated, <i>And Abraham ran unto the herd and fetched a calf tender</i> [<i>and good</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 7.</i> Now the word <i>herd</i> implies one, <i>calf</i> implies another making two, and <i>tender</i> implies a third, thus making three in all. Some say that the word <i>good</i> implies a fourth. <i>And he gave them unto the servant, and he hastened to dress them:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> E.V., <i>gave it … dress it</i>.</i> he gave them to his son Ishmael in order to train him in the performance of good deeds.<br>The Holy One, blessed be He, likewise said little and did much; as it is stated, <i>And He said unto Abram: Know of a surety that thy seed shall be a stranger in a land that is not theirs, and shall serve them; and they shall afflict them four hundred years; and also that nation whom they shall serve, will I judge; and afterward shall they come out with great substance</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XV, 13f. The Heb. for <i>judge</i> is <i>dan</i>, a word of only two letters.</i> [In His announcement] He only used a word of two letters, <i>daleth</i> and <i>nun</i>; eventually when the Holy One, blessed be He, inflicted punishment upon the enemies of Israel, He did so with seventy-two letters; as it is stated, <i>Or hath God assayed to go and take Him a nation from the midst of another nation, by trials, by signs, and by wonders, and by war, and by a mighty hand, and by an out-stretched arm, and by great terrors?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 4, 34. There are 75 letters in the Heb. text quoted, beginning with <i>to go</i>. The Midrash Rabbah, Leviticus, XXIII, §2 (Sonc. ed., p. 293) and Song of Songs 2, §2 (Sonc. ed., p. 95), suggests that the second <i>nation</i>, a word of three letters in Heb. (<i>goï</i>), should be omitted from the enumeration. The virtue of the number 72 lies in the fact that one of the sacred Names consists of 72 letters.</i> Hence you learn that when He inflicted punishment upon Israel’s enemies He only did so with seventy-two letters.<br>And whence do we know that the wicked say much and do not even a little? We find it so with Ephron who said to Abraham, <i>A piece of land worth four hundred shekels of silver</i>, [<i>what is that betwixt me and thee</i>]<i>?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 23, 15. This verse suggests that the land is of so little value that it is unworthy of their further discussion.</i> Eventually when Abraham weighed out the silver for him, it is stated, <i>And Abraham hearkened unto Ephron; and Abraham weighed to Ephron the silver …</i> [<i>current money with the merchant</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 16. Ephron insisted upon the fullest value of the money although at first he regarded the transaction as of little significance.</i>
<small>AND RECEIVE ALL MEN WITH A CHEERFUL COUNTENANCE</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that if a man presents the most precious gifts in the world to his fellow but gives them with a sullen countenance, Scripture regards him as having presented him with nothing. On the other hand, he who receives his fellow with a cheerful countenance, though he give him nothing, Scripture accounts it to him as though he had presented him with the most precious gifts in the world.

Chapter 14

R<small>ABBAN</small> J<small>OḤANAN B.</small> Z<small>AKKAI RECEIVED THE TRADITION FROM</small> H<small>ILLEL AND</small> S<small>HAMMAI</small>.<br>Hillel the Elder had eighty disciples:<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the parallel passage in Suk. 28a (Sonc. ed., p. 123) and B.B. 134a (Sonc. ed., p. 563).</i> thirty of them were worthy that the Divine Presence should rest upon them as upon Moses our teacher, but their generation was not worthy of it; thirty of them were worthy that the intercalation of the years<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The calculations were made only by the leading members of the Sanhedrin.</i> should be determined by them; and twenty were average. The greatest of them all was Jonathan b. ’Uzziel,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">[Meg. 3a (Sonc. ed., p. 9) names him as the author of an Aramaic translation of the Prophets. The extant Targum is not by him.]</i> and the least of them all was Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai. It was said of Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai that he mastered<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘he had not left (unstudied)’.</i> Scripture, Mishnah, Gemara, <i>halakoth, ’aggadoth, toseftoth</i>, the minutiae<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The subtle points of exposition.</i> of the Torah, the minutiae of the Scribes, and all the hermeneutical rules of the Sages. Not a word in the Torah did he not master, fulfilling the text, <i>That I may cause those that love me to inherit substance, and that I may fill their treasuries</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 8, 21.</i>
<small>HE USED TO SAY: IF YOU HAVE OBSERVED</small><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In Aboth 2:9 (Sonc. ed., II, 8, p. 17) ‘learnt’.</i> <small>MUCH TORAH CLAIM NO MERIT FOR YOURSELF, FOR TO THIS END YOU WERE CREATED</small>; inasmuch as human beings were created solely for the purpose of occupying themselves with the Torah.
Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai had five disciples whom he described in these terms: Eliezer b. Hyrḳanos as ‘a cemented cistern that loses not a drop’ and ‘a glazed pitcher that preserves its wine’; Joshua b. Ḥananiah as ‘<i>a threefold cord that is not quickly broken</i>’;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 4, 12. This is no doubt intended as alluding to three virtues of Joshua b. Ḥananiah, but they are not specified.</i> Jose [24<i>a</i>] the priest as ‘the saint of the generation’; Ishmael b. Ḥananiah<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In conformity with Aboth 2:10 (Sonc. ed. II, 8, p. 18) GRA emends to ‘Simeon b. Nathaniel’. This paragraph corresponds to Aboth 2:10f, but with several variants.</i> as ‘an oasis<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A doubtful word; some emend it to <i>‘arugah</i>, ‘a garden bed’ or ‘grove’, a term applied to a scholar in DEZ I, p. 567 below.</i> in the desert which retains its waters’. Happy the disciple whose teacher acknowledges and describes him [in such terms]! Eleazar b. ‘Arak he described as ‘a swift-running stream and a vigorous fountain whose waters increase and overflow abroad’, thus fulfilling the verse, <i>Let thy springs be dispersed abroad, and courses of water in the streets</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 5, 16.</i>
<small>HE USED TO SAY: IF ALL THE SAGES OF</small> I<small>SRAEL WERE IN ONE SCALE OF THE BALANCE AND</small> R. E<small>LIEZER B.</small> H<small>YRḲANOS IN THE OTHER, HE WOULD OUTWEIGH THEM ALL</small>. A<small>BBA</small> S<small>AUL SAID IN HIS NAME: IF ALL THE SAGES OF</small> I<small>SRAEL WERE IN ONE SCALE OF THE BALANCE, INCLUDING</small> R. E<small>LIEZER B.</small> H<small>YRḲANOS, AND</small> R. E<small>LEAZAR B.</small> ‘A<small>RAK WAS IN THE OTHER, HE WOULD OUTWEIGH THEM ALL</small>.
<small>HE SAID TO THEM: GO FORTH AND SEE WHICH IS THE GOOD WAY TO WHICH A MAN SHOULD CLEAVE SO</small> that thereby he may enter the world to come. R. E<small>LIEZER</small> came forward, and <small>SAID: A GOOD EYE</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Denoting generosity, freedom from envy which is termed ‘an evil eye’.</i> R. J<small>OSHUA</small> came forward and <small>SAID: A GOOD FRIEND</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Indicating the quality of friendliness.</i> R. J<small>OSE</small> came forward and <small>SAID: A GOOD NEIGHBOUR</small>,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Implies sympathy and goodwill. ‘A good impulse’, etc., is omitted in Aboth 2:13 (Sonc. ed., II, 9, p. 19).</i> a good impulse and a good wife. R. S<small>IMEON SAID: ONE WHO FORESEES THE CONSEQUENCES OF AN ACTION</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The virtue of foresight and prudence. V inserts in brackets, ‘As, for instance, Mordecai the Jew who foresaw the consequences of an action’.</i> R. E<small>LEAZAR</small> came forward and <small>SAID: A HEART THAT IS GOOD</small><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. unselfishness.</i> towards Heaven and is good towards [all] creatures. <small>THEREUPON HE SAID TO THEM: I PREFER THE WORDS OF</small> R. E<small>LEAZAR B.</small> ‘A<small>RAK TO YOUR WORDS, BECAUSE IN HIS WORDS YOURS ARE INCLUDED</small>.<br><small>HE THEN SAID TO THEM: GO FORTH AND SEE WHICH IS THE EVIL WAY THAT A MAN SHOULD SHUN SO</small> that he may enter the world to come. R. E<small>LIEZER</small> came forward and <small>SAID: AN EVIL EYE</small>. R. J<small>OSHUA</small> came forward and <small>SAID: AN EVIL FRIEND</small>. R. J<small>OSE</small> came forward and <small>SAID: AN EVIL NEIGHBOUR</small>, an evil impulse and a bad wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">So according to GRA, giving the reverse of R. Jose’s previous statement. V reads, ‘An evil eye, an evil neighbour, and an evil wife’.</i> R. S<small>IMEON</small> came forward and <small>SAID: ONE WHO BORROWS AND DOES NOT REPAY, SINCE HE WHO BORROWS FROM MAN IS AS ONE WHO BORROWS FROM THE</small> A<small>LL-PRESENT</small>, as it is stated, <i>The wicked borroweth, and payeth not; but the righteous dealeth graciously, and giveth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 37, 21.</i> R. E<small>LEAZAR</small> came forward and <small>SAID: A HEART THAT IS EVIL</small> towards Heaven and is evil towards [all] creatures. <small>THEREUPON HE SAID TO THEM: I PREFER THE WORDS OF</small> R. E<small>LEAZAR B.</small> ‘A<small>RAK TO YOUR WORDS, BECAUSE IN HIS WORDS YOURS ARE INCLUDED</small>.
When the son of Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai died, his disciples came in to console him. R. Eliezer came and sat before him and said, ‘My master, is it your wish that I say a word in your presence?’ He replied to him, ‘Speak’. Thereupon he said, ‘Adam, the first man, had a son who died, and he allowed himself to be comforted in his loss. And whence do we know that he accepted condolence? For it is stated, <i>And Adam knew his wife again</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 4, 25.</i> Therefore do you also accept condolence’. He retorted, ‘Is it not sufficient for me to bear my own grief that you have to mention Adam’s grief?’ Then R. Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">‘One must assume that the disciples came in one by one, each ignorant that his line of approach had been previously tried without success. Joḥanan’s irritation must have increased with each successive attempt’ (Montefiore and Loewe, <i>Rabbinic Anthology</i>, p. 672).</i> entered and said, ‘My master, is it your wish that I say a word in your presence?’ He replied to him ‘Speak’. Thereupon he said, ‘Job had sons and daughters who all died in one day and he allowed himself to be comforted; you too accept condolence. And whence do we know that Job accepted condolences? For it is stated, <i>The Lord gave, and the Lord hath taken away; blessed be the name of the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Job 1, 21.</i> He retorted, ‘Is it not sufficient for me to bear my own grief that you have to mention Job’s grief?’ Next R. Jose entered, sat before him and said, ‘My master, is it your wish that I say a word in your presence?’ He replied, ‘Speak’. Thereupon he said, ‘Aaron had two grown sons and both died in one day, yet he allowed himself to be comforted, as it is stated, <i>And Aaron held his peace</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 10, 3.</i> the phrase <i>held his peace</i> implying that he accepted condolence. Therefore do you also accept condolence’. He retorted, ‘Is it not sufficient for me to bear my own grief that you have to mention Aaron’s grief?’ Then R. Simeon entered and said, ‘My master, is it your wish that I say a word in your presence?’ He replied, ‘Speak’. Thereupon he said, ‘King David had a son who died, and he allowed himself to be comforted; do you also accept condolence. And whence do we know that David accepted condolence? For it is stated, <i>And David comforted Bathsheba his wife, and went in unto her; and she bore a son, and called his name Solomon</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 12, 24.</i> Therefore do you also accept condolence’. He retorted, ‘Is it not sufficient for me to bear my own grief that you have to mention King David’s grief?’ Finally R. Eleazar b. ‘Arak<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In V the name is incorrectly printed as ‘R. Eleazar b. Azariah’.</i> entered. When [Rabban Joḥanan saw him approaching] he said to his attendant, ‘Take my clothes and follow me to the bath-house, because this man is distinguished and I cannot appear before him as I am’.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. in the clothes of a mourner.</i> Then R. Eleazar came in and sat before him and said. ‘Let me tell you a parable. To what is the matter like? To a man with whom a king had deposited an article of value. Daily the man wept and exclaimed, “Woe is me! when will I be free from the responsibility of this trust?” You, too, my master, had a son versed in the Torah, who had studied the Pentateuch,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The usual connotation of this word, <span dir="rtl">מקרא</span>, is ‘Scripture’, but in this context the Pentateuch is intended.</i> Prophets and Hagiographa, Mishnah, <i>halakoth</i> and <i>’aggadoth</i>. He has departed sinless from this world. Surely you should derive comfort from having returned your trust intact!’ Rabban Joḥanan said to him, ‘Eleazar my son, you have comforted me as men can comfort’.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. as far as lies within the power of human beings to comfort.</i><br>When they left his presence, R. Eleazar said [to his colleagues], ‘I will go to Dimsith,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In Shab. 147b (Sonc. ed., pp. 749f) the name is given as Diomsith which Jastrow identified with Emmaus, a town in the plain of Judah renowned in Talmudic days for its warm springs and luxurious life. [On this passage, cf. Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i>, I, p. 71, n. 3, who thinks that Tiberias is meant.]</i> a delectable spot with excellent and refreshing waters’. The others said, ‘We will go to Jabneh,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Or Jamnia, a town north-west of Jerusalem, near the coast, the seat of the Sanhedrin after the destruction of Jerusalem.</i> a place abounding with scholars and lovers of Torah’. Because he went to Dimsith, a delectable spot with excellent and refreshing waters, his fame in the Torah waned;<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shab. <i>loc. cit.</i> (Sonc. ed., p. 750).</i> whereas they who went to Jabneh, a place abounding with scholars and lovers of the Torah, their renown in Torah increased.

Chapter 15

<small>THEY EACH SAID THREE THINGS</small>. R. E<small>LIEZER SAID: LET THE HONOUR OF YOUR FELLOW BE DEAR TO YOU AS YOUR OWN; BE NOT EASILY MOVED TO ANGER; REPENT ONE DAY BEFORE YOUR DEATH</small>.<br><small>LET THE HONOUR OF YOUR FELLOW BE DEAR TO YOU AS YOUR OWN</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that as a man regards his own honour, so should he regard the honour of his fellow; and as a man would not wish to see an ill report spread about his own honour, so should he not wish to see an ill report spread about the honour of his fellow.<br>Another interpretation of <small>LET THE HONOUR OF YOUR FELLOW BE DEAR TO YOU AS YOUR OWN</small>: Thus if a man once possessed a million and is now deprived of all his wealth, he should not lose his self-respect even to the slightest degree.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">lit., ‘to the value of a <i>peruṭah</i>’. Though he is destitute his self-esteem should remain high; cf. Sanh. 92b (Sonc. ed., p. 621): ‘Even in times of danger one should not lay aside his insignia of office’. Honour is a precious possession.</i>
<small>BE NOT EASILY MOVED TO ANGER</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should be meek like Hillel the Elder and not impatient like Shammai the Elder.<br>What was the meekness of Hillel the Elder? It is related<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shab. 31a (Sonc. ed., pp. 138ff) for variants of this passage.</i> that two men once wagered with each other the sum of four hundred <i>zuz</i>, saying, ‘Whoever will succeed in provoking Hillel to anger will take the four hundred <i>zuz</i>’. One of them went [to call on Hillel]. The day was the eve of the Sabbath towards dusk, and Hillel was then washing his head. He came, knocked at the door, and called out, ‘Where is Hillel? Where is Hillel?’ Hillel wrapped himself in his robe and went to him, asking, ‘My son, what do you require?’ ‘I have a question to ask,’ he said. ‘Ask it,’ said Hillel. Thereupon he asked, ‘Why are the inhabitants of Palmyra bleary-eyed?’ He answered, ‘Because they live in sandy deserts, and the winds scatter the sand over their eyes; for that reason their eyes are bleary’. The man departed, waited a while, returned, again knocked at the door, and called out, ‘Where is Hillel? Where is Hillel?’ Hillel wrapped himself in his robe and went out to him, asking, ‘My son, what do you require?’ ‘I have a question<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. <i>halakah</i>, the term for a legal decision, cannot have this meaning here.</i> to ask,’ he said. ‘Ask it,’ said Hillel. Thereupon he asked, ‘Why have the Africans flat feet?’ He answered, ‘Because they live in marshy places and constantly walk in the water;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">They therefore walk barefoot, and so their feet are broad because of not having been cramped by shoe or sandal.</i> for that reason their feet are flat’. The man departed, waited a while, returned, again knocked at the door and called out, ‘Where is Hillel? Where is Hillel?’ He wrapped himself in his robe, and went out to him, asking, ‘What do you wish to ask?’ ‘I have a question to ask,’ he said. ‘Ask it,’ said Hillel. The man then wrapped himself in his cloak and sat down before him. ‘What do you wish to ask?’ said Hillel. The other rejoined, ‘Is that the manner in which a President<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. is in the plural, referring to the Presidents of the Sanhedrin.</i> replies? May there not be many like you in Israel!’ ‘Heaven forfend!’ exclaimed Hillel, ‘mind your temper. What is it you require?’ He asked, ‘Why have the Babylonians long heads?’<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">An insulting remark since Babylon was Hillel’s birthplace.</i> He answered, ‘My son, you have asked an important question. Because they lack skilful midwives there, as soon as a child is born it is placed in the arms<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. is <i>’ebarim</i>, ‘limbs’, a peculiar word in the context. A variant is <i>ḥeḳ</i>, ‘bosom’.</i> of male and female slaves; for that reason their heads are long. Here [in the Holy Land] there are expert midwives, and as soon as a child is born it is placed in a cot and its head is massaged; for that reason their heads are round’. Then said the man to him, ‘Through you I have lost four hundred <i>zuz</i>’. Hillel retorted, ‘Hillel is worthy that you should lose [twice] four hundred <i>zuz</i> on his account, and Hillel should not lose his temper’.
What was the impatience of Shammai the Elder? It is related that a man<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In Shab., <i>loc. cit.</i>, he was ‘a Gentile’, but in the context a Jew is clearly intended.</i> once came and stood before Shammai and said to him, ‘Master, how many Torahs have you?’ He answered, ‘Two, one written and one oral’. The man said, ‘I believe you regarding the written, but I do not believe you regarding the oral’. Shammai rebuked him and [24<i>b</i>] angrily drove him away. The man then came before Hillel and asked, ‘Master, how many Torahs were given [to Israel]’. He answered, ‘Two, one written and one oral’. He said, ‘I believe you regarding the written, but I do not believe you regarding the oral’. Hillel said to him, ‘My son, take a seat’. He then wrote the alphabet for him. Hillel asked him, ‘What is this letter?’<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Pointing to an <i>alef</i>.</i> ‘An <i>alef</i>,’ he replied. Hillel said, ‘It is not an <i>alef</i> but a <i>beth</i>’. He asked, ‘And what is this letter?’<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Pointing to a <i>beth</i>.</i> ‘A <i>beth</i>,’ he replied. Hillel said, ‘It is not a <i>beth</i> but a <i>gimel</i>’. ‘Moreover, how do you know that this letter is <i>alef</i>, that <i>beth</i>, and the other <i>gimel?</i>’ continued Hillel. He replied, ‘So it has been handed down to us by our forebears: this is <i>alef</i>, that <i>beth</i>, and the other <i>gimel</i>’. ‘Just as you have accepted this<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The letters of the alphabet.</i> on trust, so accept the other<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The existence of two Torahs.</i> on trust.’<br>Once a gentile was passing behind a Synagogue when he heard a child reciting the verse, <i>And these are the garments which they shall make: a breastplate, and an ephod, and a robe</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 28, 4.</i> He came to Shammai and asked him, ‘Master, for whom is all that finery?’ He answered, ‘For the High Priest who stands and ministers at the altar’. The gentile said to him, ‘Make me a proselyte on condition that you appoint me High Priest’. Shammai retorted, ‘Is there no priest in Israel and have we no High Priests in Israel to serve in the High-priesthood, except this insignificant proselyte who comes with staff and scrip only and wishes to minister as High Priest?’ He rebuked him and angrily drove him away. The gentile then came to Hillel and said to him, ‘Master, make me a proselyte on condition that you appoint me High Priest, so that I may stand and minister at the altar’. Hillel said to him, ‘Take a seat, and I will tell you something. If a man wishes to have an audience of a human king, is it not proper for him to learn how to make his entrance and his exit?’ ‘Certainly,’ answered the gentile. Hillel continued, ‘As you are desirous to have an audience of the King of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He, is it not all the more incumbent upon you to learn how to enter the Holy of Holies, how to trim the lamps, how to approach the altar, how to order the table, how to arrange the altar-pile?’ ‘Do whatever seems good to you,’ said the gentile. Hillel then wrote the alphabet for him which he soon learnt, and then [he studied] the Book of Leviticus. So he continued until he reached the verse, <i>And the common man that draweth nigh shall be put to death</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 1, 51.</i> Then the proselyte reasoned within himself thus, ‘If Israelites who are designated sons of the All-present,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Deut. 14, 1.</i> of whom the Divine Presence declares, <i>Ye shall be unto Me a kingdom of priests, and a holy nation</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 19, 6.</i> are warned by Scripture, <i>And the common man that draweth nigh shall be put to death</i>, how much more so does it apply to me, an insignificant proselyte, who am come with nothing but my scrip!’ In this manner the proselyte was reconciled of his own accord. He came to Hillel the Elder and said to him, ‘May all the blessings of the Torah alight on your head! Had you been like Shammai the Elder, I would never have entered the assembly of Israel. The impatience of Shammai sought to destroy me out of this world and out of the world to come; your gentleness brought me to life in this world and in the world to come’. It is reported that that proselyte became the father of two sons; one he named Hillel and the other Gamaliel. They were called ‘Hillel’s proselytes’.
<small>REPENT ONE DAY BEFORE YOUR DEATH</small>. The disciples asked R. Eliezer.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Shab. 153a (Sonc. ed., p. 781).</i> ‘Does, then, a man know on which day he will die that he should repent [the day before]?’ He replied, ‘All the more reason that he repent to-day lest he die tomorrow, or that he repent to-morrow lest he die the day after; as a consequence his whole life is spent in repentance’.<br>R. Jose b. Judah said in the name of R. Judah b. R. Ilai, who said it in the name of his father R. Ilai, who reported it in the name of R. Eliezer the Great: <small>REPENT ONE DAY BEFORE YOUR DEATH; WARM YOURSELF BEFORE THE FIRE OF THE SAGES, BUT BEWARE OF THEIR GLOWING COALS LEST YOU BE SCORCHED, FOR THEIR BITE IS THE BITE OF A FOX, AND THEIR STING IS THE STING OF A SCORPION; YEA ALL THEIR WORDS ARE LIKE COALS OF FIRE</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">From Aboth 2:15 (Sonc. ed., II, 10, pp. 20f).</i>

Chapter 16

R. J<small>OSHUA SAID: THE EVIL EYE, THE EVIL INCLINATION, AND THE HATRED OF MANKIND DRIVE A MAN OUT OF THE WORLD</small>.<br><small>THE EVIL EYE</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that as a man regards his own house, so he should regard the house of his fellow; and as a man would not wish an evil report spread about his wife and children, so he should not wish an evil report spread about his fellow’s wife and children.<br>Another interpretation. What is meant by <small>THE EVIL EYE</small>? It teaches that no man should begrudge the learning of his fellow. The story is told of a man who looked with a grudging eye upon his colleague’s learning that his life was cut short and he departed [prematurely from the world].
<small>THE EVIL INCLINATION</small>. What does this mean? It has been said that the evil inclination is senior to the good inclination by thirteen years,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The evil inclination, it was held, entered the human body even before birth, whereas the good inclination was acquired at the age of thirteen, the age of religious responsibility.</i> for it grows with and accompanies the child from the moment it comes forth from the mother’s womb. If the child begins to profane the Sabbath it<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the evil inclination the child has no sense of wrongdoing.</i> does not deter him; if he is about to take life it does not deter him; if he is about to commit an immoral act it does not deter him. After thirteen years, however, the good inclination is born in him. If then he is about to profane the Sabbath it<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the good inclination.</i> warns him, ‘You fool! Scripture states, <i>Every one that profaneth it shall surely be put to death</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 31, 14.</i> If he is about to take life, it warns him, ‘You fool! Scripture states, <i>Whoso sheddeth man’s blood, by man shall his blood be shed</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 9, 6.</i> If he is about to commit an immoral act, it warns him, ‘You fool! Scripture states, <i>Both the adulterer and the adulteress shall surely be put to death</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 20, 10.</i> When a man’s passions are stirred and he is about to commit an act of lewdness, all his limbs are ready to obey him, because the evil inclination is king over the two hundred and forty-eight limbs of man. On the other hand, when a man is about to perform an act of piety, all his limbs become sluggish,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. <i>mith‘annin</i> is unusual in this sense, but might be derived from the verb <i>‘innah</i>, ‘to delay’ (cf. <i>‘innuï haddin</i>, ‘the delay of justice’, Aboth 5:8). Schechter suggests the reading <i>mith‘aẓẓlin</i>, ‘are slothful’.</i> because the evil inclination which is within him bears sway over the two hundred and forty-eight limbs of his body, whereas the good inclination is like one who is confined in prison, as it is stated, <i>For out of prison he came forth to be king</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 4, 14.</i> which verse has been interpreted as referring to the good inclination.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Midrash Rabbah, Ecclesiastes, <i>ad loc.</i> (Sonc. ed., p. 123).</i><br>Others interpret this verse as referring to the righteous Joseph. When that wicked woman<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Potiphar’s wife (Gen. 39, 7ff).</i> came [to tempt him] she afflicted him with her words, saying, ‘I will have you imprisoned’; and he replied, ‘<i>The Lord looseth the prisoners</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 146, 7.</i> She said, ‘I will have your eyes plucked out’; and he replied, ‘<i>The Lord openeth the eyes of the blind</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 8.</i> She said, ‘I will bend your stature’; and he replied, ‘<i>The Lord raiseth up them that are bowed down</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> She said, ‘I will accuse you of sin’;<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘I will make you wicked’.</i> and he replied, ‘<i>The Lord loveth the righteous</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> She said, ‘I will make you a stranger’;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The word in the text <i>’armai</i>, ‘a heathen’, makes no sense. The translation follows the version quoted by the Baalë Tosafoth on Gen. 39, 12. Joseph is threatened with banishment.</i> and he replied, ‘<i>The Lord preserveth the strangers</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 9.</i> Finally he said, ‘<i>How then can I do this great wickedness</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 39, 9.</i><br>Marvel not at the [virtue of the] righteous Joseph, for consider the case of R. Zadok, the greatest man of his generation. When he was taken captive, a certain matron acquired him [as a slave] and sent in a beautiful maid-servant to him. As soon as he saw her, he turned his eyes to the wall so as not to look upon her, and sat absorbed in study the whole night long. In the morning the girl went and complained to her mistress, saying, ‘I would rather die than be given to that man!’ [The matron] sent for him and asked, ‘Why did you not treat this woman as men usually act?’ He replied, ‘What else could I do, seeing that I belong to the High-priestly line? I am from a noble family and said to myself, “[I must refrain] lest I cohabit with her and increase the number of illegitimate children in Israel” ’. On hearing his words, she ordered [his release] and set him free with great honour.<br>Do not marvel at [the virtue of] R. Zadok, for consider the case of R. ‘Aḳiba who was greater than he. When R. ‘Aḳiba came to a certain land,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">MS. E. reads ‘to Rome’.</i> a malicious accusation was laid against him before the Ruler. The latter sent in two beautiful women to him who had bathed, anointed and adorned themselves like brides for their grooms. They importuned him throughout the night, each saying, ‘Turn to me’. He sat between them, spat [in disgust], and turned to neither of them. They went and complained to the Ruler, saying, ‘We would rather die than be given to that man!’ He sent for him and asked, ‘Why did you not treat these women as men usually act towards women? Are they not beautiful? Are they not human like yourself? Did not He Who created you create them?’ He answered, ‘I could not do otherwise; their odour seemed to me like the odour of carrion, torn beasts and creeping things’.<br>Do not marvel at [the virtue of] R. ‘Aḳiba, for consider the case of R. Eliezer the Great who was more distinguished than he. R. Eliezer brought up his sister’s daughter, and for thirteen years she [slept] with him in one bed until she reached puberty. Then he said to her, ‘Go and get married’.
