She replied, ‘Am I not your maid, [even contented] to be a servant to wash the feet of your disciples?’<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">By this expression she implied her desire to marry him. The parallel passage in j.Yeb. XIII, 2, 13<small>C</small> reads, ‘I am your handmaid to wash the feet of my master’s slaves’.</i> He said to her, ‘My daughter, I am now an old man; go and marry a young man of your own age’. She replied, ‘Have I not already told you that I am your maid, [even contented] to be a servant to wash the feet of your disciples?’ When he heard her words, he obtained her consent to a betrothal and married her.
R. Reuben b. Iẓtroboli said: How can a man keep far away from the evil inclination which is within his body? For the first drop which a man injects into a woman is the evil inclination.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the first seed, the result of man’s passion, creates the evil inclination in the unborn child.</i> Moreover, the evil inclination dwells at the entrances of the heart, as it is stated, <i>Sin coucheth at the door</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 4, 7. Cf. Ber. 61a (Sonc. ed., p. 384).</i> It already speaks to the human being while he is an infant in the cot, saying, ‘That man seeks to kill you’, and [in consequence the infant] is ready to pluck out his hair.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The aggressive tendencies in a child are accordingly explained as incitement by the evil inclination, the latter creating the fear that persons wish to do him harm.</i> When an infant lies in his cot and places his hand upon a snake or a scorpion so that he is bitten, this was caused by none other than the evil inclination which is within his body. Or when he lays his hand on glowing coals and is burnt, this was caused by none other than the evil inclination which is within his body, since the evil inclination it is that drives him headlong [to destruction]. Observe, however, a kid or lamb: when it sees a well it turns back, since there is no evil inclination in a beast [to lead it into harm].<br>R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: I will explain it to you by a parable. To what is the evil inclination like? [25<i>a</i>] To a piece of iron which is cast into the fire. As long as it is in the fire one can make of it whatever vessel he wishes. So it is with the evil inclination: there is no other remedy for it but the study of the Torah which is likened to fire;<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Jer. 23, 29, homiletically explained as teaching that by a man’s devotion to Torah, God will cause the enemy (i.e. the evil inclination) to be at peace with him and cease assailing him.</i> as it is stated, <i>If thine enemy be hungry, give him bread to eat, and if he be thirsty, give him water to drink; for thou wilt heap coals of fire upon his head, and the Lord will reward thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 25, 21f.</i> Read not <i>will reward</i> [<i>yeshallem</i>] <i>thee</i> but ‘will make him at peace [<i>yashlim</i>] with thee.’<br>R. Judah the Prince said: I will explain it to you by a parable. To what is the evil inclination like? To the case of two men who entered an inn and one of them was arrested on a charge of robbery. When asked, ‘Who was with you [as an accomplice]?’ he could reply, ‘There was no one with me’; but he says to himself, ‘Since I am to be executed, let my fellow be executed along with me’. In the same way the evil inclination speaks, ‘Since I am doomed in the world to come, I will drag the entire body with me to destruction’.<br>R. Simeon b. Yoḥai said: By this reasoning Israel will never see Gehinnom. This may be explained by a parable. To what is the matter like? To a mortal king who had a field of poor quality. Certain men came and leased it at a rental of ten <i>kors</i> of wheat annually. They manured it, hoed it, watered it and weeded it; but they could only make it yield one <i>kor</i> of wheat during the year. The king asked them for an explanation and they replied, ‘Our sovereign lord! You know that the field which you leased to us originally yielded nothing to you; now after manuring it, weeding it and watering it, we made it yield us no more than one <i>kor</i> of wheat’. In like manner Israel will in the future plead with the Holy One, blessed be He, ‘Lord of the universe! Thou knowest that it is the evil inclination that incites us, as it is stated, <i>For He knoweth our impulse</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 103, 14. E.V. <i>our frame</i>, the Heb. being the same as for ‘inclination, impulse’.</i>
<small>AND THE HATRED OF MANKIND</small>. What does this mean? It teaches that a man should not adopt the rule: Love the Sages and hate the disciples, or Love the disciples and hate the <i>‘Am ha’areẓ</i>. Let his rule be: Love them all, and hate [only] heretics, apostates, seducers and informers. So said David, <i>Do not I hate them, O Lord, that hate Thee? And do not I strive with those that rise up against thee? I hate them with utmost hatred; I count them mine enemies</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXXXIX, 21f.</i> But does not Scripture declare, <i>Thou shalt love thy neighbour as thyself: I am the Lord?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 19, 18.</i> And why? Because I created him. If he acts as <i>thy people</i> should act,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ex. 22, 27, <i>Thou shalt not … curse a ruler of thy people</i>, and B.Ḳ. 94b (Sonc. ed., p. 549).</i> then you must love him, but if not, then you must not love him. R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: With a great oath<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">So according to the MSS.; V reads ‘in a great hour’ which makes no sense.</i> was this command said, <i>Thou shalt love thy neighbour as thyself. I the Lord have created him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 45, 8. E.V., <i>created it</i>.</i>—if you love him, I am trustworthy to give you a good reward; but if you do not, I am a Judge to punish you.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">On this passage, cf. Finkelstein, <i>op. cit.</i>, pp. 47-52.</i>

Chapter 17

R. J<small>OSE SAID: LET THE PROPERTY OF YOUR FELLOW BE AS DEAR TO YOU AS YOUR OWN; FIT YOURSELF FOR THE STUDY OF THE TORAH, SINCE IT DOES NOT COME TO YOU BY INHERITANCE</small>.
What does this mean? It teaches that as a man regards his own property, so he should regard the property of his fellow; and as a man would not wish to see an evil report spread about his own property, so he should not wish to see an evil report spread about the property of his fellow.<br>Another interpretation of <small>LET THE PROPERTY OF YOUR FELLOW BE AS DEAR TO YOU AS YOUR OWN</small>. What does it mean? When a disciple comes to you and asks for instruction, if it is within your power to teach him, do so; but if not, dismiss him at once and take no money from him;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As remuneration for teaching; cf. Ned. 37a (Sonc. ed., p. 113) where the taking of a fee is prohibited.</i> as it is stated, <i>Say not unto thy neighbour: Go and come again, and to-morrow I will give; when thou hast it by thee</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 3, 28.</i>
<small>FIT YOURSELF FOR THE STUDY OF THE TORAH, SINCE IT DOES NOT COME TO YOU BY INHERITANCE</small>. Thus when Moses our teacher perceived that his sons had not the knowledge of the Torah to be able to succeed him in leadership, he wrapped himself in his robe and stood up to pray. He said, ‘Sovereign of the universe! Make known to me who will come in and go out at the head of this people, as it is stated, <i>And Moses spoke unto the Lord, saying: Let the Lord, the God of the spirits of all flesh, set a man over the congregation, who may go out before them, and who may come in before them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 27, 15-17.</i> Thereupon the Holy One, blessed be He, said to him, ‘Moses, <i>take thee Joshua</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 18.</i> The Holy One, blessed be He, further said to Moses, ‘Go and appoint for him an interpreter<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Also called <i>’Amora</i>, ‘speaker’; he was the scholar who attended on the Rabbi during the lecture and expounded at length and in popular style the subject-matter given to him by the instructor.</i> that he may expound [the Torah] in your presence at the head of the notables of Israel’. At that time Moses said to Joshua, ‘Joshua, as for this people that I am delivering into your charge, they are not he-goats but kids; I am not delivering to you sheep but lambs, because as yet they have not occupied themselves with the commandments, and so have not reached the stage of he-goats;<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">And must be treated with care and consideration; cf. Sifre to Deut., §305.</i> as it is stated, <i>If thou know not, O thou fairest among women, go thy way forth by the footsteps of the flock and feed thy kids, beside the shepherds’ tents</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 1, 8.</i>
Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sifre <i>ibid.</i> and Keth. 66b (Sonc. ed., p. 405).</i> was once walking in the market place when he observed a young woman picking grains of barley from under the feet of the cattle of the Arabs. He said to her, ‘My daughter, who are you?’ She remained silent. Again he asked her, ‘My daughter, who are you?’ She continued silent [for a time], and then said to him, ‘Wait a moment’. She wrapped herself in her hair, sat down before him and said, ‘I am the daughter of Naḳdimon b. Gorion’.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. above VI, 3, p. 47.</i> He asked, ‘My daughter, what became of your father’s wealth?’ She replied, ‘Master, is not the proverb current in Jerusalem, “The salt<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A preservative. The difference in the two versions is in one letter, the former having <span dir="rtl">חסר</span> and the latter <span dir="rtl">חסד</span>.</i> of wealth is its diminution [by charitable use] (or, according to another version, “[The salt of wealth is] benevolence”)?’ ‘And what became of the wealth of your father-in-law?’ he asked. She replied, ‘The one swallowed up the other’.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The fortunes of the two men were interlocked.</i> Then Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai turned to his disciples and said, ‘All my life I have read the verse, <i>If thou know not, O thou fairest among women, go thy way forth by the footsteps of the flock</i>, but did not understand its significance until to-day when I have realized its meaning, viz. that Israel will come under the domination of the lowest of the nations, and not merely under the domination of the lowest of the nations but will be among the dung of their cattle’. The young woman then asked him, ‘Master, do you remember when you put your signature to my marriage contract?’ ‘I do,’ he replied; and turning to his disciples he said, ‘By the Temple Service! I signed this young woman’s marriage contract, and it mentioned a settlement of one million Tyrian golden dinars’. [It is related that] the members of the family to which the young woman belonged never set out from their house to go to the Temple until the finest woollen rugs were spread before them.
The story<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Further anecdotes of the wretched plight of even the wealthiest families in Israel during the siege of Jerusalem and after.</i> is told of a young woman who was taken captive together with her ten maids. A Gentile acquired her and she grew up in his house. One day he handed her a jug and said, ‘Go and fetch me some water’. One of her maids arose and took it from her. ‘What is this?’ he asked; and she replied, ‘By your life, master! I was one of five hundred maids of this young woman’s mother.’ When he heard these words, he at once set her free together with her ten maids.
Another story is told of a young woman who was taken captive. A Gentile acquired her and she grew up in his house. The Angel of Dreams<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘the master of the dream’; cf. Ber. 10b (Sonc. ed., p. 55, n. 5) where another explanation is suggested.</i> appeared to him and said, ‘Send the young woman from your house’. His wife said to him, ‘Do not send her away’. Again the Angel of Dreams appeared to him and said, ‘If you do not send her away I will kill you’, and he let her go. He followed her, saying [to himself], ‘I must go and see what will befall this young woman’. On her journey she became thirsty and went down to drink at a spring, When she placed her hand on the wall, a snake came and bit her so that she died, [her body] floating on the surface of the water. He went down, took her out and buried her. On his return home he remarked to his wife, ‘This people whom you behold bear the anger of none other than their Father in heaven’.
<small>AND LET ALL YOUR DEEDS BE DONE FOR THE SAKE OF HEAVEN</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The conclusion of R. Jose’s teaching in Aboth 2:17 (Sonc. ed., II, 12, p. 22).</i> That means, for the sake of the Torah, as it is stated, <i>In all thy ways acknowledge Him, and He will direct thy paths</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 3, 6. By following the ways of the Torah one arrives at a knowledge of God.</i>
R. S<small>IMEON SAID: BE CAREFUL TO READ THE SHEMA‘ AND THE ‘AMIDAH; AND WHEN YOU PRAY LET NOT YOUR PRAYER BE A CONVERSATION</small><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The reading in Aboth 2:18 (Sonc. ed., II, 13, p. 23) is ‘a fixed task’.</i> <small>BUT SUPPLICATION BEFORE THE</small> H<small>OLY</small> O<small>NE, BLESSED BE</small> H<small>E, AS IT IS WRITTEN, FOR</small> H<small>E IS GRACIOUS AND FULL OF COMPASSION, SLOW TO ANGER AND ABUNDANT IN MERCY, AND REPENTETH</small> H<small>IM OF THE EVIL</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Joel 2, 13. A variant text is the verse from Jon. 4, 2.</i><br>R. E<small>LIEZER SAID: BE EAGER TO STUDY THE TORAH, AND KNOW HOW TO ANSWER AN UNBELIEVER</small>. Let not one word of the Torah be forgotten by you. <small>KNOW BEFORE WHOM YOU TOIL, AND WITH WHOM YOU HAVE ENTERED INTO A COMPACT</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The phrase <i>Ba‘al berith</i> as a designation for God is not found elsewhere in Rabbinic literature. GRA substitutes <i>Ba‘al melakteka</i>, ‘thy Employer’, in conformity with Aboth 2:19 (Sonc. ed., II, 16, p. 25).</i>

Chapter 18

Likewise<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This passage links up with the dictum in Aboth 2:11 (Sonc. ed., II, 8, p. 18) where Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai recounts the distinctive qualifications of his five foremost disciples.</i> R. Judah the Prince used to recount the praises of the following Sages: R. Ṭarfon, R. ‘Aḳiba, R. Eleazar b. Azariah, R. Joḥanan b. Nuri and R. Jose the Galilean.<br>R. Ṭarfon he described as a heap of stones (another version: a heap of nuts). When a man removes one, they all topple and fall over each other. So it was with R. Ṭarfon; whenever a disciple came to him and said, ‘Teach me’, he would produce for him [instruction in] Scripture, Mishnah, Midrash, <i>halakoth</i> and <i>’aggadoth</i>. When that disciple departed, he left laden with blessing and [intellectual] wealth.<br>R. ‘Aḳiba he described as a treasury with compartments.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the view of Rashi, given in Giṭ. 67a (Sonc. ed., p. 317), where he explains <span dir="rtl">בלום</span> as ‘divided into several compartments’. Jastrow, <i>s.v.</i> <span dir="rtl">בלם</span>, defines it ‘a packed treasury’ (of knowledge), but explains the variant, <span dir="rtl">בלוס</span> <i>(s.v.</i> <span dir="rtl">בלס</span>) as ‘a mind full of knowledge’. [Bacher, <i>Ag. d. pal. Amoräer</i>, II, p. 65, n. 6, gives <span dir="rtl">בלום</span> as the correct reading which he understands as ‘locked up’ or ‘packed’.]</i> To what may [25<i>b</i>] R. ‘Aḳiba be compared? To a pedlar who takes his sack and goes into a field. When he finds wheat he drops it in the sack; similarly when he finds barley, spelt, beans and lentils he drops them all in the sack. On returning home he sorts them all out, putting the wheat apart, the barley apart, the spelt, the beans, the lentils all apart. Such was the practice of R. ‘Aḳiba: he sorted all the Torah [he amassed] into its various compartments.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The text varies between <i>ṭaba‘oth</i>, ‘rings’ and <i>maṭbe‘oth</i>, ‘coins’. Rashi, <i>loc. cit.</i>, adopts the latter according to which the meaning is: As coins are put into a box according to their various denominations, so did R. ‘Aḳiba systematize the whole of the oral tradition. [Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i> I, p. 267, n. 1, accepts the former reading, and explains that R. ‘Aḳiba sorted out the heterogeneous material and arranged it like ‘a chain of connected links’.]</i><br>R. Eleazar b. Azariah he described as a perfumer’s chest. To what may R. Eleazar be compared? To a perfumer who takes his chest with him and enters a city. The townsfolk come out and enquire, ‘Have you fine oil? Have you spikenard oil? Have you balsam oil?’ and find that he has everything [they require]. So it was with R. Eleazar b. Azariah. When a disciple came to him and enquired about Scripture he enlightened him, about Mishnah he enlightened him, about Midrash or <i>halakoth</i> or <i>’aggadoth</i> he enlightened him. When the disciple departed he left laden with [intellectual] wealth and blessing.
When R. Joshua was an old man, his disciples came to visit him.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ḥag. 3a (Sonc. ed., pp. 8f). This passage is inserted as an example of the skill of R. Eleazar b. ‘Azariah in <i>‘aggadoth</i>.</i> He said to them, ‘My sons, what new interpretation was there at the House of Study to-day?’ They replied, ‘We are your disciples and drink your waters’.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We cannot possibly have anything to teach you.</i> He said to them, ‘Heaven forfend! No generation is orphaned of its Sages.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He deprecated the suggestion that there were no other great teachers.</i> Whose Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Rabban Gamaliel, the President of the College, had been deposed because of his high-handed treatment and humiliation of R. Joshua, and R. Eleazar b. ‘Azariah had been elected in his place. When Rabban Gamaliel had been reinstated it was arranged that he should give the discourse three Sabbaths in the month and R. Eleazar b. ‘Azariah one Sabbath (cf. Ber. 27b, 28a; Sonc. ed., pp. 166ff). This explains the question asked by R. Joshua.</i> was it?’ They replied, ‘It was the Sabbath of R. Eleazar b. Azariah’. He asked, ‘And what was the theme of his discourse to-day?’ They answered, ‘It was the section [beginning], <i>Assemble the people, the men and the women and the little ones</i>’.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 31, 12.</i> He further asked, ‘What exposition did he give in connection with it?’ They said, ‘His comment was as follows: The men came to learn, the women to listen, but why had the little ones to come? In order to grant a good reward to those who brought them’. He said to them, ‘You had such a precious pearl in your possession and sought to withhold it from me! Had you visited me only for the purpose of imparting this to me, it would have been sufficient’. They said to him, ‘He also gave this exposition: <i>The words of the wise are as goads, and as nails well fastened are the words of masters of assemblies:</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 12, 11. E.V., … <i>fastened are those that are composed in collections</i>.</i> as the goad guides the heifer along its furrows, so do the words of the Torah guide a man along the paths of life. But [should you contend,] As the goad is movable so the words of the Torah are likewise movable,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. not of permanent authority.</i> the text adds, <i>And as nails well fastened:</i> As nails well fastened cannot be pulled out, so the words of the Torah cannot be dislodged. <i>Masters of assemblies:</i> these are the disciples of the wise who enter [the House of Study] and sit in several groups; some declare a thing forbidden others declaring it permitted, some pronounce a thing unclean others pronouncing it clean, some rule a thing unfit others ruling it fit. Now lest a man say to you, “I will sit idle and not study”, the text continues, <i>They were given from one shepherd</i>, i.e. one God created them,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">[Cf. what ‘a heavenly voice’ declared of the controversies between the Schools of Hillel and Shammai, ‘They both speak the words of the Living God’ (‘Erub. 13b, Sonc. ed., pp. 85f).</i> one leader<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. Moses.</i> gave them, the Lord of all creation uttered them. Therefore make your ear like a funnel and allow to enter therein the words of those who forbid and those who permit, the words of those who pronounce unclean and of those who pronounce clean, the words of those who rule it unfit and of those who rule it fit’.
R. Joḥanan b. Nuri he described as a chest [filled with] <i>halakoth</i>.<br>R. Jose the Galilean he described as a collector of the finest, devoid of arrogance; because he held fast to the rule of the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">That a man should be humble.</i> derived from Mount Sinai, and diligently taught all the wise men of Israel.
Issi b. Judah used to describe certain Sages in these terms:<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Giṭ. 67a (Sonc. ed., pp. 317f.) from which the following annotations are quoted.</i> R. Meir was a wise man and a scribe;<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">That was his profession.</i> R. Judah was a wise man when he desired to be such;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. when he was not too hasty, he could be even wiser than R. Meir.</i> the Mishnah of R. Eliezer b. Jacob was little and good;<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘a <i>ḳab</i> (little in quantity) but fine (well-sifted)’. So that whenever he gives an opinion, the <i>halakah</i> follows him.</i> R. Jose always had his reasons; R. Joḥanan b. Nuri was a chest [filled with] <i>halakoth;</i> R. Jose the Galilean was a collector of the very finest, devoid of arrogance; R. Simeon b. Gamaliel was a shop stocked with the best purple; R. Simeon studied much and forgot little. Later when R. Simeon met Issi b. Judah he said to him, ‘Why do you prate about me before the Sages?’ He replied, ‘I merely said that you study much and forget little, and the little you do forget is but the bran of your learning’.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. those statements which were not followed by the <i>halakah</i>.</i>

Chapter 19

‘Aḳabya b. Mahalalel said: Whosoever takes four things to heart<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Aboth 3:1 (Sonc. ed., p. 26) where the more popular version reads: ‘Consider three things’, etc., omitting the third item, ‘what he is destined to become—dust, worms and maggots’ as being heterodox in outlook; cf. Finkelstein, <i>Akiba: Scholar, Saint and Martyr</i>, pp. 159f, where the maxim of ‘Aḳabya is described as ‘this unequivocal denial of personal immortality’. See also DER III, 1 (below, p. 543) where the saying is quoted as in the present text in the name of Ben ‘Azzai.</i> will never sin: whence he came, whither he is going, what he is destined to become, and who is his Judge. ‘Whence he came’—from a place of darkness; ‘whither he is going’—to a place of thick darkness; ‘what he is destined to become’—dust, worms and maggots; ‘and who is his Judge’—the King of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He.
R. Simeon said: Man came from a place of darkness and returns to a place of darkness. He came from a fetid drop, from a place which no eye can behold. And what is he destined to become? Dust, worms and maggots, as it is stated, <i>How much less man, that is a worm! and the son of man, that is a maggot!</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Job 25, 6.</i> R. Eleazar b. Jacob said: <i>Man that is a worm</i> when alive, <i>and the son of man that is a maggot</i> when dead. In what sense is he a worm when alive? By reason of the lice [which infest him]. And a maggot when dead? By reason of the creeping things which he generates when dead.
R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: I will illustrate it to you by a parable. To what can the matter be compared? To a king who built a large palace in which he resided,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">According to a variant reading: ‘which he elaborately adorned’. GRA reads ‘magnificent in every detail’. V has ‘large palaces’.</i> but the waste-pipe of a tannery ran through it and emptied itself at the entrance. Every passer-by exclaimed, ‘How beautiful and magnificent would that palace be if the waste-pipe of the tannery did not run through it!’ Just so is it with man; and if in his present state, when from his entrails issue fetid streams,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The natural secretions.</i> he lords it over all creatures, how much more would he lord it over all creatures if there were to issue from him streams of fine oil, balsam and spikenard!
When R. Eliezer fell ill, his disciples came to visit him. They sat before him and said, ‘Master, tell us in one word what you have taught us’.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘teach us one word of what you have taught us’. In the master’s last hours the disciples enquire of him the epitome of his wisdom and experience. In the parallel passage, Ber. 28b (Sonc. ed., p. 173), the text reads: ‘Teach us the ways of life’.</i> He replied, ‘What shall I tell you? Go forth and be mindful of the honour of your fellow-man, and when you pray know before Whom you are standing and praying. Then will you merit the life of the world to come’.<br>R. Eleazar said: Five things did we learn from R. Eliezer [on that day], and they afforded us greater pleasure than all that we had enjoyed from him in his lifetime, and they are as follows: If a round cushion, a ball, a shoemaker’s last, an amulet or a phylactery, about which you spoke to us, were torn, what would be the law?<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The five articles enumerated have in common that they are made of leather and are permanently filled with stuffing. It is established law that an article of leather can contract defilement only if it is a receptacle, i.e. it has a cavity in which it can hold things. Since the articles here listed are <i>permanently</i> filled, the Sage held the view that they are not considered to be vessels with receptacles and consequently cannot be ritually defiled. R. Eliezer, however, was of the opinion that since the filling was removable, they were to be considered vessels with receptacles and could contract defilement. Furthermore, if any one of the enumerated articles were torn the opinion was unanimous that it could contract defilement, because some of the filling must have fallen out and in consequence a receptacle was formed. If, in the circumstances stated, the article became unclean, there was a further argument between R. Eliezer and the Sages whether it was necessary to remove <i>all</i> the filling before immersion took place. The Sages held the view that this must be done otherwise the immersion would be invalid, since the filling interposed between the vessel and the water. R. Eliezer, on the other hand, ruled that it could be immersed as it was without the removal of the filling. R. Eliezer here put forward his views as ancient traditional rulings. Cf. Kel. XXIII, 1 (Sonc. ed., p. 110).</i> The answer he gave was: They can contract uncleanness so be careful with them; and [if unclean] they may be immersed as they are. These laws are well established, for they were communicated to Moses on Mount Sinai.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For textual variants of this passage, cf. below XXV 2 (p. 171), Sanh. 68a (Sonc. ed., pp. 462f) and DER III, end (below p. 544).</i>

Chapter 20

R. Ḥananiah, the deputy High Priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In Aboth 3:2 (Sonc. ed., p. 27) there is a different saying attributed to ‘R. Ḥanina, the deputy High Priest’, and in III, 5 (Sonc. ed., III, 5, p. 29) a statement of R. Neḥuniah b. Ha-Ḳanah bears some resemblance to what is quoted here.</i> said: He who lays the words of the Torah to his heart will be freed from the anxieties of the sword, hunger, madness, lust, the evil inclination, adultery, vain thoughts, and of the yoke of human cares; for so it is written in the Book of Psalms by David king of Israel, <i>The precepts of the Lord are right, rejoicing the heart; the commandment of the Lord is pure, enlightening the eyes</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 19, 9.</i> And he who does not lay the words of the Torah to his heart will be burdened with the anxieties of the sword, hunger, madness, lust, the evil inclination, adultery, vain thoughts, and of the yoke of human cares; for so it is written in the Book of Deuteronomy by Moses our master, <i>And they shall be upon thee for a sign and for a wonder, and upon thy seed for ever; because thou didst not serve the Lord thy God with joyfulness, and with gladness of heart, by reason of the abundance of all things; therefore shalt thou serve thine enemy whom the Lord shall send against thee in hunger, and in thirst, and in nakedness, and in want of all things</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 28, 46-48.</i> <i>In hunger:</i> what does this mean? When a man longs to eat barley bread and cannot afford even that, his enemies demand of him white bread and rich meat. <i>And in thirst:</i> what does this mean? When a man longs to drink a drop of sour wine or a drop of beer and cannot afford even that, his enemies demand of him the best wine of all the lands. <i>And in nakedness:</i> what does this mean? When a man wishes to wear a woollen or linen shirt and cannot afford even that, his enemies demand of him the finest silks and satins of all the lands. <i>And in want of all things:</i> he will be without a lamp, [26<i>a</i>] without a knife and without a table. Another interpretation of <i>And in want of all things:</i> he will be without vinegar and salt. When people curse one another they say, ‘May there be no vinegar or salt in your house!’<br>He used to say: [It is written:] <i>Look not upon me, that I am swarthy, that the sun hath tanned me;</i> [<i>my mother’s sons were incensed against me</i>]:<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 1, 6.</i> these are the councillors<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <i>bula’oth</i>. Cf. Giṭ. 37a (Sonc. ed., p. 151, n. 5, where the word is explained as the Greek for ‘city councils’). V has <i>banoth</i> (daughters) which does not make sense. [On the passage, cf. Büchler, <i>Studies in Sin and Atonement</i>, pp. 63f and <i>The Economic Conditions of Judaea</i>, p. 28, n. 2.]</i> of Judah who cast from themselves the yoke of the Holy One, blessed be He, and set a human king over them.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The lords of Judah were so incensed against God that in their exasperation they acted to their own hurt.</i><br>Another interpretation of <i>My mother’s sons were incensed against me:</i> this refers to Moses when he killed the Egyptian,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Moses was so incensed at the injustice which he witnessed that he acted zealously ‘for Me’, i.e. on God’s behalf.</i> as it is stated, <i>And it came to pass in those days, when Moses was grown up, that he went out unto his brethren, and looked on their burdens … and he looked this way and that way, and when he saw that there was no man, he smote the Egyptian, and hid him in the sand</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 2, 11f.</i> What do the words <i>that there was no man</i> imply? They teach that Moses had set up a Sanhedrin of ministering angels<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since no human beings were about, he had no alternative but to call upon angels to form a court of justice.</i> and said to them, ‘Shall I kill this man?’ They replied, ‘Kill him’. And do you think that he killed by the sword? No, he killed him with speech, as it is stated, <i>Dost thou</i> say <i>to kill me as thou didst kill the Egyptian</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 14. E.V., <i>thinkest thou to kill me</i>.</i> proving that he killed him by [pronouncing] the Name.<br>Another interpretation of <i>My mother’s sons were incensed against me:</i> this refers to Moses when he fled to Midian; as it is stated, <i>When Pharaoh heard this thing, he sought to slay Moses. But Moses fled from the face of Pharaoh, and dwelt in the land of Midian; and he sat by a well. Now the priest of Midian had seven daughters … and the shepherds came and drove them away; but Moses stood up and helped them and watered their flocks</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 15-17. Moses was incensed by the action of the shepherds.</i> Moses came forward and sat in judgment upon [the men]. He said to them, ‘In the ordinary way men draw<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Or ‘fill’ according to a variant.</i> the water and women give the flocks to drink, but here women draw the water and men [take it and] give their flocks to drink! This is an injustice; you are guilty in law’. Thereupon he rose up and helped [the women].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Following the emendation of GRA and Schechter. V reads ‘you are guilty at the hour’.</i> Some report the matter as follows: So long as Moses was standing by the edge of the well the waters surged up towards him, but when he moved away the waters receded. At that moment he exclaimed, ‘Woe is me! I have abandoned my people and am come to dwell with strangers!’<br>Another interpretation of <i>My mother’s sons were incensed against me:</i> this refers to the people of Israel when they made the golden calf.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The people here showed their resentment against God by making a golden calf, and the spies (next §) by spreading an evil report about the land.</i> At first they said, <i>All that the Lord hath spoken will we do and obey</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXIV, 7.</i> but later they said, <i>This is thy god, O Israel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXII, 4.</i><br>Another interpretation of <i>My mother’s sons were incensed against me</i>: this refers to the spies when they spread an evil report about the land, and in consequence caused the carcasses of Israel to fall in the wilderness; as it is stated, <i>Your carcasses shall fall in this wilderness</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 14, 29.</i><br><i>They made me keeper of the vineyards</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 1, 6. The verse concludes, <i>but mine own vineyard have I not kept</i>. Israel, God’s vineyard, was unkept by Him because of the people’s shortcomings.</i> The Holy One, blessed be He, declared, ‘Who was it that caused Me to show favour to the Gentiles, if not Israel? For so long as the Gentiles dwell at ease, the people of Israel are oppressed, rejected and tossed about’.<br>Another interpretation of <i>They made me keeper of the vineyards:</i> this refers to the people of Israel when they were exiled to Babylon. The prophets in their midst arose and bade them, ‘Set apart your <i>terumah</i> and your tithes.’ They replied, ‘It was because we did not separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes that we were exiled from our land, and now you bid us to set apart <i>terumah</i> and tithes [in exile]?’ Scripture therefore states, <i>They made me keeper of the vineyards;</i> [<i>but mine own vineyard have I not kept</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> Because Israel had not observed the laws relating to fields and vineyards, they were driven into captivity where they had to labour in the vineyards of strangers.</i>

Chapter 21

R. D<small>OSA B.</small> H<small>ARKINAS SAID: MORNING SLEEP, AND MIDDAY WINE</small>, [<small>AND CHILDREN’S TALK, AND SITTING IN THE MEETING-HOUSES OF THE IGNORANT</small>] <small>DRIVE A MAN OUT OF THE WORLD</small>.<br><small>MORNING SLEEP</small>—what does this mean? It teaches that a man should not wilfully sleep late so that the time-limit for the recital of the <i>Shema‘</i> passes; for if he sleep late so that the time-limit for reciting the <i>Shema‘</i> passes he consequently neglects the study of the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">By reciting the <i>Shema‘</i> one fulfils a dual obligation: the duty of proclaiming the unity of God and that of Torah-study; cf. Yalḳuṭ on Ps. 1, 2, §614.</i> as it is stated, <i>The sluggard saith: There is a lion in the way; yea, a lion is in the streets. The door is turning upon its hinges, and the sluggard is still upon his bed</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 26, 13f. In V. the order of the verses is reversed.</i><br><small>MIDDAY WINE</small>—what does this mean? It teaches that a man should not form the habit of drinking wine at midday; for when he drinks wine at midday, the consequence is that he neglects the [study of the] whole Torah, as it is stated, <i>Woe to thee, O land, when thy king is a boy, and thy princes feast in the morning</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 10, 16.</i> And it continues, <i>Happy art thou, O land, when thy king is a free man</i>, [<i>and thy princes eat in due season!</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 17.</i> And when is that <i>due season?</i> You must say that it is in the time to come, as it is stated, <i>I the Lord will hasten it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The coming salvation.</i> <i>in its due season</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 60, 22, E.V., <i>in its time</i>.</i> Scripture also states, <i>Like the time it will be said of Jacob and of Israel: What hath God wrought!</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 23, 23, E.V., <i>now it is said of Jacob</i>. The reference in the verse is to the time to come when Israel will enjoy close relationship with God, knowing what He is about to do.</i> Thus said the Holy One, blessed be He, to Balaam: <i>Like the time</i> (<i>ka‘eth</i>), and not ‘in the time’ (<i>ba‘eth</i>)—not in the time in which you live, but <i>like the time</i> when I am about to bring deliverance to Israel.<br><small>CHILDREN’S TALK</small>—what does this mean? It teaches that a man should not make a practice of sitting and studying at home;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">So according to the MSS. V. reads: ‘a man should not make a practice, while studying at home, of engaging in conversation with his wife and children, because all the time he sits at home and studies and converses with his children and members of his household, the outcome is’, etc. The proper place for study is the house of learning where his family will not distract him.</i> because when a man sits and studies at home and engages in conversation with his children and members of his household, the outcome is that he neglects the Torah; and it is stated, <i>This book of the law shall not depart out of thy mouth, but thou shalt meditate therein day and night</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Josh. 1, 8.</i><br><small>AND SITTING IN THE MEETING-HOUSES OF THE IGNORANT</small>—what does this mean? It teaches that a man should not sit with idlers in the market-place;<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">[On the identity of ‘idlers in the marketplace’ with ‘the ignorant’, cf. Büchler, <i>The Economic Conditions of Judaea</i>, p. 31n.]</i> [for when a man sits with idlers in the market-place] it follows that he neglects the Torah, as it is stated, <i>Happy is the man that hath not walked in the counsel of the wicked nor stood in the way of sinners, nor sat in the seat of the scornful. But his delight is in the law of the Lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 1, 1f.</i><br>R. Meir said: What does the verse mean by <i>nor sat in the seat of the scornful?</i> It refers to the theatres and circuses<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">‘and circuses’ is inserted from the MSS.</i> of the Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">V. ‘scorners’.</i> where men are condemned to death, and it is written, <i>I hate the gathering of evil-doers</i> (<i>mere‘im</i>), <i>and will not sit with the wicked</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXVI, 5.</i> By <i>mere‘im</i> the wicked are meant, as it is stated, <i>For evil-doers</i> (<i>mere‘im</i>) <i>shall be cut off</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXVII, 9.</i> And what will be their punishment in the hereafter? Even as it is stated, <i>For behold the day cometh, it burneth as a furnace; and all the proud, and all that work wickedness shall be stubble</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Mal. 3, 19.</i> And by <i>the proud</i> none other are intended but the scorners, as it is stated, <i>A proud and haughty man, scorner is his name</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 21, 24.</i>
It is related of R. ‘Aḳiba that once when he was sitting and lecturing to his disciples he recalled the conduct of his youth,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Until the age of forty, R. ‘Aḳiba was wholly unlearned in the Torah; cf. VI, 2, p. 43.</i> and offered up the prayer, ‘I thank Thee, O Lord my God, that Thou hast set my portion with those who sit in the House of Study, and hast not set my portion with the idlers in the market-place’.

Chapter 22

R. Ḥ<small>ANINA B.</small> D<small>OSA SAID: HE WHOSE FEAR OF SIN COMES BEFORE HIS WISDOM, HIS WISDOM SHALL ENDURE</small>; [<small>BUT HE WHOSE WISDOM COMES BEFORE HIS FEAR OF SIN, HIS WISDOM SHALL NOT ENDURE</small>,] as it is stated, <i>The fear of the Lord is the beginning of wisdom</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 111, 10.</i><br><small>HE USED TO SAY: HE WHOSE DEEDS EXCEED</small> [<small>HIS WISDOM, HIS WISDOM SHALL ENDURE; BUT HE WHOSE WISDOM EXCEEDS HIS DEEDS, HIS WISDOM SHALL NOT ENDURE</small>,] as it is stated, <i>We will do and obey</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 24, 7. Credit was ascribed to Israel that, at the time of the Revelation at mount Sinai, they declared their readiness ‘to do’ even before they had heard the commandments; cf. Shab. 88a (Sonc. ed., p. 418).</i><br>Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai was asked, ‘How would you describe the man who is both learned and sin-fearing?’ He replied, ‘He is a craftsman who has the tools of his craft in his hand’. ‘And the man who is learned but not sin-fearing?’ He replied, ‘He is a craftsman who has not the tools of his craft in his hand’. ‘And the man who is sin-fearing but not learned?’ He replied, ‘He is no craftsman though he has the craftsman’s tools in his hand’.<br>R. E<small>LEAZAR B.</small> A<small>ZARIAH SAID: WHERE THERE IS NO TORAH THERE ARE NO MANNERS</small>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted from Aboth 3:21 (Sonc. ed., III, 17, p. 40). The next saying is from <i>ibid.</i> 2 with variants and in the reverse order.</i> etc. He used to say: A man who has good deeds to his credit and has learnt much Torah, to what is he like? To a tree planted by the waters, whose branches are few but whose roots are many, so that even if the four winds of the world come and blow against it they cannot move it from its place; as it is stated, <i>For he shall be as a tree planted by the waters</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 17, 8.</i> etc. But he who has no good deeds to his credit and yet has learnt much Torah, to what is he like? To a tree planted in the desert, whose branches are many but whose roots are few, so that when the wind blows against it, it uproots and overturns it; as it is stated, <i>For he shall be like a tamarisk in the desert</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 6.</i> etc.<br>Rabban Gamaliel said: Provide yourself with a teacher and get a companion:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is a maxim of Joshua b. Peraḥiah in Aboth 1:6 (Sonc. ed., p. 5).</i> a teacher for wisdom and a companion for study. Remove yourself from all doubt, and do not make a habit [26<i>b</i>] of tithing by guesswork.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">cf. <i>ibid.</i> 16 (Sonc. ed., p. 9).</i>
Simeon his son said: All my days I have grown up among the wise and have found nothing better for a man than silence.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>ibid.</i> 17 (Sonc. ed., p. 9).</i> If silence is good for the wise, how much more so for fools!
Wisdom does not produce words, nor do words produce wisdom; [the chief thing] is doing.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A comment on the maxim in Aboth 1:17 (Sonc. ed., p. 9): ‘Study is not the chief thing but deed’.</i> He who multiplies words brings about sin, as it is stated, <i>In the multitude of words there wanteth not transgression;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 10, 19.</i> and it further states, <i>Even a fool, when he holdeth his peace, is counted wise</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XVII, 28.</i>

Chapter 23

B<small>EN</small> Z<small>OMA SAID: WHO IS WISE? HE THAT LEARNS FROM ALL MEN, AS IT IS STATED, FROM ALL MY TEACHERS HAVE I GOT UNDERSTANDING</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 119, 99, E.V., <i>I have more understanding than all my teachers</i>.</i> Who is the humblest among the humble? He who is humble like Moses our teacher, as it is stated, <i>The man Moses was very meek</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 12, 3.</i> <small>WHO IS THE RICHEST AMONG THE RICH? HE WHO REJOICES IN HIS PORTION, AS IT IS STATED, WHEN THOU EATEST THE LABOUR OF THY HANDS, HAPPY SHALT THOU BE, AND IT SHALL BE WELL WITH THEE</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 128, 2. In Aboth 4:1 (Sonc. ed., p. 43) the question reads, ‘Who is rich?’.</i> <small>WHO IS THE MIGHTIEST AMONG THE MIGHTY? HE WHO SUBDUES HIS PASSIONS, AS IT IS WRITTEN, HE THAT IS SLOW TO ANGER IS BETTER THAN THE MIGHTY; AND HE THAT RULETH HIS SPIRIT THAN HE THAT TAKETH A CITY</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 16, 32. In Aboth <i>loc. cit.</i> the question reads, ‘Who is mighty?’</i> Whosoever subdues his passions is accounted as though he conquered a city filled with mighty men, as it is stated, <i>A wise man scaleth the city of the mighty;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXI, 22.</i> and<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">GRA inserts here ‘Another interpretation’ which is to be preferred, because what follows is an exposition substantially different from the preceding.</i> <i>the mighty</i> are none other than those who are strong in [the performance of the] Torah, as it is stated, <i>Ye mighty in strength, that fulfil His word</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 103, 20. The verse therefore means: the wise man is raised to the category of ‘the mighty’ who fulfil God’s word.</i> Some say: They are the ministering angels, as it is stated, <i>Bless the Lord, ye angels of His, ye mighty in strength</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> The verse is thereby interpreted to mean that the wise man is raised above the level of the angels.</i> Others say: [Who is mighty?] Who turns an enemy into his friend.
R. N<small>EHORAI SAID: WANDER FORTH TO A PLACE OF THE TORAH</small>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">From Aboth 4:18 (Sonc. ed., IV, 14, p. 51).</i> etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A saying of Ben ‘Azzai, <i>ibid.</i> 3 (Sonc. ed., pp. 44f.). [Cf. Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i> I, p. 410, n. 1.]</i>He used to say: Despise no man and consider nothing impossible, as it is stated, <i>Whoso despiseth the word shal suffer thereby; but he that feareth the commandment shall be rewarded</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 13, 13.</i><br><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">GRA inserts here: ‘Elisha b. ’Abuyah used to say: If one learns as a child, what is it like? Like ink written on clean paper, etc. (Aboth 4 25, Sonc. ed., IV, 20, p. 55). Cf. §3 below.</i>He used to say: He who learns Torah in his youth, to what is he like? To a heifer which has been broken in while young, as it is stated, <i>Ephraim is a heifer well broken, that loveth to thresh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Hos. 10, 11. Trained from its early days to thresh, the animal welcomes the task. So, too, the scholar, initiated in his youth to Torah-study, finds it a welcome duty.</i> But he who learns Torah in his old age is like a heifer which was broken in only in its late years, as it is stated, <i>For Israel is stubborn like a stubborn heifer</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> IV, 16.</i><br>He used to say: He who learns Torah in his youth is like dough which a woman kneads with warm water;<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Following the emendation of GRA.</i> but he who learns Torah in his old age is like dough which a woman kneads with cold water.
R. Eliezer b. Jacob said: He who learns Torah in his youth is like writing which is written on new paper; but he who learns Torah in his old age is like writing which is written on old paper.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. paper which has once been written on and rubbed out, so that any further writing would be blurred and indistinct.</i>
Rabban Simeon b. Gamaliel added to the former’s saying: He who learns Torah in his youth is like a young man who married a virgin: they suit one another and desire one another. But he who learns Torah in his old age is like an old man who married a virgin: she suits him but he does not suit her; she desires him but he withdraws from her, as it is stated, <i>As arrows in the hand of a mighty man, so are the children of one’s youth</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 127, 4.</i> and in the following verse it is written, <i>Happy is the man that hath his quiver full of them</i>.<br>He who learns [Torah] and forgets it is like a woman who bears children and buries them, as it is stated, <i>Though they bring up their children, yet I will bereave them till none is left</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Hos. 9, 12, E.V., <i>that there be not a man left</i>.</i> Read not <i>I will bereave them</i> but ‘I will make them forget’.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The two verbs differ in only one letter, <span dir="rtl">ושכלתים</span>, ‘and I will bereave them’, and <span dir="rtl">ושכחתים</span>, ‘and I will make them forget’. The lesson is derived by drawing upon both verbs: bereavement is the result of forgetting the Torah.</i><br>R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: He who learns Torah in his youth is like a surgeon who, even when confronted with a growth, possesses a knife for the operation and remedial drugs to cure; but he who learns Torah in his old age is like a surgeon who, when confronted with a growth, possesses a knife for the operation but has no drugs to cure.<br>Therefore<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph follows logically on the last but one, and suggests means whereby the words of the Torah shall not be forgotten; cf. ‘Erub. 54b (Sonc. ed., pp. 381ff). The preceding paragraph, the maxim of R. Simeon b. Eleazar, is manifestly out of place where it stands, and is transferred by GRA to the end of chap. XXII. It could very well be inserted between §§3 and 4 of this chapter.</i> let the words of the Torah be distinguished so that one thing [may be derived] from another and one thing [may be equated] with another, as it is stated, <i>Bind them upon thy fingers, write them upon the table of thy heart;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 7, 3.</i> and Scripture also states, <i>Bind them continually upon thy heart, tie them about thy neck</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VI, 21.</i>

Chapter 24

Elisha b. ’Abuya said: <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This chapter is devoted entirely to the teachings of Elisha b. ’Abuyah, a great scholar of the second century <small>C.E.</small> and teacher of R. Meir, who entered too deeply into theosophical speculations and eventually abandoned Judaism. Thereafter he was always referred to as <i>’Aḥer</i>, ‘That other one’; cf. Ḥag. 15a (Sonc. ed., pp. 93ff). For a recent and learned study of this ‘outcast’ scholar, cf. Travers Herford in <i>Essays Presented to J. H. Hertz</i>, 1944, pp. 215-225.</i>A man who has good deeds to his credit and has also studied much Torah, to what is he like? To one who builds [a structure and lays] stones below [for the foundation] and bricks above, so that however much water may collect at the side it will not wash it away.
But a man who has no good deeds to his credit, though he has studied Torah, to what is he like? To one who builds [a structure and lays] bricks first [for the foundation] and then stones above, so that even if only a little water collects it at once undermines it.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The study of the Torah can become a permanent blessing only if it is founded on the good life. This is the theme of all the illustrations quoted.</i>
He used to say: A man who has good deeds to his credit and has also studied Torah, to what is he like? To a coating of lime spread on stone,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">So according to the MSS. and GRA. V. reads ‘bricks’, an obvious error since the contrast is between lime spread on stone and lime spread on bricks, the former enduring whilst the latter flakes off quickly.</i> so that however much rain falls on it, it will not wash it away. But a man who has no good deeds to his credit, though he has studied much Torah, is like a coating of lime spread on bricks, so that even if the lightest rain falls on it, it will immediately dissolve and fall away.
He used to say: A man who has good deeds to his credit and has also studied much Torah is like a cup which has a square base<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Heb. noun is the Greek for ‘a stone’, here a solid base with four polished sides (cf. below XXVIII, 9, p. 137). Since the cup has a wide four-square base, even if it be shaken and pushed, it will retain its balance and not lose its contents.</i> to it, so that when it is set down, though it is turned on its side, all its contents will not be split. But a man who has no good deeds to his credit, though he has studied much Torah, is like a cup which has not a square base, so that when it is set down it will at once turn on its side and all its contents will be spilt.<br>He used to say: A man who has good deeds to his credit and has also studied much Torah is like a horse with a bridle;<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <span dir="rtl">כלינוס</span>, as found in the MSS. and ‘Aruk. V. reads ‘fine trappings’.</i> but he who has no good deeds to his credit, though he has studied much Torah, is like a horse without a bridle,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Here, too, reading <i>kalinos</i> in accordance with the authorities quoted in the preceding note. V. has ‘a bridle to curb it’.</i> and as soon as a man attempts to mount it he is thrown headlong.<br>He used to say: He who learns Torah in his youth, its words are absorbed in his blood and issue from his mouth with clarity; but he who learns Torah only in his old age, its words are not absorbed in his blood and do not issue from his mouth with clarity. Accordingly the proverb runs, ‘If thou hadst no delight in them in thy youth, how canst thou attain them in thy old age?’
He used to say: The words of the Torah are hard to acquire as vessels of gold, and are easily destroyed as glassware; as it is stated, <i>Gold and glass cannot equal it</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Job 28, 17.</i> A comparison is drawn between gold and glass: a golden vessel can be repaired when it is broken, but vessels of glass cannot be repaired when broken unless they are reduced to their original element.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. melted by fire and then reshaped.</i> And how am I to interpret, <i>Neither shall the exchange thereof be vessels of fine gold?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> It teaches that he who toils in [the words of the Torah] and fulfils them, his face shines like fine gold; but he who toils in them and does not fulfil them, his face is dark like glass.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning is either opaque glass which is dull, or glass which quickly darkens.</i>
He used to say: A man may study the Torah for twenty years and forget it in two years. How can this be? If he sits idle for six months and does not revise his studies, he will in consequence pronounce the unclean to be clean and the clean to be unclean. [If he continues idle for] twelve months without revision, he will then confuse [the names of] the Sages;<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Attributing a teaching to the wrong authority.</i> if for eighteen months without revision, he will forget the opening words of the chapters; and if twenty-four months without revision, he will forget even the titles [27<i>a</i>] of the Tractates.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is the more logical order and is supported by MSS., cf. Schechter <i>ad loc.</i> V. reverses the order, putting ‘the titles of the Tractates’ before ‘the opening words of the chapters’.</i> And so, having pronounced the unclean to be clean and the clean to be unclean, having also confused [the names of] the Sages, and forgotten the chapter headings and even the titles of the Tractates, he will eventually sit in silence. Of him Solomon said, <i>I went by the field of the slothful, and by the vineyard of the man void of understanding; and, lo, it was all grown over with thistles, the face thereof was covered with nettles, and the stone wall thereof was broken down</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 24, 30f.</i> When the wall of a vineyard collapses the entire vineyard is soon destroyed.
He used to say: He who causes another to perform a commandment is regarded by Scripture as if he had performed it himself…. <sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">There seems to be a lacuna here, because the parable which follows has no relation to the preceding statement. GRA inserts here the teaching of R. Dostai b. Yannai in Aboth 3:10 (Sonc. ed., III, 8, p. 32): ‘Whosoever forgets one word of his study, him Scripture regards as though he was guilty against his soul.’ This dictum is well illustrated by the parable. Cf. Men. 99b (Sonc. ed., p. 607)</i>This may be illustrated by a parable. To what can it be compared? To a human king who caught a bird and gave it to one of his servants saying, ‘Be careful with the bird. If you guard it, well and good; but if not I will take your life for it’. So, too, did the Holy One, blessed be He, say to Israel, ‘I have entrusted the words of the Torah to you. If you guard them, well and good; but if not I will take your lives for them’. For it is stated, <i>Only take heed to thyself, and keep thy soul diligently, lest thou forget the things which thine eyes saw</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Deut. 4, 9. In Aboth <i>loc. cit.</i> this verse is quoted in support of R. Dostai’s teaching mentioned in the preceding note.</i> [And it is also stated,] <i>For it is no vain thing for you, because it is your life</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXII, 47.</i>

Chapter 25

Ben ‘Azzai said: He whose mind is at ease because of his learning,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The knowledge which he acquired did not make him overbearing, but confirms him in his faith and helps him to live the good life. Cf. Tosif. Ber. III, 5.</i> it is a good omen for him; and he whose mind is ill at ease because of his learning, it is a bad omen for him. He whose mind is at ease with his inclination,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. his desires are not in constant conflict within him. A variant reading has ‘his Creator’ for ‘his inclination’.</i> it is a good omen for him; and he whose mind is ill at ease with his inclination, it is a bad omen for him. Whoever holds the good opinion of the Sages at the hour of his death, it is a good omen for him; but whoever does not hold the good opinion of the Sages, it is a bad omen for him. Whoever [dies] with his face directed upwards<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Indicating that he is prepared to meet his Maker with a clear conscience. Cf. on this passage, Keth. 103b (Sonc. ed., p. 661).</i> it is a good omen; if it is turned downwards it is a bad omen. If his eyes look straight towards the people [about him] it is a good omen; if they do not look towards the people [about him] it is a bad omen. Whoever [dies] with a cheerful countenance it is a good omen; if with a frowning countenance it is a bad omen.<br>When Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai was about to depart from this world,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Ber. 28b (Sonc. ed., p. 173).</i> he wept aloud. His disciples said to him, ‘Master! Exalted pillar! Light of the world! Mighty hammer! Why do you weep?’ He replied, ‘Is it into the presence of a human king that I am going? If he be angry with me his anger endures only in this world; if he imprison me the imprisonment endures only in this world; and if he kill me my death is only in this world. Moreover, him I can appease with words and bribe with money. Behold, I am going into the Presence of the King of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He. If He be angry with me His anger endures in this world and in the world to come; and Him I cannot appease with words or bribe with money. Furthermore, before me lie two ways, one leading to the Garden of Eden and the other to Gehinnom, and I know not whether I will be condemned to Gehinnom or conducted to the Garden of Eden; and concerning this<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The day of his death.</i> Scripture declares, <i>All they that go down to the dust shall kneel before Him</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 22, 30. This verse is interpreted in Midrash Rabbah, Numbers, XIV, 22 (Sonc. ed., p. 640), as indicating that at death all are led into the Presence of God.</i> Of Moses Scripture states, <i>And he expired, and died, and was gathered to his people</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This verse is not found of Moses, but only of Abraham (Gen. 25, 8) and Isaac (<i>ibid.</i> XXXV, 29). The text is in disorder, and the verses which follow have but a slender connection with the theme of the passage (cf. GRA and Schechter’s notes <i>ad loc.</i>). The verse in the text suggests that the righteous are ‘gathered’ to God and are shown the reward which has been stored up for them. Cf. Midrash Rabbah, Gen. 62, 2 (Sonc. ed., p. 549).</i> And it further states, <i>And I will take away My hand</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 33, 23. This verse also suggests that at the hour of death God ‘takes away His hand’ and reveals their reward to the righteous.</i> It also states, <i>And He spread it before me, and it was written on its face and on its back</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 2, 10, E.V., <i>written within and without</i>. The scroll which was spread before the prophet apparently contained the doctrine of retribution; cf. ‘Erub. 21a (Sonc. ed., p. 147).</i>—<i>face</i> refers to this world and <i>back</i> to the world to come. According to another interpretation <i>face</i> refers to the suffering of the righteous on earth and the prosperity of the wicked in this world, and <i>back</i> to the reward of the righteous in the hereafter and the punishment of the wicked in Gehinnom. <i>And there was written therein lamentations, and sweet music, and woe</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i>, E.V., <i>lamentations, and moaning, and woe</i>.</i>—<i>lamentations</i> [<i>ḳinim</i>] refers to the punishment of the wicked<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel passage in ‘Erub. <i>loc. cit.</i>, as well as in Rashi’s commentary on the verse of Ezek., <i>lamentations</i> refers to the sufferings of the righteous on earth.</i> in this world, as it is stated, <i>This is the lamentation</i> [<i>ḳinah</i>] <i>which shall be chanted; the daughters of the nations shall chant it</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXII, 16, E.V., <i>lamentation wherewith they shall lament … shall lament therewith</i>. This verse proves that the term <i>ḳinah</i>, the singular of <i>ḳinim</i>, signifies a dirge of misery and suffering.</i> <i>Sweet music</i> [<i>hegeh</i>] refers to the reward of the righteous in the hereafter, as it is stated, <i>With an instrument of ten strings, and with the psaltery; with sweet music</i> [<i>higgayon</i>] <i>upon the harp</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 92, 4, E.V., <i>with a solemn sound</i>. The words <i>hegeh</i> and <i>higgayon</i> are related in meaning, suggesting melodious song.</i> <i>Woe</i> [<i>he</i>] refers to the punishment of the wicked in the world to come, as it is stated, <i>Calamity</i> [<i>howah</i>] <i>shall come upon calamity, and rumour shall be upon rumour</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Ezek. 7, 26. The words <span dir="rtl">הי</span> and <span dir="rtl">הוה</span> are here brought together, each signifying ‘sorrow, woe’.</i><br>[In his last utterance] he<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">viz. of Rabban Joḥanan b. Zakkai.</i> said, ‘Remove all vessels from the house that they be not defiled, and prepare a throne for Hezekiah king of Judah’.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Who will accompany him into the next world. Rabban Joḥanan, like king Hezekiah, had acted zealously for the spread of Torah-study and the observance of the commandments; cf. Sanh. 94b (Sonc. ed., p. 637).</i>
He used to say: He who dies in a tranquil frame of mind it is a good omen for him;<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Because he is able to put his affairs in order with calmness and deliberation.</i> if in a state of delirium it is a bad omen for him. [If he dies] while speaking it is a good omen; if while silent it is a bad omen. If while conversing about the Torah it is a good omen, about business affairs it is a bad omen; if while engaged upon a religious act it is a good omen, upon a worthless pursuit it is a bad omen. [If he dies] in the midst of a joyous atmosphere it is a good omen, amidst sadness it is a bad omen; [if he dies] smiling it is a good omen, if weeping it is a bad omen. Should a man die on the Sabbath-eve it is a good omen,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">He will enter immediately into rest.</i> if at the termination of the Sabbath it is a bad omen. If he dies on the eve of the Day of Atonement it is a bad omen,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He dies with his sins unexpiated.</i> if at the termination of the Day of Atonement it is a good omen.
The day when R. Eliezer fell ill was, according to tradition, the Sabbath-eve. R. ‘Aḳiba and his colleagues came to visit him, and he was sitting in his canopied four-poster<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">So according to a variant reading and the parallel passage in Sanh. 68a (Sonc. ed., p. 461). V. reads: ‘he was sleeping in his room’.</i> while they waited in an anteroom. His son Hyrḳanos went in to remove his father’s <i>tefillin</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Which are not worn on the Sabbath.</i> but he would not allow him to do so. Hyrḳanos came out weeping and said to the Sages, ‘My masters, it seems that my father’s mind is deranged’. [Thereupon R. Eliezer] called out, ‘My son, it is not my mind that is deranged but yours, because you put off kindling the Sabbath lamp for which you incur the penalty of death at the hand<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">To light the Sabbath lamp after the Sabbath had begun constitutes a profanation of the holy day involving the death penalty.</i> of Heaven and occupied yourself with the <i>tefillin</i> for which you incur only the penalty of transgressing a Rabbinic precept concerning the Sabbath rest’.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Wearing the <i>tefillin</i> on the Sabbath is comparable to the wearing of an adornment, which is forbidden only by Rabbinic law.</i> When the Sages perceived that his mind was clear, they entered, sat at a distance of four cubits away from him,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer had been placed under the ban of excommunication (cf. B.M. 59b, Sonc. ed., p. 353) and all intercourse with him by his colleagues was carried out at a distance of four cubits.</i> and enquired of him, ‘Master, if a round cushion, a ball, a shoe-last, an amulet or a phylactery were torn, can they contract uncleanness?’<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">On the questions and the ritualistic principles involved, cf. above XIX, 4 (p. 108, n. 6).</i> He replied, ‘They can, and [if they are unclean] they may be immersed as they are. Be careful about these laws, because they are important laws which were communicated to Moses at Sinai’. They continued to question him on matters concerning cleanness and uncleanness and the regulations of ritual immersion. They said to him, ‘Master, what is the law in this case?’ and he answered, ‘It is unclean’. ‘And what is the law in that case?’ and he answered, ‘It is clean’. Throughout he pronounced the unclean to be unclean and the clean to be clean. After that R. Eliezer said to the Sages, ‘I am concerned about the disciples of the present generation lest they be punished with death at the hand of Heaven’. They asked him, ‘Master, what is the reason?’ He replied, ‘Because they did not come and attend upon me’.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">On account of the ban imposed upon him, the scholars had not sought his instruction.</i> He then said to ‘Aḳiba b. Joseph, ‘ ‘Aḳiba, why did you not come and attend upon me?’ He replied, ‘Master, I had no leisure’. He retorted, ‘I wonder whether you will die a natural death’. Some say that R. Eliezer did not make that rejoinder, but as R. Eliezer spoke to his disciples in this manner, R. ‘Aḳiba at once felt his blood melt within him and exclaimed, ‘Master, what will my death be?’ and he replied, ‘Yours will be the most cruel of all’.<br>R. ‘Aḳiba then approached, sat down before him and said, ‘Master teach me now’. He began and taught him three hundred <i>halakoth</i> on the subject of ‘the bright spot’.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A symptom, as well as a type, of leprosy; cf. Lev. 13.</i> At that time R. Eliezer raised his two arms and, laying them upon his breast, exclaimed, ‘Alas for these two arms of mine, [the equal of] two scrolls of the Torah, which must leave this world! For though all the seas were ink, all reeds quills and all men scribes, they could not set down all the Scripture which I read, all the Mishnah which I studied, and all that I had gained by attending upon the scholars at their sessions. And I took from my teachers no more than a man takes of the sea when he dips his finger in it; and my disciples took no more from me than a paint-stick takes of the paint in the tube.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The text is evidently faulty and the translation follows the emendation of GRA which accords with the parallel passage in Sanh. 68a (Sonc. ed., p. 462). V is lit., ‘I withheld not of all that my eyes desired only to the extent of a man dipping his finger in the sea, and I no more lost of my learning than a paint-stick removes paint from a tube’.</i> Moreover, I can recite three hundred (another version: three thousand) <i>halakoth</i> on the prohibition, <i>Thou shalt not suffer a sorceress to live</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 22, 17.</i> yet no one ever questioned me about them except ‘Aḳiba b. Joseph. For on one occasion he said to me, “Master, teach me how cucumbers are planted”<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The reference to the planting and uprooting of cucumbers has a magical connotation; cf. Sanh. <i>loc. cit.</i> V adds in brackets, ‘and how they are uprooted’.</i>. So I pronounced a word and the entire field [about us] was filled with cucumbers. He then said to me, “You have taught me how they are planted, now teach me how to uproot them”. I again pronounced a word and all the cucumbers were gathered in one place’.<br>R. Eleazar b. Azariah enquired of him, ‘Master, what is the law if the shoe is still on the shoemaker’s last?’<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since the shoe is still on the last, it could be regarded as unfinished, and consequently should not contract defilement.</i> He answered, ‘It is clean’. He made reply [to many questions] declaring the unclean to be unclean and the clean to be clean until with the word ‘clean’ [on his lips] his soul departed. At once R. Eleazar b. Azariah rent his clothes and wept. He went out to the Sages and said, ‘Come, my masters, and behold R. Eliezer who is clean for the world to come, because his soul departed with the word “clean” [upon his lips]’. After the Sabbath, R. ‘Aḳiba set out and encountered [his master’s] bier as it proceeded from Caesarea to Lydda. Forthwith he rent his garments and plucked his hair, and [beat his breast until] his blood flowed to the ground. He wept and cried, ‘Woe is me! I grieve for you, my master! Woe is me! I mourn for you, my teacher who left his whole generation orphaned!’ As they stood in a row<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Which was formed around the bier.</i> he began his funeral oration and said, ‘<i>My father, my father, the chariots of Israel and the horsemen thereof</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">2 Kings 2, 12.</i> I have many coins, but there is no money-changer to exchange them’.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Many difficult questions of law will now be left unanswered because none but R. Eliezer, with his vast accumulation of traditional rulings, could have solved them.</i> [27<i>b</i>]
B<small>EN</small> ‘A<small>ZZAI SAID: RUN TO FULFIL EVEN A LIGHT PRECEPT</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">[This passage is discussed in Büchler, <i>Studies in Sin and Atonement</i>, pp. 311f.]</i> He used to say: If you have performed a precept and have no regrets with regard to it, it will in the end draw many precepts in its train. And if one has committed a transgression and has no regrets about it, it will in the end draw many transgressions in its train; <small>FOR ONE PRECEPT LEADS TO ANOTHER PRECEPT, AND ONE TRANSGRESSION TO ANOTHER TRANSGRESSION; FOR THE REWARD OF A PRECEPT IS ANOTHER PRECEPT, AND THE REWARD OF A TRANSGRESSION IS ANOTHER TRANSGRESSION</small>.<br>He used to say: Desist<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">From committing a transgression (Emden). Alternatively: ‘Allocate charity of your own accord’ (B.Y.), or ‘Keep silent of your own accord and wait not for others to silence you’ (Meiri).</i> of your own accord and you will reap the reward for desisting; but let not others make you desist, because they will then reap the reward for making you desist.<br>He used to say: Step down from your place two or three degrees and sit there, because it is better for you to be told to ascend than to be told to descend; as it is stated, <i>For better is it that it be said unto thee: Come up hither, than that thou shouldest be put lower in the presence of the prince, whom thine eyes have seen</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 25, 7.</i>
There are three whose life is not worth living, and they are: He who must look to the table of another [for sustenance], he who lives in an attic, and whoever is dominated by his wife. Others add: he whose body is afflicted with sufferings.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Beẓah 32b (Sonc. ed., p. 165). [Cf. also Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i>, I, p. 414, n. 4.]</i><br>He used to say: It is easier to rule over the whole world than to sit and teach<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘to study in the presence of’.</i> people who are wrapped in linen robes.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A term for hypocrites, who wrap themselves in a mantle of piety but inwardly are of evil mind. Cf. Midrash Tehillim on XVIII, 44. [Buber, <i>ad loc.</i>, defines the phrase as ‘judges’. Bacher, <i>op. cit.</i>, n. 1, holds the meaning to be doubtful.
