He quotes Midrash Rabbah, Ecclesiastes, to I, 7 (Sonc. ed., p. 24) where it refers to two Sages who were humble men.]</i>

Chapter 26

R. ‘Aḳiba said: A fence<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The ‘fence’ is here taken in the sense of a safeguard or an aid to. With this paragraph, cf. Aboth 3:17.</i> to honour is [the avoidance of] jesting,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">That this is the true meaning of the saying is proved by the wording in Schechter’s second version of ARN XXXIII (beg.), ‘a fence to honour is not to jest’. Cf. Finkelstein’s translation of this maxim in his <i>Akiba: Scholar, Saint and Martyr</i>, p. 111, ‘Laughter protects one’s honour’.</i> a fence to wisdom is silence, a fence to vows is self-restraint,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">By practising abstinence the making of vows becomes unnecessary.</i> a fence to purity is holiness, a fence to humility is the fear of sin.
He used to say: Come not among scoffers lest you learn of their ways. Eat not bread with a priest who is an <i>‘am ha-’areẓ</i> lest you trespass in hallowed things. Be not free with vows lest you trespass in oaths.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The breaking of an oath is a more serious matter than the breaking of a vow. Cf. Ned. 20a (Sonc. ed., p. 56) and R. Nissim <i>ad loc.</i></i> Be not in the habit of feasting sumptuously lest in the end you will have to eat the bread of charity.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <i>haḳḳuppah</i>, lit. ‘[the charity] chest’ which is found as a variant. The word in the text <i>haḳḳanim</i>, ‘the reeds’, makes no sense. The emendation of GRA is attractive, <i>haẓẓiḳnin</i>, ‘the scrapings of the pot’.</i> Bring not yourself to a state of doubt lest you come up against a certain [transgression]. Do not leave the land [of Israel] lest you serve idols; for so David said, <i>For they have driven me out this day that I should not cleave unto the inheritance of the Lord, saying; Go, serve other gods</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 26, 19.</i> Can it, however, enter your mind that king David would serve idols? But this is what David meant: Whosoever leaves the land of Israel and goes abroad, Scripture regards him as an idolater.<br>He used to say: Whoever is buried in other lands is considered as though he were buried in Babylon;<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Death being an expiation, when a person dies and is interred, he is brought one degree closer to the Divine Presence which manifests its greatest concentration and brilliance in the Throne of Glory which is directly above the altar in Jerusalem. The degrees in ascending scale which lead to the Divine Glory are: other lands, Babylon, the land of Israel, Jerusalem (this is omitted in the text), the altar, and the Throne of Glory. Cf. Kel. I, 6ff (Sonc. ed., pp. 10ff).</i> whoever is buried in Babylon is as though he were buried in the land of Israel; whoever is buried in the land of Israel is as though he were buried beneath the altar, because all the land of Israel is fit [to be the site of] the altar; and whoever is buried beneath the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is a strange statement. GRA substitutes ‘in Jerusalem’ for ‘beneath the altar’. Cf. Keth. 111a (Sonc. ed., p. 714).</i> is as though he were buried beneath the Throne of Glory; as it is stated, <i>The throne of glory, on high from the beginning, is the place of our sanctuary</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 17, 12. E.V., <i>Thou throne … thou place of our sanctuary</i>.</i>
<small>HE USED TO SAY: AN IGNORANT MAN CANNOT BE PIOUS, THE BASHFUL MAN CANNOT LEARN, AND THE IMPATIENT MAN CANNOT TEACH</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A saying ascribed to Hillel in Aboth 2:10.</i> He used to say: Why do disciples of the Sages [often] die young? It is not because they are immoral or because they rob; but because they interrupt their study of the Torah to indulge in idle talk. Moreover, [when returning to their studies] they do not begin where they left off.
R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: The Israelites who live outside the land of Israel serve idols in all innocence.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘in purity’. Cf. ‘A.Z. 8a (Sonc. ed., p. 38). [The word for ‘in purity’ is omitted in the Tosiftha to ‘A.Z. IV, 6, and Büchler, <i>Studies in Sin and Atonement</i>, p. 319, n. 2, explains it as ‘without evil intention’.</i> How is this? For example, a Gentile prepares a banquet for his son and invites all the Jews of his town to it; although they eat and drink [of their own], and their own attendant waits on them and serves them with drink, Scripture accounts it to them as though they had eaten of the sacrifices of the dead; as it is stated, <i>And they call thee and thou eat of their sacrifice</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 34, 15.</i><br>R. E<small>LIEZER OF</small> M<small>ODIN SAID: IF A MAN PROFANES THE SABBATHS</small>,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In Aboth 3:15: ‘the hallowed things’.</i> <small>SLIGHTS THE FESTIVALS, MAKES VOID THE COVENANT IN THE FLESH</small>,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. disregards the rite of circumcision. Aboth, <i>loc. cit.</i>, reads, ‘the covenant of Abraham our father … the Torah contrary to the <i>halakah’</i>. [Some Jewish scholars have detected here a polemic against the early Christians cf. Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i> I, p. 190, n. 1.]</i> <small>OR MISINTERPRETS THE TORAH, EVEN THOUGH HE POSSESSES TORAH AND GOOD DEEDS, HE HAS NO SHARE IN THE WORLD TO COME</small>.<br>R. ‘Aḳiba said: Whoever marries a wife who is unsuited to him transgresses five commandments, namely: <i>Thou shalt not take vengeance;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 19, 18.</i> <i>Thou shalt not bear a grudge;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> <i>Thou shalt not hate thy brother in thy heart;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> <i>Thou shalt love thy neighbour as thyself;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> and <i>That thy brother may live with thee</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXV, 36.</i>—because since he hates her, he wishes her dead, and, in consequence, he abstains from fulfilling the duty to be fruitful and multiply in the world.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 1, 28.</i>
He used to say: If a man eats food which is unsuitable for his constitution,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A variant reading is: ‘over which no blessing should be recited’. Cf. R. Judah’s opinion in Ber. VI, 3 (Sonc. ed., p. 251).</i> he transgresses three commandments: he disgraces himself, he disgraces his food, and he recites a benediction to no purpose.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘that is not in accordance with the regulation’. Since the food is harmful to health, no blessing is to be recited, and one who does so pronounces the name of God in vain.</i>
R. Judah b. Ilai said: If a man died and left a son, and that son, who had learnt no Torah from his father, now seeks to learn Torah from others, he is obviously insincere in his quest.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since he missed the opportunity of being instructed by his own father. The passage, however, is obscure (cf. the commentaries <i>ad loc.</i>).</i><br>R. Eleazar ha-Ḳappar said: Be not like the top lintel of a doorway which is out of reach of a man’s hand, nor like the top of the door-frame which can injure the face, nor like the middle of the door-frame which can hurt the legs; but be like the threshold below upon which all tread, and when the entire building is demolished it remains in its place.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">A maxim stressing the importance of humility with its enduring virtue, and the harmfulness of pride and arrogance in whatever degree.</i>

Chapter 27

R. J<small>OSE SAID: HE WHO HONOURS THE TORAH IS HIMSELF HONOURED BY MANKIND</small>, as it is stated, <i>For them that honour Me I will honour, and they that despise Me shall be lightly esteemed</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1 Sam. 2, 30. ‘Me’ is here identified with the Torah, a doctrine frequent in the Zohar and other mystical literature.</i><br>Another interpretation<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Omitted in V, but inserted by GRA.</i> of <i>For them that honour Me I will honour:</i> this refers to Pharaoh king of Egypt who paid honour to Him Who spoke and the world came into being by going out at the head of his council of ministers. His servants said to him, ‘Usually kings go out behind their ministers, but you go out at their head!’ He replied, ‘Am I going out to meet a [28<i>a</i>] king of flesh and blood? No, I go to meet the Presence of the King of kings, the Holy One, blessed be He’. Therefore the Holy One, blessed be He, paid Pharaoh honour, and He Himself meted out retribution to him, as it is stated, <i>I have compared thee, O my love, to a steed in Pharaoh’s chariots</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Cant. 1, 9. This verse in no wise proves the point that God Himself inflicted punishment upon Pharaoh. GRA substitutes for this verse Hab. 3, 15, quoted in the preceding paragraph. This latter verse suggests that God set forth on His horses in order to requite Pharaoh; cf. Sanh. 94b (Sonc. ed., p. 634).</i>
R. Pappias said: The congregation of Israel praised the horses and chariots of Pharaoh, as it is stated, <i>Thou hast trodden the sea with Thy horses</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Hab. <i>ibid.</i> This verse too offers no proof for R. Pappias’ statement, and it has been suggested that Cant. 1, 9 should be read here as indicating the high esteem in which Pharaoh’s steeds were held, since God likens His love for Israel to a highly prized mare among Pharaoh’s chariots. The text of the passage is evidently in disorder and Schechter gives it up as hopeless. [Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i> I, p. 319, n. 3, thinks that the appropriate quotation is Hab. 3, 8.]</i><br>R. Joshua b. Ḳorḥah said: When Pharaoh came to the Red Sea he was riding on a stallion, and the Holy One, blessed be He, appeared to him on a mare,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">To lure Pharaoh’s stallion to follow headlong into the sea; cf. Midrash Rabbah, Song of Songs, on I, 9 (Sonc. ed., p. 71).</i> as it is stated, <i>To a steed in Pharaoh’s chariots</i>. But did He not ride upon a cherub, as it is stated, <i>And He rode upon a cherub, and did fly; yea, He did swoop down upon the wings of the wind?</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 18, 11.</i> The cherub appeared to the steeds of Pharaoh in the form of a mare, and they followed her into the sea.<br><i>And they that despise Me shall be lightly esteemed:</i> this refers to Sennacherib who behaved contemptuously before Him Who spoke and the world came into being, as it is stated, <i>By thy servants hast thou taunted the Lord, and hast said: With the multitude of my chariots am I come up to the height of the mountains, to the innermost parts of Lebanon; and I have cut down the tall cedars thereof, and the choice cypress-trees thereof; and I have entered into his farthest height, the forest of his fruitful field. I have digged and drunk water, and with the sole of my feet have I dried up all the rivers of Egypt</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 37, 24f.</i> Therefore the Holy One, blessed be He, meted out punishment to him by an angel who shaved his head and beard,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. 96a (Sonc. ed., p. 646).</i> so that he returned shamefacedly to his land.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the MSS. quoted in Schechter’s edition the words ‘who shaved … land’ are omitted, and in their place is quoted the appropriate verse from Is. 37, 36, <i>And the angel of the Lord went forth and smote in the camp of the Assyrians</i>, etc.</i><br>R. I<small>SHMAEL THE SON OF</small> R. J<small>OḤANAN B.</small> B<small>EROKA SAID: HE WHO LEARNS IN ORDER TO TEACH</small>, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">From Aboth 4:6 (Sonc. ed., IV, 5, p. 46).</i> He used to say: It is not incumbent upon you to undertake to finish the whole of the Torah, yet you are not free to keep aloof from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">An adaptation of R. Ṭarfon’s dictum in Aboth 2:21 (Sonc. ed., II, 16, p. 24).</i> But he who greatly amasses [knowledge of the Torah] accumulates abundant reward.<br>R. E<small>LEAZAR B.</small> Ḥ<small>ISMA SAID: THE LAWS CONCERNING BIRD-OFFERINGS AND THE PURIFICATION OF WOMEN ARE THE ESSENTIAL ORDINANCES</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">From Aboth 3:23 (Sonc. ed., III, 18, p. 41).</i> R. Joḥanan b. Nuri said: The <i>halakoth</i>, the laws of ritual purity and of menstrual women and bird-offerings are the essentials of the Torah. He used to say: The arranging of the table<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">To set out the Shewbread (cf. Lev. 24, 5ff). According to GRA, the provision of a large and lavish table to feed the scholars and the needy.</i> and the establishment of a <i>Beth Din</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">To administer justice and establish law and order in the community.</i> as well as its upkeep bring welfare to the world.
R. Joḥanan b. Dehabai said: If a man says, ‘This law is not acceptable’,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Reading <span dir="rtl">נראית</span> for <span dir="rtl">נבראת</span>. The statement is a denial of the authenticity and Divine origin of the Torah; cf. Sanh. 99a (Sonc. ed., pp. 671f).</i> he has no share in the world to come. He used to say: Keep not aloof from a pursuit which is limitless or from a task which is endless.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. the study of the Torah. This is an exposition of R. Ṭarfon’s dictum in Aboth 2:21 (Sonc. ed., II, 16, p. 24). The author may well be R. Ṭarfon himself.</i> [This may be illustrated by] a parable. To what is the matter like? To a man who was [hired] to carry away the waters from the sea and pour them out on the dry land. The sea did not grow less nor was the dry land filled up; so the man became dispirited. Whereupon people said to him, ‘You fool! why are you dispirited? Continue to draw your pay of one golden <i>dinar</i> daily’.
R. E<small>LIEZER B.</small> S<small>HAMMUA‘ SAID: LET THE HONOUR OF YOUR DISCIPLE BE AS DEAR TO YOU AS YOUR OWN, AND THE HONOUR OF YOUR COLLEAGUE AS THE REVERENCE FOR YOUR TEACHER, AND THE REVERENCE FOR YOUR TEACHER AS THE REVERENCE OF HEAVEN</small>. Whence do we know that the honour of a disciple should be as dear as one’s own honour?<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As corrected by the Commentators. V. reads, ‘It teaches that … as the honour of his colleague’.</i> All may derive it from Moses our teacher who said to Joshua, <i>Choose for us men</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 17, 9. E.V., <i>choose us out men</i>.</i> It is not stated, ‘Choose for me’ but <i>Choose for us</i>, proving that he regarded Joshua as an equal, although he was the master and Joshua his disciple.<br>Whence do we know that the honour of a colleague should be as dear as [the reverence for] one’s teacher? As it is stated, <i>And Aaron said unto Moses: Oh my lord</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 12, 11.</i> But was not Moses his younger brother? Yet he acknowledged Moses as his master.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">By addressing him as <i>my lord</i>.</i><br>And whence do we know that the reverence<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The text has ‘honour’, but the intent is ‘reverence’ as set out at the beginning of the passage.</i> for a teacher should be as dear to one as the reverence of Heaven? As it is stated, <i>And Joshua the son of Nun, the minister of Moses from his youth up, answered and said: My lord Moses, shut them in</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XI, 28.</i> He then considered his teacher’s honour as equal to that of the Divine.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Joshua regarded disobedience to his teacher Moses as disobedience to the Divine command.</i><br>For<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This word is deleted by GRA as the passage has no connection with what has gone before; but cf. the commentary of B.Y.</i> at first they said, ‘Corn [is plentiful] in Judah, straw in Galilee, and chaff beyond the Jordan’. Later, however, they said, ‘In Judah there is no corn [only straw], in Galilee there is no straw but only chaff, and beyond the Jordan there is neither the one nor the other’.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The passage describes in metaphor the state of learning which prevailed in Judah and the provinces, and proceeds to contrast the attainments of the early generations with the later, showing how the standard had deteriorated in the course of the years. Cf. ‘Erub. 53a (Sonc. ed., pp. 370ff). Schechter considers this passage out of place here and suggests its transference to XXVIII, 2.</i>

Chapter 28

R. Nathan said: There is no love like the love for the Torah, no wisdom like the wisdom of the land of Israel,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">So MSS. and GRA; V, ‘worldly affairs’.</i> no beauty like the beauty of Jerusalem, no wealth like the wealth of Media, no might like the might of Persia, no immorality like that of the Arabs, no arrogance like the arrogance of Elam, no hypocrisy like the hypocrisy of Babylon, as it is stated, <i>And he said unto me: To build her a house in the land of Shinar</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Zech. 5, 11.</i> and no magic like the magic of Egypt.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">On this passage, cf. Ḳid. 49b (Sonc. ed., pp. 248f).</i>
R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: If a sage who has been living in the land of Israel leaves it for a foreign country, he has lost merit;<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. B.B. 158b (Sonc. ed., p. 691): ‘The climate of the land of Israel makes one wise’.</i> and he who remains there is more worthy than he. But although he has lost merit, he is still far more worthy than the meritorious of other lands. [This may be illustrated by] a parable. To what can the matter be compared? To Indian iron<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Iron from India was highly prized for the making of armour. Cf. ‘A.Z. 16a (Sonc. ed., p. 79).</i> which has been exported to a land<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">So GRA. V. reads, ‘which has come from a land’.</i> beyond the sea; even when it has deteriorated it is still superior to the best of other lands.
R. Simeon b. Gamaliel said: Whoever makes peace in his house, Scripture ascribes it to him as though he made peace for every individual in Israel; but whoever brings jealousy and strife into his house, Scripture ascribes it to him as though he brought these among Israel; because each person is a king in his home, as it is stated, <i>That every man shall bear rule in his own house</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Esth. 1, 22.</i>
Rabban Gamaliel said: By four methods heathen States devour [their inhabitants]: by their taxes, bath-houses, theatres and annual levies.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The exorbitant taxes, and the life of ease, sport and luxury indulged in by the upper classes, contributed largely to the disintegration of the Roman Empire.</i>
He used to say: The words of the Torah are as difficult to acquire as fine woollen clothes, but are as easily lost as linen garments. Foolish and frivolous words are easily acquired, but are as hard to lose as a sack. Often a man buys a sack in the market for a <i>sela‘</i> and continues using it for four or five years.<br>R. Judah the Prince said: Whoever indulges in the pleasures of this world will be withheld from the pleasures of the world to come; but whoever denies himself the pleasures of this world will be granted the pleasures of the world to come.
He used to say: To what can the righteous who are unfortunate in this world be compared? To a chef who prepares a meal for himself; for though it cost him much effort, he prepares it for none other than himself; but to what can the wicked who are unfortunate in this world be compared? To a chef who prepared a meal for others; for though it cost him much effort, he has prepared nothing for himself but only for others.
He used to say: Let your private life be open to full view;<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. act in private as you would in public. The phrase used is equivalent to the more usual expression <i>toko kebaro</i>, ‘whose inside is as his outside’, i.e. sincere; cf. Ber. 28a (Sonc. ed., p. 168).</i> and what is not meant to be heard<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. a confidential matter.</i> tell not to your fellow.
H<small>ILLEL SAID: DO NOT SEPARATE YOURSELF FROM THE CONGREGATION</small>, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">From Aboth 2:5 (Sonc. ed., II, 4, p. 14).</i>
He used to say: The more one eats the more he discharges; the more flesh one puts on the more worms and maggots he breeds; the more good deeds [28<i>b</i>] one does the more peace he provides for his body.<br>R. Eleazar b. Shammua‘ said: There are three types of disciple: a cut stone, a corner-stone and a plinth. ‘A cut stone’—what does this describe? The disciple who has studied <i>Midrash</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Laws derived from the text of the Torah by exegesis.</i> only, so that whenever a scholar comes to consult him on a Midrashic subject he can enlighten him. Such a one is like a cut stone which has only one polished surface. ‘A corner-stone’—what does this describe? The disciple who has studied <i>Midrash</i> and <i>halakoth</i>, so that whenever a scholar comes to consult him either on a Midrashic or halakic subject he can enlighten him. Such a one is like a corner-stone which has two polished surfaces. ‘A plinth’—what does this describe? The disciple who has studied <i>Midrash, halakoth, ’aggadoth</i> and <i>tosephoth</i>, so that whenever a scholar comes to consult him on a subject in the <i>Midrash</i>, in the <i>halakoth</i>, in the <i>’aggadoth</i> or in the <i>tosephoth</i> he can enlighten him. Such a one is like a plinth which has four polished surfaces on its four sides.
R. Judah b. Ilai said: He who treats the words of the Torah as of primary importance and worldly affairs as secondary will himself be of primary importance in the world;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">V. prints ‘this’ with ‘world’ in brackets. MS. E. has ‘in the world to come’.</i> but he who treats worldly affairs as of primary importance and the words of the Torah as secondary will himself be of secondary importance in the world.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">V. prints ‘this’ with ‘world’ in brackets. MS. E. has ‘in the world to come’.</i> [This may be illustrated by] a parable. To what is the matter like? To a public roadway which runs between two paths, one formed of fire and the other of snow. If a man walks close to the path of fire he will be scorched by the heat, and if he walks close to the path of snow he will be frost-bitten; so what should he do? He should walk in the middle of the roadway and so protect himself from being scorched by the fire or bitten by the frost.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The view expounded by this parable is that of the <i>via media</i>, a wholesome combination of Torah-study and worldly affairs.</i>

Chapter 29

R. S<small>IMEON B.</small> E<small>LEAZAR SAID IN THE NAME OF</small> R. M<small>EIR: DO NOT APPEASE YOUR FELLOW IN THE TIME OF HIS ANGER; DO NOT COMFORT HIM IN THE TIME OF HIS MOURNING</small>;<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Aboth 4:23 (Sonc. ed., IV, 18, pp. 53f) where the text reads: ‘when his dead lies before him’. This is the meaning required here; at that hour silence is the greatest kindness. The phrase ‘do not enter … calamity’ is omitted.</i> <small>DO NOT QUESTION HIM IN THE HOUR OF HIS VOW; DO NOT ENTER HIS HOUSE IN THE DAY OF HIS CALAMITY; AND DO NOT STRIVE TO SEE HIM</small> [<small>IN THE HOUR OF HIS DISGRACE</small>].<br>He used to say:<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of GRA instead of V. ‘some say’.</i> If among your colleagues there are some who rebuke you and others who praise you, love him who rebukes you and hate him who praises you; because it is the one who rebukes you that leads you to the life of the world to come, while he who praises you drives you out of the world.<br>He used to say: Wherever a man goes his heart goes there too; wherever he stands his heart stands there too; and wherever he sits his actions confront him.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘words (or, actions) are settled before him’. The thought seems to be that it is wholly within the power of man to guide his heart on the course which he chooses for himself; and once the choice is made he cannot escape the consequences. [Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i> II, p. 427, n. 4, suggests the following interpretation based on the use of ‘heart’ for ‘mind’ in Hebrew psychology: When a man walks about his mind is active; when he stands still his mind is passive; when he sits his mind is settled and not agitated.]</i>
He used to say: Whoever is diligent in the study of Torah is afforded many opportunities for being diligent, and whoever is neglectful of the study of Torah is likewise afforded many causes for neglect; e.g. a lion, wolf, leopard, tiger, a snake or a band of troops or robbers will come and encircle him and inflict punishment upon him; as it is stated, <i>Verily there is a God that judgeth in the earth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 58, 12. God offers man the opportunity for the performance of good or evil, but he has the power to determine his step. Cf. Yoma 38b (Sonc. ed., p. 181): ‘If a man goes to defile himself, they open the door for him; and if he goes to purify himself, they assist him.</i>
Abba Saul b. Nanos said: There are four types of scholars: he who studied but did not teach others; he who taught others but did not teach himself; he who studied and taught others; and he who neither studied nor taught others. ‘He who studied but did not teach others’—how is this? A man learnt a chapter or two or three [of the Mishnah] but did not rehearse them with another and occupy himself with them so as not to forget them—he is one who studied but did not teach others. ‘He who taught others but did not teach himself’—how is this? A man learnt an Order<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">One of the six divisions of the Mishnah.</i> or two or three [of the Mishnah] several times and taught them to others, but he did not occupy himself with them so that he forgot them—he is one who taught others but did not teach himself. ‘He who studied and taught others’—how is this? A man learnt an Order or two or three [of the Mishnah] and rehearsed them with others, occupied himself with them so that he did not forget them, and both he and they grasped them firmly—he is one who studied and taught others. ‘He who neither studied nor taught others’—how is this? A man learnt an Order or two or three [of the Mishnah] several times but did not teach them to others or occupy himself with them so that he forgot them—he is one who neither studied nor taught others.<br>R. Ḥananya b. Jacob said: He who keeps awake at night by reason of Torah-study, it is a good omen for him; if by reason of general conversation,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">A variant reading is: ‘business affairs’.</i> it is a bad omen for him.<br>R. Jacob b. Ḥananya said: He who keeps awake at night and does not utter any words of Torah, for him it were better—and indeed he would deserve it—had his mother’s afterbirth been turned upon his face [and suffocated him], and he had not come forth into the atmosphere of the world or ever seen its light.
R. Eliezer ha-Ḳappar said: Whoever honours his fellow for the sake of gain<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">He flatters him in the hope of personal gain.</i> will in the end depart from him in shame; and whoever slights his fellow in connection with a religious cause<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He rebukes him for his shortcomings, in accordance with the precept in Lev. 19, 17.</i> will in the end depart from him in honour. Whence do we know that whoever honours his fellow for the sake of gain will in the end depart from him in shame? We find it so with the wicked Balaam who honoured Balak for the sake of gain, as it is stated, <i>And Balaam answered and said to the servants of Balak: If Balak would give me his house full of silver and gold</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 22, 18.</i> And whence do we know that he departed from him in shame? For it is stated, <i>Now flee thou to thy place … but, lo, the Lord hath kept thee back from honour</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXIV, 11.</i> Whence do we know that whoever slights his fellow in connection with a religious cause will in the end depart from him in honour? We find it so with Moses our teacher who slighted Pharaoh in connection with a religious cause, as it is stated, <i>And all these thy servants shall come down unto me, and bow down unto me, saying</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 11, 8. Moses slighted Pharaoh and his court by declaring that he would refuse to listen to their appeals and supplications.</i> But was Pharaoh standing on the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Implied in the verb <i>come down</i>.</i> and Moses on the ground? It was rather this that Moses said to Pharaoh, ‘Even if all your servants, who stand before you and prostrate themselves before you on your dais, were to stand and implore me, I would not hearken to them’. And whence do we know that he departed from him in honour? For it is stated, <i>And he called for Moses and Aaron by night</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XII, 31. Pharaoh thus honoured Moses by personally calling upon him with the appeal to depart.</i> They said to Pharaoh, ‘Are we thieves that we should leave [Egypt] by night? You must wait until the Holy One, blessed be He, will produce the seven clouds of glory for us, that we may go forth in them joyfully and with head uncovered,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">An expression meaning ‘defiantly, fearlessly’, equivalent to the Biblical idiom <i>with a high hand</i> (Ex. 14, 8).</i> as it is stated, <i>On the morrow after the Passover the children of Israel went out with a high hand</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Num. 33, 3.</i>
To enquire about the four different forms of atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">On this paragraph, cf. Yoma 86a (Sonc. ed., p. 426).</i> R. Matthia b. Ḥeresh paid a visit to R. Ishmael b. Eleazar ha-Ḳappar at Laodicea. He said to him, ‘Have you heard [a tradition] concerning the four different forms of atonement which R. Ishmael expounded?’ He replied, ‘I have heard [a tradition] and there are only three forms, and repentance is imperative with each. One verse states, <i>Return, ye backsliding children</i> (<i>saith the Lord</i>) <i>I will heal your backslidings</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Jer. 3, 22. <i>Saith the Lord</i> is not in the Biblical text.</i> A second verse states, <i>For on this day shall atonement be made for you, to cleanse you</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 16, 30.</i> A third verse states, <i>Then I will visit their transgression with the rod, and their iniquity with strokes</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 89, 33.</i> And a fourth verse states, <i>Surely this iniquity shall not be expiated by you till ye die</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 22, 14.</i> How are these verses [to be reconciled]? If a man transgressed a positive command and repented, before he stirs from his place he is forthwith forgiven; and concerning him it states, <i>Return, ye backsliding children</i>. If a man transgressed a negative command and repented, the repentance suspends [the punishment] and the Day of Atonement effects atonement; and concerning him it states, <i>For on this day shall atonement be made for you</i>. If a man transgressed a prohibition punishable by <i>kareth</i> or by death at the hands of the court and repented, the repentance and the Day of Atonement together suspend [the punishment] and sufferings in the course of the year will purge the sin;<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The text is in some disorder; the translation follows Schechter’s text and is required by the context.</i> and concerning him it states, <i>Then will I visit their transgression with the rod</i>. But if a man profaned the Name of Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">‘By sinning himself and causing others to sin’ (Rashi, Yoma <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> [and repented], there is no power in the repentance to suspend [punishment], or in sufferings to purge the sin, or in the Day of Atonement to effect atonement; but repentance [with the Day of Atonement and]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Inserted by GRA and required by the context.</i> with sufferings together suspend [the punishment], and death purges the sin; and concerning him it states, <i>Surely this iniquity shall not be expiated by you till ye die</i>.
Isi b. Judah said: Why do scholars [sometimes] die before their time? It is not because they are immoral or because they rob, but because they make themselves contemptible.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">By neglecting their appearance. Cf. XXVI, 3, p. 130.</i>
R. Isaac b. Phinehas said: Whoever is versed in <i>Midrash</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Here the word seems to be a synonym of <i>’aggadah</i>, the ethical and moral expositions of the Bible.</i>and not in <i>halakoth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Here the analytical reasoning and exegetical argument on the Biblical law.</i> has never experienced the taste of wisdom; and he who is versed in <i>halakah</i> and not in <i>Midrash</i> has never experienced the taste of the fear of sin.<br>He used to say: Whoever is versed in <i>Midrash</i> and not in <i>halakoth</i> is like a strong man without weapons; whoever is versed in <i>halakoth</i> and not in <i>Midrash</i> is like a weak man with a weapon; but whoever is versed in both is like a strong man with a weapon.<br>He used to say: Be careful to greet your fellow-man;<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In Aboth 4:20 (Sonc. ed., IV, 15, p. 52) the reading is ‘be beforehand in greeting all men’.</i> enter not into a quarrel nor be eager to see it; sit in the company of your fellows,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Or, your equals.</i> <small>AND BE RATHER A TAIL TO LIONS THAN A HEAD TO FOXES</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">From Aboth <i>loc. cit.</i></i>

Chapter 30

R. N<small>ATHAN</small><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Aboth 4:11 (Sonc. ed., IV, 9, pp. 48f) where the dictum is given in reverse order in the name of R. Jonathan. GRA emends to R. Jonathan b. Joseph, a disciple of R. ‘Aḳiba.</i> <small>B.</small> J<small>OSEPH SAID: WHOEVER NEGLECTS THE STUDY OF THE TORAH IN THE MIDST OF WEALTH SHALL IN THE END NEGLECT IT IN POVERTY; AND WHOEVER FULFILS THE WORDS OF THE TORAH IN POVERTY SHALL IN THE END FULFIL THEM IN THE MIDST OF WEALTH</small>. [29<i>a</i>]<br>He used to say: Comforting mourners, visiting the sick, and the practice of lovingkindness bring welfare into the world.
R. Meir said: Whoever is in doubt whether he has transgressed or not is regarded by Scripture [more gravely than]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">So according to B.Y. and required by the context.</i> if he transgressed of a certainty. How is this? If a man sinned [in error] and then became aware of his sin, he must bring a sin-offering of the value of one <i>sela</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The usual price of a sin-offering; cf. Ker. 10b (Sonc. ed., p. 81).</i> or a tenth of an ephah [as a meal-offering]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If the sinner is poor and unable to afford an animal offering.</i> of the value of one <i>pondion;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Cf., however, Ker. <i>loc. cit.</i>, where a tenth of an ephah of fine flour is priced at one <i>peruṭah</i> (i.e. a sixteenth of a <i>pondion</i>).</i> whereas if he was in doubt whether he had sinned or not, he must bring a trespass-offering with the ‘added fifth’,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If his doubt referred to an inadvertent misappropriation of Temple property (cf. Lev. 5, 15f).</i> or a guilt-offering of the value of two <i>selas</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This is the minimum price of a ram for a guilt-offering (Lev. <i>ibid.</i>) Consequently the cost of the sacrifice in the case where a man sinned in error is less than that of the sacrifice where a man was in doubt whether he had sinned in error; in the former the cost is one <i>sela</i>, in the latter two <i>selas</i>.</i> Now which Divine attribute is the greater, that of reward<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘goodness’.</i> or that of punishment? You must agree that the attribute of reward is the greater;<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For God’s lovingkindness extends to thousands of generations, while punishment for sin reaches only to the third and fourth generation (Ex. 20, 5f).</i> so is there not here an argument from the lesser to the greater? For if with regard to the attribute of punishment, which is the lesser, he who is in doubt whether he has transgressed or not is regarded by Scripture [more gravely<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has to bring a more costly sacrifice.</i> than] if he had transgressed of a certainty, how much more is it so with the attribute of reward, which is the greater!<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Thus a man who is not sure whether he has performed a meritorious deed will receive a reward as if he had done it of a certainty.</i><br>R. Nathan b. Joseph said: Whoever transgressed in error is regarded by Scripture as if he had acted wantonly. How is this? If a man killed a person unintentionally and fled to a city of refuge,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In accordance with Num. 35, 11f.</i> and the avenger of blood found him<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Outside the city of refuge.</i> and killed him, the avenger of blood is not culpable;<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Num. <i>ibid.</i> 27.</i> whereas if a man killed wantonly and the avenger of blood found him and killed him, he is put to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">So MS. E. and GRA. V. reads, ‘he must escape into exile because of him’; but this is not established law. It is, however, evident that the avenger of blood may in certain circumstances exercise his right of vengeance against the unintentional killer but not against the wanton killer, so proving the thesis that the transgressor in error is in a graver position than the deliberate transgressor.</i> Now which of the Divine attributes is the greater, that of reward or that of punishment? Surely that of reward. And if with regard to the attribute of punishment, which is the lesser, it is held that he who transgressed in error is regarded by Scripture as if he had acted wantonly, how much more so is it with the attribute of reward, which is the greater!
R. ‘Aḳiba said: <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mak. I, 7 (Sonc. ed., p. 29).</i>Whoever joins himself to those that commit a transgression, even though he does not act like them, is punished as they are; and whoever joins himself to those that fulfil a commandment, even though he does not act like them, is rewarded as they are. How is this? If two testify of another saying, ‘That man killed a person’, and they are found to be false witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘plotters’; i.e. they were proved by the evidence of two subsequent witnesses to have been absent at the time of the alleged crime. Their punishment is on the principle of retaliation (cf. Deut. 19, 19).</i> so that sentence of death is passed upon them, and as they are being led to the place of execution<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘house of stoning’.</i> a third man comes running after them calling out, ‘I know something concerning this testimony’, they say to him, ‘Come and offer your testimony’. This man also is found to be a false witness, so that sentence of death is passed upon him. As he is led to the place of execution, he exclaims, ‘Woe is me! had I not come, I would not have been sentenced to death; but now that I came and joined them I am sentenced to death’.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Though his evidence alone would be worthless since two witnesses at least are required.</i> They say to him, ‘Fool that you are, even if one hundred men were to come after you and found to be false witnesses, they would all be put to death!’ Now which Divine attribute is the greater, that of reward or that of punishment? Surely that of reward. And if with regard to the attribute of punishment, which is the lesser, it is held that he who joins himself to those who commit a transgression, even though he does not act like them, is punished [as they are], how much more is it so with the attribute of reward which is the greater!
R. Simeon said: Such is the penalty of a liar, that even when he speaks the truth he is not believed;<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. 89b (Sonc. ed., p. 596). This passage follows naturally on the preceding which deals with perjured witnesses.</i> for we find it so with the sons of Jacob. When they first lied to their father he believed them, as it is stated, <i>And they took Joseph’s coat, and killed a he-goat;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 37, 31.</i> and it is written, <i>And he knew it, and said: It is my son’s coat</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 33. Jacob believed their story. [Cf., however, <i>Soferim</i> XXI, below p. 332.]</i> But finally, although they spoke the truth to him, he did not believe them, as it is stated, <i>And they told him, saying: Joseph is yet alive … And his heart fainted, for he believed them not</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XLV, 26.</i> Some say that the Holy Spirit, which had departed from our father Jacob [during his grieving for Joseph], now settled upon him, as it is stated, <i>The spirit of Jacob their father revived</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 27. It was only the return of the Divine Spirit to him that convinced him of the fact that Joseph was alive, but he did not accept the word of his sons on this occasion when they spoke the truth.</i>

Chapter 31

<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph has no connection with the theme of this chapter, which is an extensive commentary on Aboth 5. Such commentary begins with §2. Accordingly it has been suggested that this paragraph should be joined to the previous chapter. This arrangement has been adopted by Schechter.</i>R. Aḥai b. Josiah said: He who buys grain in the market,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Not having land of his own, he must resort to the market for grain for his household. This was considered a precarious existence; cf. Men. 103b (Sonc. ed., p. 637).</i> to what may he be compared? To a child whose mother has died; though it is taken around to the homes of nursing mothers, it is not satisfied. And he who buys bread in the market, to what may he be compared? To a man who digs his own grave.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘is dug and buried’. His is a wretched existence; cf. the statement in Men. <i>loc. cit.: ‘And shalt have no assurance of this life</i> (Deut. 28, 66): this refers to one who has to rely upon the bread dealer [for his daily needs]’ (Sonc. ed., p. 638).</i> But he who eats of his own produce is like a child reared at his mother’s breast.<br>He used to say: When a man eats of his own labour his mind is at ease. Even when he is dependent upon his father or mother or children, his mind is not at ease; and needless to say when he is dependent upon strangers.
<small>BY TEN SAYINGS WAS THE WORLD CREATED</small>. What need is there for mankind to [know] this? It is to teach that if a person performs one precept, or keeps one Sabbath, or saves one life, Scripture imputes it to him as though he had saved a whole world which was created by ten sayings.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The importance of the world is enhanced by the fact that God, in view of His omnipotence, took so much trouble with its creation, for instead of a single fiat He resorted to ten utterances to perfect the world.</i> And if a person commits one sin, or profanes one Sabbath, or destroys one life, Scripture imputes it to him as though he had destroyed a whole world which was created by ten sayings. We find it so with Cain who slew his brother Abel; as it is stated, <i>The voice of thy brother’s bloods crieth unto Me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 4, 10, E.V., <i>brother’s blood</i>. The Heb. noun is plural.</i> He shed the blood of one person, yet Scripture speaks of many bloods! But it teaches you that the blood of his sons, of his sons’ sons and of his descendants to the end of all generations that were destined to issue from him, all stood and cried to the Holy One, blessed be He. Hence you learn that one man is equal in worth to the entire creation.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. IV, 5 (Sonc. ed., pp. 233f.).</i>
R. Nehemiah said: Whence do we know that one man is equal in worth to the entire creation? For it is stated, <i>This is the book of the generations of Adam</i>. [<i>In the day that God created man, in the likeness of God made He him</i>];<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> V, 1.</i> and elsewhere it states, <i>These are the generations of the heaven and of the earth when they were created</i>, [<i>in the day that the Lord God made earth and heaven</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> II, 4.</i> As in the one verse [which describes the creation of the world] the terms <i>created</i> and <i>made</i> are used, so in the other verse [which describes the creation of man] the terms <i>created</i> and <i>made</i> are used. This teaches that the Holy One, blessed be He, showed Adam all the future generations that were to issue from him standing and sporting, as it were, before him. Some say that He showed him only the righteous [of every generation], as it is stated, <i>Every one that is written unto life in Jerusalem</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 4, 3. The verse refers to the righteous, God’s elect, whose names are inscribed in the ‘Book of Life’, which is identical with <i>the book of the generations of Adam</i>.</i><br>R. Joshua b. Ḳorḥa said: It states, <i>Thine eyes did see mine unformed substance, and in Thy book they were all written</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 139, 16. <i>Thy book</i> is interpreted as <i>the book of the generations</i>.</i> This teaches that the Holy One, blessed be He, showed Adam, the first man, every generation and its teachers, every generation and its administrators, every generation and its leaders, every generation and its prophets, every generation and its heroes, every generation and its transgressors, every generation and its pious men, and that in a certain generation such a king will arise and in a certain generation such a Sage will live.<br>R. Eliezer the son of R. Jose the Galilean said: Nine hundred and seventy-four generations before the creation of the world the Torah was already written<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Divine plan, as intimated <i>ibid.</i> CV, 8: <i>The word</i> (i.e. the Torah) <i>which He commanded to a thousand generations</i>, was that a thousand generations were to pass before the giving of the Torah to man. Since, in fact, Moses received the Torah and he lived in the twenty-sixth generation from the creation, it follows that the Torah must have been in existence for nine hundred and seventy-four generations before the creation. Cf. Ḥag. 13b, 14a (Sonc. ed., p. 82, n. 7).</i> and lay in the bosom of the Holy One, blessed be He, singing praises together with the ministering angels; as it is stated, <i>Then I was by Him as a nursling; and I was daily all delight</i>, [<i>playing always before Him</i>,] <i>playing in His habitable earth</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Prov. 8, 30f.</i><br>[R. Jose the Galilean said: All that the Holy One, blessed be He, created in the world, He also created in man.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is omitted in V. It is found in the Oxford MS. of this work [cf. Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i>, I, p. 365, n. 1] and serves as a fine introduction to the parable and the extensive paragraph which follow.</i>] This may be illustrated by a parable. To what can it be compared? To a man who takes a piece of wood intending to engrave many designs on it; but as he has insufficient space for his designs, he is dejected. He, however, who draws his designs on the ground can do so interminably, and keep everything distinct and apart. On the other hand,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">So according to Schechter’s text which has MS. authority. V reads ‘so too’. The contrast in the thought is this: whereas man requires the whole surface of the earth to draw his designs, God formed within the confined space of his body everything which He had created in the world. This is the concept of the ‘microcosm’: that man is regarded as an epitome of the universe, a world in miniature.</i> the Holy One, blessed be He,—may His great name be blessed for ever and to all eternity—in His wisdom and understanding created the whole world: He created the heaven and the earth, the celestial beings and terrestrial creatures. He likewise fashioned in man all that He had created in His world: He created forests in the world and forests in man, viz. his hairs; He created wild beasts in the world and wild beasts in man, viz. the intestinal worms;<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The text varies between ‘the intestines of man’ and ‘the worms in man’. Both readings apparently refer to the same thing, the intestinal worms.</i> He created destructive agents<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Heb. <i>ḳorẓin</i>, a difficult word with doubtful meaning. According to Jastrow ‘destructive insects’, but he emends the end of the line to ‘intestines’ in place of ‘ears’. Bacher, R.E.J. XXXVII, pp. 301f, compares this word with the Aramaic expression in Dan. 3, 8: <i>wa’akalu ḳarẓehon</i>, ‘and they brought accusations’ and translates here ‘accusers’, ‘slanderers’.</i> in the world and destructive agents in man, these [act via] the ears of man;<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Which are the receptacles of slanderous and malicious talk.</i> He created odours<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Another reading is ‘wind’.</i> in the world and odours in man, these are [sensed by] the nose of man; He created a sun in the world and a sun in man, viz. the light of [the countenance of] man;<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Another reading is ‘his forehead’.</i> fetid water in the world and also in man, viz. his nasal discharge; salt water in the world and also in man, viz. his tears; streams in the world and also in man, viz. the secretions;<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘tears’; but as tears have just been mentioned with the qualification ‘of the eyes’, the word might mean ‘secretions’. GRA reads ‘the urine’.</i> ramparts<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Another reading is ‘seals’.</i> in the world [29<i>b</i>] and also in man, viz. his lips;<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. ‘Arak. 15b (Sonc. ed., p. 87) where it is stated that the tongue is surrounded by two walls, one of bone (the teeth) and one of flesh (the lips), to guard it from speaking evil.</i> doors in the world and also in man, viz. his teeth; vaults in the world and also a vault in man, viz. his tongue;<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Or, the palate, which is the vault of the mouth.</i> sweet water in the world and also in man, viz. his sputum; contours<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘jaws’. GRA reads ‘stars’; cf. Shab. 151b (Sonc. ed., p. 774) where the stars represent man’s cheeks.</i> in the world and also in man, viz. his jaws; towers in the world and a tower in man, viz. his neck;<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Cant. 7, 5 where the neck is likened to <i>a tower of ivory</i>.</i> riggings<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The word is unique in Heb. literature and the meaning is a matter of conjecture. Jastrow’s translation is adopted here. [Krauss, <i>Lehnwörter</i>, p. 413, suggests a connection with <span dir="rtl">סרדיוטות</span>, ‘officers’, which is also found in the commentary <i>Tummath Yesharim</i>.]</i> in the world and also in man, viz. his arms; pegs in the world and also in man, viz. his fingers; a king in the world and also in man, viz. his head; [wine-bearing] clusters in the world and also in man, viz. his blood;<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The red juice of the grape is designated ‘blood’ in Deut. 32, 14. In the MSS. ‘his breasts’ is read in place of ‘his blood’; for this cf. Cant. 7, 8.</i> counsellors in the world and also in man, viz. his kidneys;<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For this and the following two comparisons, cf. Ber. 61a, b (Sonc. ed., p. 384): ‘the kidneys prompt [thought]’.</i> grinding mills in the world and also in man, viz. his stomach; crushing mills in the world and also in man, viz. his spleen; refuse bins in the world and also in man, viz. the belly; pits in the world and also in man, viz the navel;<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Which forms a slight depression on the body.</i> running waters in the world and also in man, viz. the urine; life in the world and also in man, viz. his blood; trees in the world and also in man, viz. the bones; hills in the world and also in man, viz. the hips; a pestle and mortar in the world and also in man, viz. his knees;<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">These articulate in ball and socket fashion, i.e. like a pestle and mortar.</i> horses in the world and also in man, viz. his shanks;<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">[GRA reads ‘deers in the world and also in man, viz. his legs’.]</i> the angel of death in the world and also in man, viz. his heels;<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Man can crush to death beneath his heel.</i> hills and valleys in the world and also in man, for erect he is like a hill, fallen he is like a valley. Hence you learn that all that the Holy One, blessed be He, created in His world He also created in man.

Chapter 32

<small>THERE WERE TEN GENERATIONS FROM</small> A<small>DAM TO</small> N<small>OAH</small>. What need is there for mankind to [know] this? It is to teach you that although those generations provoked Him continually, the Holy One, blessed be He, did not bring the waters of the flood upon them on account of the righteous and pious who were among them. Some say that as long as Methuselah was alive the flood did not descend upon the earth; and even when Methuselah died the flood was yet suspended for them for seven days after his death, as it is stated, <i>And it came to pass after the seven days</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 7, 10.</i> What special feature was there about those seven days? They were the days of mourning for that righteous man which stayed the retribution; therefore it is stated, <i>And it came to pass after the seven days</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Sanh. 108b (Sonc. ed., p. 744).</i><br>Another interpretation of <i>And it came to pass after the seven days:</i> it teaches that the Holy One, blessed be He, granted them this further respite after the original period of one hundred and twenty years, in which time they might repent;<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The verse in Gen. 6, 3, <i>Therefore shall his days be a hundred and twenty years</i>, is interpreted by the Rabbis as a period of warning which God granted to that generation in which to repent. When this ended, He allowed them a further seven days’ grace.</i> but they did not do so, therefore it is stated, <i>And it came to pass after the seven days</i>.<br>Another interpretation is: the words teach that the Holy One, blessed be He, reversed for them the order of the universe, the sun rising in the west and setting in the east, perchance they would notice it, be awestruck and repent; but they did not do so, therefore it is stated, <i>And it came to pass after the seven days</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The words <i>the seven days</i> recall the days of the creation and suggest that God interfered with the order of nature, as laid down at the creation, in the hope that mankind would be awestruck at the phenomenon and repent.</i><br>Another interpretation: The words teach that the Holy One, blessed be He, spread His table before them and showed them His bounty of the kind to be enjoyed in the world to come, so that they might scrutinize themselves and say, ‘Woe to us for all this good which we shall have lost!’ But they corrupted their way upon the earth,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The text is in disorder and the translation follows Schechter’s text which gives the MS. reading. V reads ‘and [woe to us] for having destroyed our seed from the earth’.</i> as it is stated, <i>And God saw the earth, and, behold, it was corrupt;</i> [<i>for all flesh had corrupted their way upon the earth</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> VI, 12.</i>
R. Eleazar b. Parṭa said: It is written, <i>My spirit shall not judge man for ever</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> 3. The verb <i>yadon</i>, translated in the Versions as ‘abide’ or ‘contend’, is here given the meaning ‘judge’.</i> The Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘I shall not judge them until I shall have first doubled their reward’,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is in agreement with the Rabbinic doctrine (cf. Ḳid. 40b, Sonc. ed., p. 202) that God bestows prosperity upon the wicked in this world, paying them a double reward for the few good deeds to their credit in this world (cf. B.B. 15b, Sonc. ed., p. 75), in order to deny them the world to come.</i> as it is stated, <i>They spend their days in prosperity, and peacefully they go down to the grave</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Job 21, 13. Their reward is thus double: they live in prosperity and die in tranquillity without suffering; cf. Sanh. 108a (Sonc. ed., p. 739) where the verse is explained as referring to the generation of the flood.</i><br>R. Jose the Galilean said: Scripture states, <i>Shall not judge</i>. The Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘I shall not regard the evil impulse as equal to the good impulse’. When is this so? Before the decree has been sealed; but once the decree has been sealed, both impulses bear equal responsibility for sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The intention of this passage is obscure. Perhaps the meaning is: because the evil impulse enters man at birth (Gen. 8, 21) and the nature of man is prone to fall under its sway (<i>ibid.</i> VI, 5), God will not judge it on the same footing as the good impulse, but make allowance for human frailty. Once man has been sentenced at the seat of judgment, both impulses are on equal footing as regards responsibility for sin. Cf. the somewhat similar statement by the same author in Midrash Rabbah, Genesis, XXVI, §6 (Sonc. ed., pp. 216f).</i>
He used to say: From the righteous the evil impulse is removed<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘he takes away from them’.</i> and they are left with<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">lit. ‘and he gives them’.</i> the good impulse only, as it is stated, <i>My heart is slain within me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 109, 22, E.V., <i>is wounded. By heart</i> is meant the desires of the heart, i.e. the evil impulse. [According to Bacher, <i>Ag. d. Tannaiten</i>, I, p. 361, n. 3, the interpretation is based on the use of <i>leb</i> for <i>heart</i> instead of <i>lebab</i> both here and in the next quotation. The latter is explained by the Rabbis as the two impulses while the former denotes only one of them.]</i> From the wicked the good impulse is removed and they are left with the evil impulse only, as it is stated, <i>Transgression speaketh to the wicked, methinks—there is no fear of God before his eyes</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XXXVI, 2. <i>Transgression speaketh</i> is interpreted as ‘the evil impulse prompts’.</i> Average people are left with both impulses: he who draws near to the evil impulse is swayed by it, and he who draws near to the good impulse is swayed by it, as it is stated, <i>Because He standeth at the right hand of the needy, to save him from them that judge his soul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CIX, 31. The <i>needy</i> are the average persons who are lacking in good deeds. <i>Them that judge his soul</i> are the two impulses in man which control him. On the passage, cf. Ber. 61b (Sonc. ed., p. 385).</i><br>R. Simeon b. Eleazar said: Scripture states, <i>Shall not judge</i>. The Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘I will not judge them until I have rewarded the righteous’. This is so only in this world;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment of the wicked will be the more poignant when they see the bliss of the righteous. This, however, can only happen in this world and not in the world to come, because the generation of the flood will not stand in the judgment in the world to come; cf. Sanh. 107b (Sonc. ed., p. 737).</i> but as for the world to come Scripture declares, <i>His breath goeth forth, he returneth to his dust</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> CXLVI, 4. Consequently the wicked will not see anything of the reward granted to the righteous in the hereafter.</i><br>R. ‘Aḳiba said: Scripture states, <i>My spirit shall not judge man for ever</i>. The Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘They did not scrutinize themselves as mortals,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">According to this interpretation the subject of <i>yadon</i>, here understood as ‘they did not judge themselves’ is the generation of the flood.</i> but displayed an arrogant spirit towards the Most High’, [as it is stated,] <i>Yet they said unto God: Depart from us</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Job 21, 14.</i><br>R. Meir said: Scripture states, <i>Shall not judge</i>. The Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘That generation declared, “The Lord shall not judge” [meaning,] there is no Judge in the world; the All-present has abandoned the world’.<br>Rabbi [Judah the Prince] said: Scripture states, <i>Shall not judge</i>. The Holy One, blessed be He, said, ‘They do not set up courts of justice on earth, so I will set up<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">There is a variant, ‘I, too, will not set up’; i.e. I will punish them without recourse to judicial procedure.</i> against them courts of justice on high’.

Chapter 33

T<small>HERE WERE TEN GENERATIONS FROM</small> N<small>OAH TO</small> A<small>BRAHAM</small>. What need is there for mankind to [know] this? It is to teach that all those generations were provoking Him, and there was not one man who would walk in the ways of the Holy One, blessed be He, until Abraham our father came and walked in His ways; as it is stated, <i>Because that Abraham hearkened to My voice</i>, [<i>and kept My charge, My commandments, My statutes, and My laws</i>.]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 26, 5.</i> The verse does not say ‘law’ but <i>laws</i>. But whence did he learn them?<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In the plural. So according to a variant reading. V reads, ‘Did he keep one law? Surely he kept many laws!’</i> It teaches you that the Holy One, blessed be He, provided Abraham with two reins which, like two Sages, instructed him, counselled him, and taught him wisdom all the night long, as it is stated, <i>I will bless the Lord who hath given me counsel; yea, in the night seasons my reins instruct me</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 16, 7. Cf. above XXXI, 3, p. 148f., where the kidneys are said to act as counsellors.</i> Moreover, our father Abraham was wont to practise righteousness first and justice afterwards, as it is stated, <i>For I have known him, to the end that he might command</i> [<i>his children and his household after him, that they may keep the way of the Lord, to do righteousness and justice</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Gen. 18, 19. In this description of Abraham <i>righteousness</i> precedes <i>justice</i>.</i> Whenever two disputants came before our father Abraham with a lawsuit, the one claiming a <i>mina</i> from the other, he would take out a <i>mina</i>, give it to the defendant, and say to him, ‘Argue your case before me’. After their case had been argued, if [he found that] one was in debt to the other, he would say to the defendant in whose hand was the <i>mina</i>, ‘Pay the <i>mina</i> to your companion’; but if [the claim was] not [proved], he would say to them, ‘Divide it between you and depart in peace’. King David, on the other hand, did not act so; he was wont to do justice first and righteousness afterwards, as it is stated, <i>And David executed justice and righteousness unto all his people</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">2 Sam. 8, 15.</i> When disputants came before king David with a lawsuit, one claiming a <i>mina</i> from the other, he said to them, ‘Argue your case’. After their case had been argued, if [he found that] one owed a <i>mina</i> to the other, he would take out a <i>mina</i> of his own and give it to the claimant; but if [the claim was] not [proved] he would say to them, ‘Depart in peace’.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In V the text reads, as with Abraham, ‘Divide it between you and depart in peace’. This is an obvious error, because where the case was not proved, king David made no gift of money. Consequently the words ‘divide it between you’ are incorrectly interpolated.</i>
<small>WITH TEN TRIALS WAS</small> A<small>BRAHAM OUR FATHER TRIED BY THE</small> H<small>OLY</small> O<small>NE</small>, <small>BLESSED BE</small> H<small>E, AND HE WAS FOUND STEADFAST IN THEM ALL</small>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The version in Aboth 5:4 (Sonc. ed., V, 3, p. 59) is ‘and he stood firm in them all’.</i> They are: twice when he had to leave his home,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The first time when he was bidden to leave his native land (Gen. 12, 1), and the second time when he set out for Egypt because of a famine in the land (<i>ibid.</i> 10).</i> twice with his two sons,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">First when he was induced to drive his son Ishmael out of the house (<i>ibid.</i> XXI, 10), and then in the supreme test of the binding of Isaac (<i>ibid.</i> XXII).</i> twice with his two wives,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">When Sarah was taken to Pharaoh’s palace (<i>ibid.</i> XII, 15), and when he was induced to send Hagar from his home (<i>ibid.</i> XXI, 10). Alternatively, the second trial was the occasion when Sarah was taken by Abimelech (<i>ibid.</i> XX, 2).</i> once [when he fought] against the kings,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XIV.</i> once at the Covenant between the Pieces,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> XV, 7ff.</i> once in Ur of the Chaldees<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">According to a tradition, Abraham had been thrown by Nimrod into a fiery furnace because of his belief in God, from which he came forth unharmed. The name <i>Ur</i> means ‘fire’ in Heb.</i> and once at the Covenant of Circumcision.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>ibid.</i> XVII, 10.</i> [30<i>a</i>] Why [was he subjected to these trials]? So that when our father Abraham will come to claim his reward, the angels<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">GRA reads ‘all will say’. There is a variant ‘the nations of the world will say’.</i> will say, ‘Far more than all of us is our father Abraham worthy of claiming his reward’; as it is stated, <i>Go thy way, eat thy bread with joy, and drink thy wine with a merry heart</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Eccl. 9, 7. Midrash Rabbah, Ecclesiastes, <i>ad loc.</i>, applies the verse to Abraham when, after his many trials, he was steadfast in his faith.</i><br>Corresponding to the ten trials with which our father Abraham was tried, in all of which he was found steadfast, the Holy One, blessed be He, performed ten miracles for his children in Egypt.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The children of Israel were immune from the ten plagues which afflicted the Egyptians.</i> On account of them He brought ten plagues [upon the Egyptians]; so, too, on account of them ten miracles were wrought for Israel at the Red Sea;<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">These are enumerated in the passage which follows, each miracle being introduced by the formula ‘we will not cross until’.</i> and also on account of them He brought ten plagues upon the Egyptians at the Red Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Implied in the ten expressions used in Ex. 15 describing the complete destruction of the Egyptians.</i><br>The Egyptians thundered against Israel with their voices; so did the Holy One, blessed be He, thunder against them with His voice at the Red Sea, as it is stated, <i>God thundereth marvellously with His voice</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Job 37, 5.</i> The Egyptians advanced towards the Red Sea with bow and arrow; and the Holy One, blessed be He, likewise revealed Himself to them with bow and arrow, as it is stated, <i>Thy bow is made quite bare</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Hab. 3, 9.</i> and it is also stated, <i>And He sent His arrows, and scattered them</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 18, 15.</i> The Egyptians advanced towards the Red Sea with swords; and the Holy One, blessed be He, also came against them with swords and spears, as it is stated, <i>And He sent out His arrows, and scattered them; and He shot forth lightnings and discomfited them</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> ‘lightning’ indicates nothing else than the sword, as it is stated, <i>A sword, a sword, it is sharpened, and also furbished; it is sharpened that it may make a sure slaughter, it is furbished that it may be as lightning</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Ez. 21, 14f. The last words of the quotation are rendered as in the Revised Version.</i> The Egyptians gloried in shield and buckler; and the Holy One, blessed be He, did likewise, as it is stated, <i>Take hold of shield and buckler, and rise up to my help</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 35, 2.</i> The Egyptians advanced with spears; so, too, did the Holy One, blessed be He, as it is stated, <i>At the shining of Thy glittering spear</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Hab. 3, 11.</i> The Egyptians advanced with stones and slings; and the Holy One, blessed be He, triumphed over them with great hailstones, as it is stated, <i>At the brightness before Him, there passed through His black clouds, hailstones and coals of fire</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 18, 13.</i><br>At the time when our ancestors stood at the Red Sea, Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until the Sea appears to us as yawning holes’.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Here are enumerated ten miracles which God wrought for Israel at the Red Sea. Cf. on this passage Mekilta, <i>Beshallaḥ</i>, on Ex. 14, 16.</i> Moses took his staff and struck the Sea so that it became like yawning holes, as it is stated, <i>Thou hast stricken through with his own rods the head of his rulers</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Hab. 3, 14. The verb <i>naḳabta, Thou hast stricken through</i>, is of the same root as <i>neḳabim</i>, ‘holes’.</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until the Sea will become before us like a valley’. Moses struck the Sea and it became before them as a valley, as it is stated, <i>He cleaved the sea and caused them to pass through;</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 78, 13. The verb <i>baḳa’</i>, <i>He cleaved</i>, is of the same root as <i>biḳ‘ah</i>, ‘valley’.</i> and it is also stated, <i>As the cattle that go down into the valley</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Is. 63, 14.</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will be divided before us into parts’.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">i.e. into lanes, a separate lane for each tribe.</i> [Moses struck the Sea so that it was divided into parts,] as it is stated, <i>To Him who divided the Red Sea into parts</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 136, 13. E.V., <i>in sunder</i>.</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will become for us like clay’.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Or, ‘like a heap’.</i> Moses took his staff and struck the Sea so that it became for them like clay, as it is stated, <i>Thou hast trodden the sea with Thy horses, the clay of the mighty waters</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Hab. 3, 15. E.V., <i>the foam of the mighty waters</i>. The noun, <i>ḥomer</i>, is the same as for ‘clay’. According to the alternative rendering, ‘heap’, the end of the verse should read, ‘the heap of the mighty waters’.</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will become for us as a wilderness’. Moses took up his staff and struck the Sea [and it became for them as a wilderness], as it is stated, <i>And He led them through the depths, as through a wilderness</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Ps. 106, 9</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will crumble into pieces before us.’ Moses took his staff and struck the Sea [so that it crumbled into pieces], as it is stated, <i>Thou didst break the sea in pieces by Thy strength</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> LXXIV, 13.</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will turn into rocks for us’. Moses took his staff and struck the Sea [and it turned into rocks], as it is stated, <i>Thou didst shatter the heads of the sea-monsters on the waters</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> and you must say that the heads of sea-monsters can only be shattered on rocks. Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will become dry land for us’. Moses took his staff and struck the Sea [and it became dry land], as it is stated, <i>He turned the sea into dry land</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i> LXVI, 6.</i> and it further states, <i>But the children of Israel walked upon dry land in the midst of the sea</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Ex. 14, 29.</i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will stand up before us like walls’. Moses took his staff and struck the Sea [so that it stood up like walls], as it is stated, <i>And the waters were a wall unto them on their right hand, and on their left</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ibid.</i></i> Moses said to them, ‘Rise up and cross’; but they replied, ‘We will not cross until it will stand up for us [as in] bottles’.
